READ THIS,
Western Australia is a much richer colony than is
generally supposed as regards minerals, agriculture and
pastoralism.— Sir Samuel Griffith,
Professor Brown, of Victoria, who made a thorough
examination of the south-western portion of Western
Australia, states that not the other colonies only, but
r yen the people of Western Australia evidently do not
know how much there is waiting For development. He
saw thousands of acres in contiguous blocks of fine choco-
late lands, light clay loams, and peaty soils. He thinks
t he important features of the district are its grand climate
and regular rainfall.
“ History repeats itself,” and as surely as Sydney and
Melbourne have made lightning-like progress, so will
PERTH,'
the Capital of West-Australia.

SPECIAL MEDICAL NOTICE.
Dr. G, H. RAYMOND,
Tub Eminent
Botanic Physician, Specialist & Medical Writer,
ijaa commenced Practice in Sydney, after a residence of
four years in Queensland, twelve months in Western Aus-
tralia, nearly three years in Soutli Australia, and eighteen
months in the City of Melbourne.
The latter place Mr. Ray mond was compelled to abandon
in consequence of; the climate proving detrimental to his
health.
During DR. RAYMOND’S practical experience of nine
years in Australia he has built up a reputation un-
approached by any other physician in chronic diseases in
the colonies. ' Dr. Raymond has also relieved and cured
patients who had tried to no avail the so-called leading
medical talent in the world. Proof of this will be seen in
His valuable and scientific publications; and Dr. Raymond
would call the attention of deaf people to his latest scien-
tific paper on the “ Merits and Demerits of Artificial Ear-
Drums and Electro-Magnetic Treatment.”
DR. RAYMOND would also inform deaf persons that he
is the only AURIST in the British Empire that ever
exposed the fallacy of sticking ear-drums in the car for
cure of deafness; and, moreover, it is rank quackery for
any “Aurist”or “Oculist” to induce people to believe
that a mere external remedy will cure a disease, the root
of which lies in the general constitution.
DR. RAYMOND is a Thorough Physician, as well as a
Specialist ; hence his unrivalled success.
A series of Aural and Ophthalmic Papers, supplementary
to a volume published inTBB7, will be sent on receipt of a
penny stamp. Address
No. 62 Carrington Street, Wynyard Square,
SYDNEY, N.S.W.
Works acknowledged by H.R.H. the Princess of Wales.
NOTE.—No mineral Poisons or the Knife used, nor
agonising blisters. Veritas odium parit!
Dr. Raymond intends shortly to Lecture on the eminent
American Medical Philosopher—Dr. Samuel Thomson,
founder of the Botanic System of Practice, and the first
man to expose the Errors and Fallacies of the Faculty.
Dr. Raymond will, when he mounts the public platform,
he the" youngest medical lecturer and advocate in the
English-speaking world, he not having yet attained his
thirtieth year.
“ Tempos omnia revelat ’’—Time reveals all things.

Noble-street, Newton, Geelong,
10th December, 1888.
To If. F. Kloelmaxx, Esq.
Dear Sir, This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
tamed into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took n great
qnanlilg of medicine without it doing me the slightest
goo I, but 1 became worse every day, wasting a wag until
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains iu my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill 1 consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of, three months I was taken had one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time T commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank, God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in (lie
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Petek Smythe, J.P.

DESCRIPTION.
The Platter or Bowl, marked
B, is the receptacle for holding
lib. of butter.
The Cover, marked A, is filled
with water at the top; as this
evaporates through the porous
cover, it produces a grea* degree
of cold, making the butter quite
HARD and FIRM during the
11OTTEST W FATHER.
OUR INSTRUCTIONS FOR
USE.
Soak the whole of the Butter
Cooler in clean water for twenty-
f. ur hours. The butter can then
be placed in the dish marked B,
at the same time filling the hole
in the top with cold water.
The water must be replenished
from time to time as necessary,
but the whole cooler need only
be soaked once.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 25, 1890.
Land Nationalisation.
I The Single-tax Leaguers of New South
Wales have duly realised that which it was
certain they would, sooner or later, discover,
namely, that the Freetrade Government, led
hy Sir Henry Parkes, has been simply
playing with them. The relations between
the political faction headed by Parkes and
the Georgeites has always been one of veiled
discordance. The Single-taxers thought
they were making a cats-paw of the Govern-
ment, while the Government knew that it
vas making a cats-paw of the Single-taxers.
l’o a Parliamentary hand so experienced and
so astute as Sir Henry Parke.s, it must all
dong have been quite apparent that although
he Georgeites are exceedingly active and
“xtraordinarily noisy, they do notconstitutea
«»dy really possessing any considerable
tower in the constituencies. They are cx-
cllent at newspaper controversy, desperately
n earnest, zealous to the point of self-
acrihce. Parkes probably regarded them
s a great continental commander did
British soldiers. They were first-rate, but—
acidly there were not many of them. To a
olitician like Parkes, with whom principles
re but playthings, and all associations of
ealots merely pawns, the Single-taxers were
ertain to be rated pretty nearly in the pro-
option of importance which their actual
uting power bears to the electors as a whole,
hus regarded, they would be to him not so
inch Tennyson’s cousin Amy who was sup-
osed to be to her wealthy bridegroom-
respective in “ Locksley Hall”: Some-
dug better than his dog, a little dearer
lan his horse.” The Single-taxers would
3 to a man like Parkes something less than
s Yellow Pup, a little cheaper than his
alking horse, the Local Optionists.
o # #
The disillusionment which the Single-
xers have experienced was certain to
“fal them sooner or later. It is a distinct
in to the cause of Land-Nationalisation
at it has happened thus early. Had
fitters continued in their expected course
was probable that this wing of the
herents of the cause would have drifted
to almost inextricable entanglement with
at essentially conservative body, the Free-
iders of New South Wales. Seduced, as
consequence of the inexperience in prac-
al politics of their enthusiastic leaders,
;o believing the jarofessions of mere
miners, the Single-taxers imagined
mally that the class of people of whom
■rdekin, Bruce Smith, M'Millan and
orge Reid are the natural representa-
es, and of whom the whole tribes of
porters, station-agents, property-holders,
idlords and bankers, and the dependents
these, constitute the forces, could be
oled into even approximating to a system
ich would destroy their most cherished
ights and wipe them out of existence to
all intents and purposes. And in their raw
enthusiasm they entered upon a crusade so
extraordinary by a preliminary step of cor-
responding fatuousness. Their first proceed-
ing in view of this momentous enterprise
was to divide the forces of Land-Nationalisa-
tion, by repulsing and repudiating all those
who adhered to the methods of Protection.
When Land-Nationalisation first took root
in New South Wales, it was among the
radicals, as was natural. And the radicals
were Protectionists to a man. It was not
until the principles of the cult were pretty
widely adopted that it seemed good to a few
active propagandists to diverge, and by
seeking an alliance with the Conservatives
in the colony, who styled themselves
h reetradors merely because they had become
wealthy under the existing mongrel fiscal
conditions, they objected to any alteration
which might disturb the advantages they
I now how to turn to account. Protection
was repugnant to them, not because they
were students of economics and comprehended
the principles involved, but merely because
it was a change, where they objected to any
change. The simple-minded enthusiasts who
led the Single-taxers actually conceived that
these people were real enthusiasts for a
principle. Honest and earnest themselves,
they were not prone to suspect that others
were merely making-believe and mouthing
tine economic phrasing while having at heart
nothing save a preservation of their own
existing advantages.
* * #
The recent fiat of the so-called Freetrade
Ministry in once more refusing to deal with
the existing bastard and anomalous tariff,
succeeding so many previous demonstrations
of disinclination to meddle with the economic
status quo, has at last proved too severe a
strain for even the sanguine and credulous
faith of the Single-taxers. Their eyes are
opened, at length, to the disheartening fact
that it is they that have been the cat’s-paw
all along, not the Freetrade Conservatives.
Now that the glamour of plausible profes-
sions and prospective promises has lost its
influence, it is unlikely that the Single-
taxers will again fall under the subtle spell.
They will see things and persons as they
actually exist, not as their distempered fancy
had painted them. They will recognise the
ridiculous incompatibility of legislation to
appropriate ground-rents by taxation and
alliance with Burdekin arid Co. They will
be able to appreciate the significant fact
that of the sixty odd members of the Legis-
lative Assembly who constitute the Free-
trade representatives, there are actually
only three—and those three men of no
standing or influence in the party —who are
not utterly antagonistic to that interference
with the sacred rights of property, that
forms the very essence of the Single-Tax
doctrine. They will perceive, further, that
among the protectionists on the Opposition
benches there are more friends to Land-Na-
tionalization than sit on the Ministerial side,
and that, in effect, all the progressive and
Liberal tendencies of the age are repre-
sented on the latter side, whereas the Go-
vernment benches have almost a monopoly
of the Conservative elements.
* * *
AN ith minds thus emancipated from a
transient access of delusion, it should be-
come possible for the Single-taxers to appre-
hend that even had the present Government
ostensibly given a partial effect to their
views, there would have been no real ap-
proximation to their ultimate ideal. The
most that could have been expected from so
subtle an opportunist and timeserver as Sir
Henry Parkes would have been a land-tax
limited by sweeping exceptions. All lands
under a defined value would have been
exempted for certain. Parkes never puts
his foot further than he believes the ground
to be solid. He would make provision for
having the support of the comparatively
small land-holders in taxing the great ones.
And he would assuredly not press his taxa-
tion so far as even to drive the latter
to desperation. But an exemption—any
exemption—would, of course, knock the
bottom out of any Single-tax character,
which otherwise even mild land-taxation
might be imagined to have. A land-tax
with exemptions would not bring the Single-
taxers’ millenium one fraction nearer to
accomplishment than it is to-day. The big
men would not be stung into wanting to cut
the tails off the small holders. Their exer-
tions would be concentrated upon getting
back their own tails. In other words, they
would work upon the apprehensions of small
land-holders, lest, in view of the alleged
influence and admitted objects of the Single-
taxers, they also should, after a while,”be
taxed. And they would work up, with all
the wealth and influence which have been
so strongly utilised against fiscal charges, an
agitation to take taxation off land and place
it upon anything else in this wide world.
By such means the hands of the purely fiscal
and professionally-political section of the
Protection party would be strengthened. It
would be but a question of time when the
propertied class and its hangers-on would
split from the importers and their depen-
dants, and would throw its weight and in-
fluence on the side of - the Protectionists.
But the propertied class would, in such case,
strike a bargain. In order to attract it, the
political Protectionists—those whom Parkes
kicked into Protection because they were
anti-Parkesites, and when secured the support
of the metropolitan morning journals by
declaring for Freetrade would make a
business of denouncing taxation of land
as unnecessary and undesirable. In such
case, Land-Nationalisationists would have
been left utterly out in the cold.
Humbugged and betrayed by the Free-
traders and repulsed and contemned by the
Protectionists, these principles would suffer
another period of submergence, such as they
have experienced for over half a century.
Principles may possess an irradiable essence
of vitality. But that will no more prevent
their lying inoperative for a season than the
fertility of a grain of wheat will enable it to
bud and produce leaf and flower, if by any
means it be enclosed in the sarcophagus of a
Pll akaoll. The principles of Land-National-
isation had an earnest and devoted exponent
in Hpknorr. Vet, despite his unllinchingand
most industrious propagandist!!, they fell
into abeyance from the day of his ‘death
until Henry George revived and replanted
them.
* * *
The fact appears to be that in New South
Walse the cause of Land-Nationalisation has
fallen into the hands of men of immature
judgment and over-sanguine temperament.
These imagine that the refrain they devote
themselves to bring about can be accom-
plished with a hop, skip and jump. More-
over, their reverence for the latest and most
eloquent exponent of the principles of the
comrnunisation of land has overborne or
extinguished their own faculty of original
thought, and even of adaptation. Because
Henry Georoe is a Freetrader as well as a
Land-Nationaliser and has concerted a plan
of making each of his favourite theories
dependent on the other, or, at least, to
fit in with the other, they have
abandoned their own judgment, and have
submitted to follow their leader in all
his windings and vagaries. They have thus
failed to perceive—just as they failed to see
that the Freetraders were trifling with them
—that Mr. George has really subordinated
Land-Nationalisation to Freetrade by his
preference for Single-Taxism over actual
abolition of private property in land. By
this divergence from the straight path of his
own argument, up to that point he has
interposed enormous and quite unnecessary
difficulties in the way of the general adoption
of the greater reform. So long as Land-
Nationalisation be advocated in its simple,
unadulterated essence, ihe people of any
country in the world could set to work to
adopt it straightaway. But the Single-Tax
application of the remedy stands in quite a
different position. Before that can be
adopted by any civilized com mb pity on the
face of the earth, save Great Britain, Turkey
(if that can be termed a civilized country)
and New South Wales, every one must first
undergo the particularly difficult process of
reversing its fiscal system, without any real
necessity, so far as the adoption of Land-
Nationalisation is concerned.
* * *
Then again, reverting to New South
Wales, the Single-Tax contingent lose sight
of the most essential of all considerations.
They don t take into account human nature.
They reckon they can talk small freeholders
and selectors into giving up their present
advantage, in expectation of an advantage
more than equal, but quite in the theoretical
stage. Every selector in the colony, and
nearly every freeholder, looks to the un-
earned increment on his land to make him
rich. On this, far more than to any present
use, than to any result of industry applied
to the soil, the selector relies to give him
ease in middle-age and wealth in more ad-
vanced life. The Single-Taxers expect to
eradicate this general and all but universal
turn of expectation, and to convince (he
bucolic mind that things will be much better
if all unearned increment be surrendered in
consideration of wages becoming high, and
clothes, notions, and groceries cheap.
* # #
This is a sort of expectation so delightfully
infantile and verdant that it is almost painful
to disturb it. But to entertain it can lead to
nothing but disappointment. It may be
stated as a certain rule that no ordinary
man surrenders a certain present advantage
in actual possession for another sort of
advantage not quite certain, no matter
how lovely it may be made to look.
Land-owners know quite as well as Single-
Taxers can tell the landless, that so long as
they possess the land, they hold the pull over
all the rest of tllfi community. Are not the
Single-Taxers preaching this fact every day ?
The men who hold the land are masters of
everything else and of every other class. If
they never knew it before, they have learned
it from the Single-Taxers’ discussions and
broad-cast pamphlets. Yet the Single-
Taxers' theory supposes that they will
amicably surrender all these advantages,
and consent not to be the fortunate
ones of the world’s population. There
is a high and etherial conception of
human nature in the nineteenth century, ex-
pressed in this amiable expectation. But,
looking at it with the cool eye of practical
probability, it has no aspect of workableness
about it. The Land-Nationalisationist, pure
and simple, takes a different view. He
reckons that the land-owner won’t abate one
jot of his pretensions without being com-
pelled to. And compulsion, by constitutional
means, requires that the landless shall out-
number, out-influence, out-manoeuvre, and
out-vote the land-owning.
* * #
fo attain this there may be more ways
than one. But the most obvious way is un-
doubtedly to transfer to our own colony the
industrial activity which creates great popu-
lations in concentration. That is to sav
manufacturing industries must be encouraged
and fostered. The urban artizan and
mechanic is the proletaire who reads
and thinks, and discusses, and debates, ami
reflects on his condition, and on social pro
blems generally. It is lie who in all coun-
tries, and during all ages has been the source
and spring of revolutions. It is from him
and by him only that this great reform can
and will be accomplished. So lorm as
New South Wales remains a mere farm
turning out raw materials by the labour of a
scattered and dissevered population of buco-
lic toilers, all hope of any greai reform, such
as the abolition of land-monopoly, is hope-
less, and those- who strive for it are mere
beaters of the air.
An Ideal City.
'' >tEN the human race begins, from any reason
to congregate together in a new land, - its first
act is to construct an offensive plague-spot and
call it a city, it acquired tin's habit very early
in the world’s history, and it will probably con-
tinue to follow the practice through all time
Somewhere, away back in the hazy beginning
of humanity, two patriarchs pitched their ten is
side by side in the wilderness, and threw t w
bones and garbage out in front of their door
because the ash-barrel was not invented in those
days, and the dustman had not yet been di
covered ; and thus were laid the foundations
the first city known to history. Then the
reared families, who married and intermarriee
and gradually consolidated into a fixed coi
munity ; and by-and-bye the gathering d
veloped into a motley confluence of sores am
sin and mud-built hovels, and the grass Mas a
trodden away into slush round the rising tow ,
and the bills of mortality began to swe, ,
because the adjacent stream was polluted wi [
seM'age, and all sorts and conditions of unclea
ness. Gradually, too, the dwellers in th
original city became a stunted and knock-knecr
race, built on a spidery plan and specificatio
that was entirely their own. There was i
particular order of architecture about the r
homes, and in this respect their descendants o ’
the nineteenth century have carefully follower
their example ; there was no pleasure and litt e
romance in their lives—theirs was merely tl
story of men who lived, and grew old, am
died, and were not missed when they were
gone, and the citizens of to-day, as a ml
leave much the same record behind them. Am ’
so far as the masses are concerned, the modem
city is just the same as its original progenito
Damascus, the eternal and unchangeable capita
of the Syrian oasis, stands as it did in the day
of Abraham, and yet the dismal, ungainly
hovels that fringe the new suburbs of Waterlo
and Botany, or lie scattered about the Wes
Melbourne swamp, would not be much out
place in the street which is called Straight. Th
leprosy-stricken shanty of Galilee, and th
Chinaman’s den in Sydney, are equally desir-
able residences, for civilisation has little appre
ciation of beauty, and its knowledge of cleanl
ness is chiefly superficial. And, therefore, th
ideal city is still a dream, and the grea
communities of this and every other land ar
mere agglomerations of dispiriting ugliness, am
disease, and red brick, and angular lamp-posts
and broken pavements, and every othe
unsightly object which man has ever learned t
construct for his own insane gratification, am
for the demoralisation of posterity.
* * #
The modern city, at its best, consists of on
or two elegant thoroughfares surrounded bj
ugliness and desolation. As a rule, even the
show r thoroughfare—the one which is writtej
up in guide books, and exhibited to gaping
visitors, and drivelled about at banquets when
the prosperity of the nation is on the pro
gramme—tails off into Chinamen and slop
buckets at either end, but the central portioi
of if exhibits the results of many centuries o ’
progress, and the community is proud of i ,
accordingly. It serves as a disguise to ai
incurable eruption of back-lanes and rights
of-way, among which lie a shapeless mass
of sheds and shanties, but these things
are varnished over by the stately tene-
ments in front, which serve the same pur-
pose as a coating of whitewash on a corpse.
But beyond and around and on every side of
this region there is a land of scraggy ungain -
lincss where the city begins to run to seed. The
streets are hare, narrow and dusty, angular,
triangular, crooked, spiral, and cut into all
sorts of mathematical figures, and disfigured by
the flaring posters and placards of that evil
genius, tiie unhanged advertising fiend. Then
the gaunt, unlovely terrace comes into evidence
with its banged and battered door opening
abruptly into the street, and its naked gable
glaring at the sky ; and betw'een the terraces
there linger sundry remnants of the past in the
shape of st raggling, mouldered hovels, one storey
high, roofed with old, green, time-rotted
shingles, and situate either a yard above or a
yard below the present street level ; and be-
tween the hovels is that inevitable piece of
waste ground on which rubbish is shot and left
to fester, because some speculative public enemy
is holding the property for a rise. And some-
where in the same vicinity will lie found the
square, black, galvanised-iron church, looking
like a hat-box gone mad, in which the members
of a vociferous creed thud and howl and sing
tuneless hymns for the glory of the Lord, and
thus add their humble quota to the grimness of
a joyless civilisation.
# # »
It is no one’s business to disguise the ugli-
ness of a modern city. The great, inflamed
gasometer is therefore planted in a conspicuous
situation that all the world may recognise its
presence, and though the gasometer and the
palace are capable of equally picturesque effect,
nobody is fined or imprisoned for having cursed
his fellow-men witli so much gratuitous un-
sightliness. The huge towering factory comes
into the same category, but when civilisation
is somewhat further advanced there is hope
that the owner will be compelled to carry on
his avocation without making himself a public
eyesore, or if he insists on being an eyesore that


Jtis gaunt, hungry-looking building will be cou-
liscated and reconstructed at the owner’s ex
pense. The red-painted girder bridge, whose
tints glare out like an angry sunset as it
straddles across some dirty creek, will also be
toned down at the risk and cost of the indivi-
dual who is responsible for its unnecessary
hideousness; and the shot-tower, and the
fourteen-storey coffee-palace will both be pulled
down and carted away, and something will be
done to the individual who chalks advertise-
ments on the pavements, and the lady whose
washing is hung out where it will be an offence
to the public eye will be regarded as an enemy
to the commonwealth and treated as such.
Also, the man who builds a boiler-foundry will
be required, for the good of his fellow men, to
devote the same attention to architectural
beauty as the person who builds a hotel or a
bank in a great city thoroughfare, and the
mansion which is now built of freestone in
front, and of garbage keg and broken down
vate behind, shall be constructed alike at either
end, by virtue of an Act of Parliament pro-
hibiting any human being from wrecking, either
in whole or in part, the landscape which is the
common property of all.
* * *
The sodden, bloated, pimply tenement house,
too, has a conspicuous place among the mon-
strosities of the modern city, and if civilisation
is ever to he worth anything it must either be
abolished or wholly reconstructed. It is caked
in grime from the foundation to the roof, and
its crumbling stairways, and hingeless doors,
and uprooted door-bells, its leprous banisters
and battered scraper, and rusted railings and
unclean doorsteps, its foulness and malaria and
internal darkness are diseases that can only be
cured by fire or dynamite. The long rows of
mangy, tenth-rate shops, where the unwhole-
some apple is displayed in company with a stock
of sticky confectionery, and the caved-in tart
and the flatulent ginger-beer are exhibited
side by side with the old brown-paper cigar, all
call aloud for the same drastic remedy. And if
the white man is in any way superior to the
Hottentot or black aboriginal, and desires to
retain that superiority, then the gruesome
Bethsaida Chapel must also be sacrificed, along
with the dingy pawnshop next door, and the
malodorous public-house at the corner ; and
the marine-store fiend, and the man who
pounds on galvanised-iron tanks for his liveli-
hood, and the ghoul with the apple-stall, and
the individual who trades in gravestones,
and a hundred others, must find some
means to disguise their unsightly occupations,
at their landlord’s expense, and cease to offend
the eye of the nation at large. And civilisa-
tion calls aloud for some adequate vengeance
against the artist in old boards who constructs
the cheap suburban cottage—that square con-
trivance on four legs, with three wooden steps
leading up to the door, and a pool of stagnant
water underneath, which is dashed down by the
bare roadside, unshaded, unfenced, and
decorated only by a pile of coal and wood
beside the back-entrance, a shrill female hang-
ing out washing, and the footprints of a goat
beneath the window. The cities of the Nine-
teenth Century, from Berlin to Melbourne and
from Brisbane to far-off Mexico or Bagdad have
one great feature in common—they all want to
be re-built by a race of comparatively sane
architects on an entirely new model.
* * *
When the boastful Anglo-Saxon has fully
realised that even Ids race, the greatest and
most enlightened on tire earth, has lived since
the beginning of its history after the manner
of the mole and the hog, and has grown so
accustomed to its squalid surroundings as to be
unconscious of their all-pervading foulness,
then he will probably rise up and construct his
cities afresh. And when tire re-construction is
completed the ideal city of the future will be
the beginning of a new era for humanity. Its
■streets will be at least 150 feet in width, and
shaded everywhere by rows of trees, while each
gutter will be a flowing stream of crystal water
instead of a stagnant drain exhaling pestilence
and miasma. A strict building law will enforce
grace and uniformity of architecture, and will
abolish simultaneously the gigantic coffee-palace
which scrapes the clouds, and the grotesque
shanty built of old scrap-iron and disused kero-
sene-tins, which disfigures the modern thorough-
fare ; and the individual who erects an ungainly
hoarding and pastes thereon a flaring adver-
tisement in divers colours will be declared a
felon and treated as such. Then the hideous
slums which breed vice and moral disease will
cease to exist, for a slum of wide boulevards
and clear water and spreading foliage would bo
at once an anomaly and an impossibility ; and
even the larrikin and the Chinaman will lie
compelled to live in the light of day, because
the courts and alleys in which they love to con-
gregate will be swept away for ever, and the
speculator who attempts to rebuild these dens
will be classed with the burglar and the
assassin, and be dealt with in the same way
as the other avowed enemies of society. And,
above all things, beauty will be held to be as
essential as existence itself, and the proprietor
nf the factory or the gasometer or the old bone
depot, will be required to construct the abode of
bis noxious calling in such a manner that it may
be placed alongside tire viceroy's palace or the
president’s mansion without outraging the eye ;
and cleanliness will be enforced by law, whereby
the proprietor of the rack-rented tenement
bouse will find it necessary to keep his proper ty
as fresh and spotless as the morning air, with
the- alternative of serving a term of imprison-
ment while his buildings are purified and reno-
vated at Iris expense.
*. # #
And even here the reform wrll not be ended.
For the earth is wide and space is plentiful,
and when men understand this fact the cramped
and frowsy terrace, with its doors and windows
opening on the pavement, will be abolished, and
the citizen wlro builds a house without a few
yards of lawn or garden between it and tire
busy street will be extinguished by Act of Par-
liament, and, if be persists in Iris project, will
be sent to the same asylum as the other citizen
who endeavoured to build a house without walls
or roof. And even the inborn right of the free
Anglo-S axon to keep a festering eyesore in his
own yard for his own private use and
benefit will be rudely interfered with,
and the battered fence and the gate which
hangs on one damaged hinge will be a
crime, and the foul brick smoke-stack of
the factory will be a misdemeanour, and the loud
street preacher, and the wandering banana em
porium, and the slimy, mouldering wharf, and
the crumbling hulk left abandoned and rotting
in the mud, and everything else of an unsightly
character which the arm of the law can reach
will be wiped out, and the individual who is
responsible for their existence will be called
'upon to answer for his offence in thus disfigur-
ing the earth, whose beauty is the common pro-
perty of humanity. And in these days even
the Yarra will become a crystal stream, and
the scum-laden waters of Sydney’s inner bays
will be restored to their original purity, for
when mankind begins to realise the value of
grace and cleanliness, it will resolve that for
every act which befouls the earth and the
waters someone must be held responsible.
* * *
As for the cost of this great transformation
it can only be defrayed in one way—by the
confiscation to the Slate of the land on which
the old city is built the laud which the
capitalist and the landlord have so miserably
misused. Sooner or later the necessities of
civilisation will render this course unavoidable.
The value of land depends entirely on the
presence of a landless population ; without
them the sites of Sydney and Melbourne would
l>e of no more account than the empty wastes
beside the Gulf of Carpentaria or the Great
Australian Bight; and when the masses com-
prehend that they alone have made the fortunes
of the land-grabber and the monopolist, they
will comprehend also their right to share in the
millions which they have created. And even
if the title of the hereditary land-stealer had
been originally based on divine right and the
express decree of Providence, instead of upon
greed and fraud and villainy, he has long since
been weighed and found wanting, and any
claim he ever possessed is justly forfeited. It
is he and he alone who is responsible
for the slums and the fever-dens, the
noisome by-ways and filthy alleys and moulder-
ing tenements, which make the fairest city of
the earth a plague-spot and a scene of human
degradation ; he has built these places for his
own profit and for the moral and physical ruin
of humanity, and so long as he remains they
will endure and flourish. He has grown rich
on cholera and leprosy, and has turned typhoid
into gold, and has paid dividends out of infec-
tion and ugliness, and, therefore, it is but a
limited measure of justice that the monstrosities
which he has created should be removed at his
expense. But, unhappily, the masses are slow
to realise the necessity for reform, and are
pitiably ignorant as well; and the alleged edu-
cated citizen is proud of the jagged highways,
and the dust, and the ash-box in the gutter, and
the dull, dead monotony of the miles of weary
streets, all equally guiltless of grace and beauty ;
and he gushes about these things in guide-books
and drinks about them at banquets, when he
ought rather to pray for the coming of a con-
flagration which will wipe them all out and
start things afresh.
One Person One Vote.
The Electoral Bill at present in the hands of
the New South Wales Government makes, it is
understood, certain provisions for the enfran-
chisement of women. The full character of the
provisions has not yet been given to the public,
but no secret is made of the fact that the Bill
will try to place women on an equal footing
with men so far as the right of voting at Parlia-
mentary elections is concerned. It will extend
the principle of One Man One Vote into One
Person One Vote, thus answering the demands
of abstract justice and redeeming sex from
being a political disability. But a Bill is one
thing and an Act another, and one of the most
effective means of retarding a reform for which
the majority is ready is the inclusion of it in a
Bill containing items acceptable to a minority
only. Democratic sentiment demands that each
citizen shall possess one vote and no one more
than one, but this sentiment is less strong in
some regions than in others, and to withhold its
application to politics where it is strong until it
can be applied where it is weak is tactically bad
and worse as statesmanship. It is, in fact, the
method of a blunderer or an enemy. Sir Henry
Parkes in effect tells a community ready to
accept one man one vote that he will not let
them have it now and will only give it to them
when, at some distant date, he ascertains that
they will take one woman one vote also.
# * *
If the concession of one man one vote meant
the retardation of one woman one vote, we
would go with Sir Henry ; but not one word
of argument can be adduced in support of such
a hypothesis. On the other hand, from the
day the community shows itself ready to accept
one man one vote its Parliament, elected on a
different principle, ceases to be democratically
representative. True political growth consists
in the adoption of governing principles when
they ripen. If the adoption be deferred, injury
is inflicted on the community, not only in
respect of the loss of political power residing
in the principle, but also in respect of its arrest
of development. Sir Henry’s Bill, if defeated,
will not alone gain nothing for women but it will
prevent men from coming into possession of a
fundamental right. And if the Bill be up to
the promises of its designer there is every pro-
bability that defeat will be encountered. Those
opposed to one man one vote will be against it ;
those opposed to women voters will be against
it; but many who are in favour of one man one
vote will also be against it. These last will see
in enfranchised women clerical tools. Visions
of ballot-boxes stuffed with votes recorded
under dictation will rise before their eyes and
they will divide against the Bill. Visions of
enfranchised women, whipped up to the bust
ings by teetotal and religious fanatics, will also
have an effect. Neophytes are prone to be
over-zealous, and woman, come into this new
power, will be tempted to strive to settle every
question of personal right and freedom by a
vote at the ballot-box. It must be admitted
that such excesses are probable. For ourselves,
we are prepared to meet the probabilities, hav-
ing confidence that sex is no bar to the acquisi-
tion of knowledge, and that whatever advantage
may he taken of her ignorance at the start,
woman will, before irretrievable harm is done,
set her face to the light. But this teaching
requires time to have itself approved, and Pie-
nlier Pabk.es’ Bill comes before a majority is
ready. That is the pinch of the situation. The
male democracy will continue to be practically
disfranchised by the vote of property, not
because the community rejects one man one
vote, but because it, for the present, rejects one
woman one vote. That is why we regard the
proposed Bill as the work of a blunderer or an
enemy of one man one vote. But all through
we have treated the Bill as a genuine measure,
intended to secure" one person one vote in the
full meaning of the words. It may turn out
that we have taken too much for granted, and
that only such a female franchise as the present
male one will be offered. If so, the Bill should
be promptly wrecked, as its effect would be to
double existing inequalities.
* * *
If the drawing-room is to have the franchise,
so must the scullery ; if the rustle of silk is to
be heard at the ballot box, so plain cotton must
be seen there; if the owner of property be ein
powered to run his pencil through the name of
a Parliamentary aspirant a similar power must
be conferred on the woman who has no more
than she stands up in. Men, themselves on the
point of emerging from a system of manhood
suffrage where the man, owing to residential
restrictions, possesses less voting power than an
unimproved allotment of stony earth, must see
to it that women are not pushed into their
empty places. Under every, view, then, the
proposed Bill excites distrust. It stands be-
tween men and true manhood suffrage, and
owing to the exigencies of Parliamentary life it
may be kept where it is for an indefinite period,
The natural course is here the true one. Let
the present New South Wales Parliament give
one man one vote and let that Parliament, re-
presenting the male democracy, give one person
one vote.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Old Order Changeth.
On the recommendation of Mr. Attorney-General
Simpson the New South Wales Government last
week decided that every person accused of
capital crime and unable to fee counsel on his
own behalf, shall in future be permitted to
select his attorney and counsel at the expense
of the State. Hitherto such accused persons
were left without legal assistance until (some-
times within half-an-hour of their trial) the
presiding judge nominated a barrister to defend
them. As a general rule, the practitioner thus
nominated belonged to the extremely-junior bar
and stood towar ds the prisoner in the position
occupied by a just-diplomaed medical student
towards a patient upon whom he had
been suddenly called to perform by him-
self the most difficult and dangerous operation
in surgery. For years The Bulletin has
stood alone in drawing public attention
to the monstrous judicial injustice involved
in the system which has just been obli-
terated in New South Wales by Attorney-
General Simpson, and, as opportunity permitted,
the right of a moneyless accused to legal aid
matching in ability that employed for the
prosecution was consistently advocated in these
columns. There was more in the subject than
mere justice to an accused person ; the relation
of the State to crime and innocence was in-
volved. We held, and hold, that the State
should be as solicitous to prove the innocence as
the guilt of a person charged, whereas by the
animating sentiment hitherto the resources, of
the State have been entirely directed to the
conviction of prisoners. This was simply the
adoption by the State of the common police-
man’s frailty. That officer is disposed to regard
an acquittal as a calamity, and a conviction as
an event on which to be complimented. The
policeman may yet be educated to the point of
seeing that as strenuous efforts should be made
to secure the acquittal of an innocent as the
conviction of a guilty man. Anyhow, the New
South Wales Government has led the way in
this direction, and The Bulletin rejoices that
at least one iniquity is about to be wiped out of
the administration of the Criminal Law of New
South Wales.
A National Programme.
The coming general election in New Zealand
will be the first in Australasian history in which
the power has been actually wielded by the
people—the much vaunted but hitherto non-
existent principle of absolute and unqualified
manhood suffrage is about to assert itself.
Twelve months ago Sir George Grey —almost
the only colonial democrat now living who has
worked for instead of merely screaming for the
Democracy—carried by sheer force of will a
measure in which plural voting was abolished,
and the overwhelming power of bricks and
earth to override the voice of the nation became
a thing of the past. Under the new system
every adult male citizen acquires a vote after six
months’ residence—the workman whose qualifi-
cation is the tenancy of a four-roomed cottage,
or a bark hut, or a tent upon a goldfield in the
lone interior, can gain no less, and the plutocrat
with property scattered over fifty electorates
can gain no more. In the face of these new
conditions more than one politician who has
lived for years on the multiplied suffrage of the
publican and the landlord and the money-
lender has already retired from the contest, and
they have all relieved their feelings as they fled
by stating that the moral decay of Parliament
has compelled them to withdraw, and seek the
association of brighter minds and abler in-
tellects. The Labour party, meanwhile, has
wisely resolved that it will fight for its
own cause alone, and will let religion
Dnd teetotalism and Imperial politics and
all other extraneous questions slide. Its
programme consists of the establishment of a
land and income tax as the principal source of
revenue, the extension of the Eight Hours’
system to all branches of industry, and the
total abolition of the infamous sweating system
-—that dubious blessing of British origin which
is fast taking root in all parts of Australasia.
The party has also pledged itself to absolutely
prohibit the further accumulation of large
estates, and will do its utmost to .recover, those
already alienated ; and should it secure a ma-
jority in the House of Representatives one of
its first proceedings will be to re-introduce the
Employers’ Liability Bill, the Bill for the aboli-
tion of the truck system, and other democratic
measures which were contemptuously rejected
by the Council last session, with the under-
standing that this time the question will be
resolved into a trial of strength between the
nominee Chamber and the nation in which the
“ Lords” must either submit, or forever be
abolished. If the National Party will but hold
together and refuse to be led away upon side
issues and sectarian differences, then Govern-
ment by the people will become a reality for the
first time in any part of the British Empire.
Hitherto the most that Democracy has ac-
complished is to give one vote to the work-
man and twenty-five to the landlord, and
the importer, and the illiterate pugilist who
has made a pile by pounding his fellow-man ;
and consequently the landlord and the importer
and the pugilist have run the affairs of the
country, and because they have failed the
world has resolved that popular government is
only a mitigated success at the best. Probably
the weakest point in the New' Zealand Labour
Party is its leader. Sir Robert Stout is a
lawyer who longs for popular freedom and six-
and-eightpence—a barrister who yearns for
democracy and “refreshers”—a liberal agnostic,
and a howling teetotaller, who is supposed to
be free from bigotry and guile. He is a man of
great ability, and has, so far, proved himself
too clever to succeed. Also, he has more
opinionsjthan any other man on earth, and
owns an eye which quells the judge and jury
when he rises in defence of the suspected hog-
thief. But whether he knows enough or
whether he doesn’t know too much to lead the
National Party with reasonable success time
alone can reveal.
Certain “ Portions of Liver.”
The accomplished cable-liar is never so happy
as when he can get hold of a great bloated
horror and send it out in all its howling naked-
ness to garnish the literature of Australia. He
is a literary ghoul scratching among the dust
of a charnel house—a vampire with a diseased
appetite for severed heads, and brains, and feet
that have been removed with a saw and then
hidden away in a cellar. A corpse in a cave
makes this animal feel better, especially if it is
an old corpse and much trodden down ; and he
loves to forward full and complete details about
a person who crunched his uncle’s aged bones
beneath a cart, and then perforated his whole
family with a pickaxe. But for some time past
the trade in exploded remnants of humanity has
been slack, so last week he sent along the
following statement with its accompanying con-
tradiction, in order to gladden his readers and
shed a gleam of sunshine over each cultivated
intellect :
A woman living in Whitechapel has informed the police
that a young man of superior class hired a bedroom on
the upper story of her house, and that on one occasion
she saw portions of liver, some plain brass rings, resem-
bling wedding rings, and numerous blood-stains about the
room. The man left his lodgings secretß' on the day of
the last murder, and the woman has not since heard or
seen anything of him.
LONDON, 12th October.
The woman who related a story to the police regarding
the conduct of a man supposed to be Jack the Ripper has
modified some of the statements and disclaimed others.
It will be noted here that by some unhappy
mistake the anecdote and the contradiction
were allowed to appear, not only in the same
paper, but in the same paragraph, and thus the
liver is snatched away before the unwholesome
reader has time to appreciate it. This error
should be guarded against for the future. If
the second cable had only been held over till
the next day the public could have spent 24
hours in mentally accumulating a joyous
picture of horror. They could have filled in a
shadowy sketch of a dingy room at the top of a
dingy stair, and with a window consisting
partly of glass and chiefly of brown paper and
old felt hat. The ceiling would be cracked and
fallen in, and the paper in rags, while the door
and the doorposts would be black with age. A
rusted frying-pan would be standing on top of
a pile of ashes in the grate, and in lieu of a
table there would be an abandoned boot-trunk,
and in place of a bed a pile of rags and news-
papers in the corner, and where a chair ought
to have been there might be observed a board
torn out of the floor and propped up by bricks
at either end. And seated on this latter con-
trivance the eye of faith might have discerned
“ a young man of superior class” stained all
over with gore, and musing profoundly over
certain Portions of Liver. He was an indivi-
dual with black hair, and pallid features,
and a glassy light shining in his optic—
evidently a person who took a Dead Body three
times a day regularly, and was addicted to a
Mangled Corpse. Also he had the Stealthy
Style so freely expounded in penny novels, and
he was armed with a knife, a large gun, two
axes, and a saw, the last three weapons having
brains adhering to the edge. The picture is
completed by a dim silhouette of the landlady
fainting on the rotten stair, having been scared
out of her senses by seeing the aforesaid Liver
through the keyhole. But, alas, before the
reader has time even to imagine the thrilling
details the whole picture is busted into thin air.
There was no young man brooding in a murky
room at all; there were no axes and deadly
weapons, no blood and no mystery of any sort.
There wasn’t even any Liver. And when these
features are abolished there is no story left, and
what on earth the cable was sent for passes
human understanding.
Dear Bulletin, —Divorce by mutual consent
has not “ proved itself an uunfixed evil where-
ever it has been tried.” Take the case of
Prussia. The Prussian Landrecht (country’s
law) makes divorce by mutual consent very
easy, the proceedings being thus:—The dis-
satisfied couple appear before the judge of the
Divorce Court and swear that they possess
towards each other a “ mutual, unconquerable
aversion,” as the translation of the technical


term, “ uniiberwindliche abneigung,” would be.
The judge, after having enquired into details,
gives them six months (or three months, I am
not certain about this) time to re-consider their
decision. If there are any young children in
the case, the applicants for divorce have during
this time to satisfy the judge appointed for the
interests of under-aged children (the judiciary
“chief guardian,” as he is called) about the
arrangements for the children’s future ; and if
the judge in divorce considers the parson
of the parish a reasonable and discreet
man, he also usually informs him officially
about the intended divorce, so that the
latter may try to establish a reconciliation
if possible; but this is not obligatory on the
judge’s part. If, at the end of the six (or three)
months, the couple still declare that they have
a mutual unconquerable aversion, if they pro-
duce the certificate of the chief guardian that
he has no objection, and if the judge is satisfied
about the provisions made for the wife, the
marriage is at once declared null and void with-
out any more evidence. This law has been
given to Prussia by Frederick the Great,
and it has been in force ever since in all
those Prussian provinces which constituted
the Prussia of Frederick’s time. There is not
yet a common civil law in existence for the
German empire, although a Commission of the
greatest law-teachers of the different States
has been working out a common code for many
years past. And the old Prussian Divorce Law
—so far as I am aware—far from being con-
sidered an “ unmixed evil,” has given such
satisfaction that, amongst its principal statutes,
the “divorce by mutual consent” will, in all
probability, be the law for the whole of
Germany, as it has been for the greater part
of Prussia for more than 100 years past.—
Yours, A German Doctor.
». ■—
A vigorous contemporary is apparently seeking
after information about the New South Wales
Assembly. This is what he wants to know :
Then what shall be said of a Parliament in which
bestial drunkenness is tolerated and winked at ? What is
to be thought of an Assembly in which members roll and
stagger about in a state of filthy intoxication, interrupt-
ing the proceedings with their drunken jabherings,
vomiting over the floor where they lie wallowing like
hogs until carried out unconscious by the attendants?
Things are bad enough thus in all conscience, but when
the red noses of dipsomaniacs propped up in drunken
sleep between other members, &c., &c.
Well, what is to be said on these subjects, any-
how ? From a personal standpoint we give the
question up, but, perhaps, the best plan would
be for our contemporary to say the whole
dictionary right off, and then he could at least
feel that he had hove the entire English language
into the chasm, and done everything that words
were capable of. There are upwards of 120,000
expressions in the language at present, including
blessings, curses, names of animals, obsolete
verbs, geometrical terms, and remarks used in
botany; let him dash them all down at once,
and state that is the way he feels on the matter.
And if he finds this mode of expressing himself
to be weak and inadequate, we can only suggest
that he should walk into the house, and
solemnly, but firmly, deposit on the table an
empty bottle, a dead cat, a skunk, an old dish-
cloth, a snake, an obscene novel, and a deceased
cuttle-fish, and explain that these objects are
intended to denote a contempt that is too large
for words, and a loathing that exceeds the
limits of time and space, that bursts all earthly
dimensions, and makes grammar fall down
incapable and paralysed through the sense of
its own impotence.
—
The Rev. Stacey Chapman, of St. Paul’s,
Melbourne, in his foot-note to a sermon on
“ Strikes,” which for some undiscoverable
reason he has seen fit to publish, says—
A fallacy that has gained extraordinary currency
respects the production of wealth—the wealth that be-
comes capital. All wealth, it is insisted, is the product
of labour. Labour should certainly receive what it pro-
duces: the wealth belongs to it accordingly. That is,
the world’s capital is in the wrong hands.—Q.E.D.
Answer.—Assume for the sake of argument, and only
for the sake of argument, that wealth (capital) is the
product of labour; and grant without reservation that
labour should receive what it produces. Whose labour
was it that produced the wealth which has become
capital ? Assuredly it was not the labour of the men who
now talk of division. Wealth (capital) has come down
through scores of generations. So, even on their own
ground, these would-be appropriates have no claim.
They didn’t do the work ; they can’t demand the product.
We might point out to the reverend illogician
that Capital, which in its economic season
simply means the instruments of production, is
not indestructible, but requires to be continually
replaced, and that as a certain amount of the
daily earnings of labour is always appropriated
for that purpose the labourer of to-day is
as truly the producer of capital as was his
father before him, or indeed anyone since Adam
reluctantly became an agriculturist. But, accept-
ing the clerical argument as sound, it becomes
evident that no one, either now or in any prior
generation, ever has or had any right to appro-
priate capital to private uses, and here the
parson has fallen into the hands of the Socialist
who, arriving at this conclusion by a somewhat
different route, asserts that all instruments of
production as well as natural resources should
be used for the benefit of the entire community.
How is it that men so destitute of information
on subjects within the range of mortal intelli-
gence should presume to guide public opinion
in matters where supernatural intelligence is
required ?
Young Countries and Small Jobs.
The Victorian Government, not content with
merely taking the “ martyr” Dow back to its
cast;iron bosom, intends to draw upon the
public nurse for the wherewithal to pay that
restored lamb’s costs in the recent Building
Society prosecutions. Not that any distinction
is to be made in the case of Minister Dow.
Common decency' demands that gander Fer-
ousont at least shall participate in the sauce
intended for the Government goose. Should
other directors if the Premier Permanent
Society succeed in wriggling out of the ruin they
have wrought, their cases will be “ considered ”
with the inevitable result of a further un-
authorised bleeding of the community at large.
Thus much was practically admitted by Chief
Secretary De akin the other evening, in reply to
\ sympathetic enquiry from Munho, a severe
Government critic who had a relative implicated
in that shares charge of which nothing more is
to be heard. The unanimity of Government
and Opposition in regard to the settlement of
whitewashed defendants’ lawyer bills is not
the least beautiful feature of what an ignorant,
scoffing public describe as the Premier job. For
once the Victorian Parliament seems to be an
absolutely united crew, prepared to form a
ring round each official as he escapes
from the nerveless grasp of justice and join
hands in a dance of joy. No dissentient voice
is raised in the unsullied Senate Chamber to
point out that gentlemen who experience the
ill-fortune to be charged with fraud, and the
good fortune to be acquitted, are in the habit
of paying the costs of their defence. No one
referred to the trifling fact that Mr. Dow was
an offender against the laws regulating or not
regulating building societies, during the whole
time he drew a director’s fees from the concern
which came to shameful disaster under his dis-
tinguished patronage and forty shillingsworth
support. All, all agree in considering that the
Premier prosecutions, instituted by an honest
man and supported by the best of legal opinion,
were and are doosidly unpleasant affairs for
the parties most interested, and that the
country should pay for the privilege of behold-
ing them trip from court without a stain upon
their characters.
* * *
Under these circumstances 1) UNO AN Gillies
and Co. deserve to be commended for their
moderation. The Government’s intention is
merely to “consider”—shall we say favourably
consider’—the necessity for paying the bare
expenses of friend Dow and his companions in
injured innocence. Deakin says nothing about
alleviating their sufferings with special grants
from the Crown, or a nice fat pension apiece.
Yet no stupid political prejudices would stand
in the way of such a scheme were it discreetly
mooted. A free prosecution, when one conies
to think over the matter, seems only a natural
corollary to the free railway passes, lunches,
water picnics, and etceteras which members of
Parliament already enjoy. Something more is
required to meet the exigencies of this interest-
ing case. The people of Victoria should be
compelled to come down handsomely for the
absolved directors of that bust-up Building
Society, and the bust-up shareholders are here-
with exhorted to pawn their last pair of boots
on behalf of the' poor fellows who put them
through their savings. Let the Government
motto be—-The whole hog or none. The Bul-
letin grows frivolous, however,
# # *
Until human nature changes for the better we
must expect to find crude, unvarnished jobs of
the P. B. Association kind perpetrated in a very
young country, where every description of in-
fluential person shakes hands with every other
description of influential person, and the powers
that be constitute but a small clique. Senators,
judges, councillors, barristers, and mercantile
‘ ‘ aristocracy ” are all known to one another,
more or less intimately. They eat, drink and
loyalise together. In private life they are
quite near neighbours, they travel to town in
clusters, and cannot follow their daily occupa-
tions without rubbing shoulders at every turn.
They are only men after all—large, fat, average
human beings, pompous in their demeanour
towards outsiders, perhaps, but ‘ ‘ free, easy,
and accessible ” among themselves. They
remember one another in the old days,
on the goldfields, on the wallaby - track.
Many a time have they swilled cold tea from
the same billy-can, before they became rich,
obese, and “representative.” When an espe-
cially fine, respectable swindle opens out, they
are all, or nearly all, fairly in it. A bond of
cousinhood unites them, and if they occasionally
throw aside principles and honour in order to
assist a wounded member of the family at the
expense of justice and other people’s money—
why, are they much worse than we of the lower
orders ? The letting off the rascally municipal
Geddes with three months’ imprisonment and
the official desire to quash all Premier B.A.
prosecutions, are calamities incidental to the
present condition of Victoria. Compare
the public life of an Australian colony with
that of England, where Lord Salisbury
is scarcely on nodding terms with three-fourths
of his own party, and didn’t as much as know
Parnell by sight up to three or four years
ago, at any rate. There the Lord Mayor is an
almost unknown quantity to the judges, and
the judges move in circles apart from politicians,
and very few of the politicians are known to
sassiety. The idea of the whole House of
Commons winking at the proposal to charge a
Cabinet Minister’s lawyer’s bill upon the
country is as absurd as the idea of any Cabinet
Minister investing £2 in a building society and
becoming a director. Equally absurd is the
idea of a London police-court case in which the
defendants, barristers and bench of magistrates
might have all dined together at one table
or met in a Mayor’s ballroom. Australia is as
yet only a small place, when measured by
population, and its jobs seem meaner and more
contemptible than the scandals of an older,
wider civilisation, because they involve a much
larger percentage of leading men, and come
right under the notice of the general public.
As the country grows older it will gradually
become encrusted with solemn hereditary jobs
and sunk in respectable iniquity of long descent.
That is the law of human nature.
To farmers. The codlin-moth’s your
friend —not the short locust.
With the splendid, and indeed over-ripe
old age of 84, Sir Robert Molesworth, Victorian
Judge, has distanced his confreres Stawell, Barry,
Williams, Fellows, and Stephen. Yet 20 years
ago anyone would have booked him for the first to
leave this planet. He had one fearful calamity in
his life, but bravely surmounted it, taking no
notice of hints made to him to resign. We refer
to the divorce case of .Molesworth v. Molesworth
and Ireland. It was the common saying that old
Moley had more law in his little finger than the
rest of the Bench in their whole bodies. His
writing of judgments was a reportorial terror, a
fly crawling out of the inkbottle, on the well-
known little blue sheets of foolscap, tied up with a
blue ribbon at one corner. He preserved his
health by shuffling about five miles daily near his
Hawthorn residence, at the rate of a mile an hour.
Minister for Justice Gould cannot recom-
mend the release of Reseigh Martin sentenced to
two years hard labour for libelling Railway Com-
missioner Eddy. Moral (for the two-thousandth
time); Sentences must be fixed by juries, not by
judges. By the way, it is not very surprising that
this tin-pot god of justice can find no reason for
reducing Martin’s sentence. He has been unable
to find any irregularity in the Mount Rennie
trial.
There are in London alone no less than
60,000 Hebrews, and their income each year is as
nearly as possible eighty-two pounds a head, or
something like £5,000,000 ip the aggregate, while
the average income of the Britisher is something
like £34 a head, so that the Hebraic ones are more
than twice as rich as the native race. Again, as
regards those who are in the possession of ten
thousand a year, the Semitic race tops the natives
more than twenty times over.
Twenty-live years ago Carlyle wrote :
“In no time since the beginning of Society was the lot
of the dumb millions of toilers so entirely unbearable as
it is in the days now passing over us. It is not to die, or
even to die of hunger, that make a man wretched, but to
live, miserable, we know not why : to work sore, and yet
gain nothing ; to be heart-worn, weary, yet isolated, girt
in with a cold, universal laissez faire ; it is to die slowly
all our life long, imprisoned in a deaf, dead, infinite in-
justice, as in the accursed iron belly of a Phalaris bull.”
A quarter of a century has gone past, and stil*
things are much the same to-day.
The member for Braidwood asked Premier
Parkes, in the N.S.W. Assembly, if he intended
to carry out his promise made at Braidwood con-
cerning the construction of a railway to that town ?
Whereupon the political three-card man said that
he “ hoped the member’s question was not the
beginning of his being called to account for every
promise he had made in public meeting,” and then
went on to assert that it was known that he “kept
all promises that should be kept in the interests of
the country.” That is to say, that he bribes con-
stituencies and individuals with lavish promises
and then compounds with his alleged conscience
for the deceit, and puts them off when they
inquire about the fulfilment by asserting that to
keep these promises would be prejudicial to
general interests.
Mr. Bennett Burleigh, the well-known
war-correspondent, at a meeting of London East
End workers said he had found by full personal
investigation that the women who make match-
boxes earned as little as 7s. per week, out
of which they had to pay 4s. 6d. for rent, the
balance being all that was left to supply food and
clothing. The House of Commons lengthily and
solemnly discussed the “preservation of hares,” but
how to preserve human life in the East End
might better have occupied their attention. A
match-box maker who was at the meeting said
she had a dying husband at home and was only
able to earn five shillings weekly, and sometimes
not even so much, on account of being summoned
for not sending her starving children to school.
Even by working until the small hours in the
morning she could not really keep body and soul
together. Several other workers gave their testi-
mony to the same effect, and one woman stated
that she had been twenty years in the trade and
was now worse paid than formerly, the price for
boxes having been reduced from 3d. to 2d. per
gross. Happy England !
Writes a Wellington (N.Z.) correspon-
dent
Dear Bulletin,— Some queer sights have lately been
visible aboard the U.S.S. Co.’s Takapuna when the “ free ”
labourers come off duty on the wharf. During the strike
the company have had to fill all vacancies on their vessels
with more or less amateur sailors, stewards, &0., and
public feeling ran so high in the matter that the men
were compelled to board on the steamer to avoid being
roughly handled by the crowd. Some 200 men are now
as I write located aboard the floating hash-foundry, and
after the coal black and grease have been partially washed
off they all assemble in the cabin to dine. As the com-
pany are short of trained stewards all the new hands have
a turn at waiting upon the “ blacklegs,” amongst whom
are a number of Wellington’s greatest “ toffs ” who happen
to be down in their luck, also such people as a divine who
got struck off the list for not being particular about the
ladies he associated with; a dead-beat M.D., the ceiling
of whose pants has been out of repair for some time ; an
ex-gubernatorial aide-de camp ; a famous embezzling
merchant, a once exquisite masher, still suave and rubi-
cund and healthy, who left Sydney short in his accounts,
after scooping in all his best-natured friends’ available
cash; and a host of ex-bank clerks and other ne’er-do-
wells.
There is in N.S. Wales no more demo-
cratic electorate than West Sydney, one of the four
Assembly seats for which constituency has j ust been
vacated by the death of Mr. Alfred Lamb. The
ghastly mess made by the officials of the labour,'
party in their election arrangements is plainly
attributable in no small degree to the unmanly
jealousies existent between several of the chief
officials of the Trades and Labour Council whose
conduct shows them to be utterly unworthy of
commanding so loyal a body of men as those who
at so great a sacrifice, have unhesitatingly obeyed
their orders in the great strike. The choice of the
delegates fell upon Mr. Brennan after an ex-
haustive ballot, and it then clearly became the
duty of every man owing allegiance to the Trades
and’Labour Gounciltosupport the chosen man even
if he had been a black-fellow ; but what do we find ?
One or two trades aspirants for Parliament, in
submitting their candidature before a ballot, to
the result of which it was understood all would
bow, petulantly and inconsistently announced
their intention to go to the poll at all hazards ;
and after the ballot had been held and Brennan
was before the electors, the men who should have
backed him up as the choice of the labour-
machine, to his face at his public meetings uttered
remarks which discounted his candidature and
showed that the most unworthy and childish
jealousy rankled in their breasts. The Bulletin
doesn’t know Mr. Brennan, and, judging from his
public utterances, it is of opinion that he would
make but a very poor and commonplace Par-
liamentarian ; but then the same remark applies
to all the men who have so selfishly fought for
nomination, and should it eventually prove that
a great labour crisis can, in N.S. Wales, bring
forth no more interesting representatives of hard
graft than they, may Heaven help the toilers’
cause ! Nevertheless, the intrusion of Mr. A. G.
Taylor on the scene as a self-constituted labour-
champion The Bulletin regards as grossly
insolent under the circumstances, for Mr. Taylor
came forward not to courteously offer his services
to the associated workmen—he came as a man
who boldly and bluntly said in effect that what-
ever happened he was going to the poll; would
go there heedless whether he helped or wrecked
the plans of the labour organisations. Judiciously
organised, the labour-party could carry West
Sydney ; as matters stand at time of writing, the
seat promises to fall into the occupancy either of
the erratic Mr. Taylor (who left Parliament a
year or two ago to gnaw a bone flung to him by
Parkes), or into that of some fat nonentity nomi-
nated by the Foreign-trade and Conservative
Association in the interests of “ law and order.”

There is little to be said about “ A Pair of
Spectacles,” now running at Sydney Criterion,
except that it has caught on and is drawing very
good houses. Mrs. Brough has little to do but
look ladylike and speak clearly as Mrs, Goldfinch,
and Lillian Seccombe as Lucy Lorimer has only
one show, in a little wheeze about the sparrows and
the crumbs, which she gets off in her usual chirpy
fashion. Cecil Ward, as the law-student, has a
dissipated but not emaciated appearance, and the
Percy of Owen Harris is an unforced and natural
piece of work. “ Ted” Corlesse scores as Lorimer,
** * *
“The Long Strike,” a play that under
ordinary circumstances would deservedly have
run for a couple of months at Her Majesty’s, will
be withdrawn, after a three weeks’ run, on Satur-
day in favour of “ Called Back,” when the Brothers
Rignold will appear, for the first time in conjunc-
tion in Australia, as Macari and Ceneri, and Kate
Bishop will play Pauline. Three pieces are in
consideration and preparation to follow, from
which “ Masks and Faces ” may be selected, with
Maggie Moore as Peg Woffington, and then “ The
Merry Wives of Windsor ” may, if there is time
for its production, precede the staging of “Dick ”
at Christmas. On William Rignold’s first Sydney
appearance, we said : “ What a Falstaff he’d
make!”
** * *
The poor business at Sydney Royal is due
in part to the prevailing depression, but chiefly
to the thinness of “ Chilperic ” and the fact that
folks have had a surfeit of legs, limelight, slangy
jokes, and tinkly-tura-tum melodies. The Potter-
Bellew audience-ogling, grand mash combination
appear on Saturday night in “Romeo and Juliet,”
which will be followed by “ Hero and Leander,”
their great Melbourne success. Johnny Toole
will then emit a few good old wheezes from the
right-hand corner of his victual entrance close
down to the footlights prior to the production of
“Cinderella,” for which Mr. Musgrave has im-
girted the properties, dresses and music used at
rury Lane in the grand procession during last
pantomime season. By the way, it turns out that
Pantoudes de veir was the original title of this
charming story, but that the individual who
appropriated it for the English mistook the
obscure Latin word veir, which means soft fur, for
verve, the French for glass.
** * »
Harry Rickards now fills the bill, and
Sydney School of Arts, with “ Brio-a-Brac,”
popularly known as Break-a-Brick. in which he
sustains seven characters —that of ’Arry, the
London Toff, being a particularly fine, large and
life-like impersonation. The other members of
the company, including Mrs. Rickards, have
each three or four characters allotted to them, and
the whole thing—one of those absurdities, inter-
spersed with song and dance, that makes you
laugh, though reason says “No” —is such a
success that the entrepreneur has had to purchase
a buggy-horse to replace one that broke down
lately on the Randwick-road with the takings.
-** * *
At Bondi Aquarium, last Saturday, Mile.
Viola unhitched herself from Price’s hot-air soar-
ing-machine at a height variedly estimated from
5000 to 3000 feet, and dropped through the
empyrean with the grace and velocity of a falling-
star on to a sandy patch near Randwiek. This
was the lady’s twelfth Australian air-jump, and,
wind permitting, she will do it again next Satur-
day. For the Sunday concerts, the Wyburd has
engaged the Faust Family, the London Bell-
ringers, Julia Phillips, Harry Leston, W. Cadzow,
and others ; also, the American Midgets for a brief
term, while a party named Jaques Ibbot has
undertaken to fast for forty days. He has our
full permission to eat all night for forty nights.
** * *
Annis Montague’s illness in Brisbane for-
tunately did not last long. Her place was capably
if not fully, filled by Josephine Deakin.
** * *
The Phonograph, which has been doing
good business at Sydney Centenary Hall, plays
there till Saturday, when it skips to Newcastle
for a brief season. Lecturer Archibald informed
his audience the other evening that a melody
played into the instrument by Edison’s band, and
about to be re-produced for their benefit, had
been transcribed from the instrument by a Mel-
bourne musician, scored for the orchestra and
played at Melbourne Opera House for the Sappho
ballet though the authorised score, published in
America, has not yet reached this country. By
the way, there will be a matinee performance on
Saturday at 2.30, for the benefit of the profession
and others unable to visit the show at night.

nautical musical burlesque, localised up to date
and adapted for the present occasion,” dumb-

is not a photograph, nor a photo, a ghost—but a good photo, is a
true reflection of the original. For high-art photos, go to the
London Palace of Art, 10 Park-street, Sydney. Country photographers’ work from developement

“ Drink, pretty creature, drink.” What? The
London & Sydney Tea Co.’s pure teas,
“ Little Black-eyed Susan ” is the mutilated
corpse of a forgotten outrage, laid-out afresh in a
patchwork gown of puerile gags, dragged around
the stage to the most contemptible of music-hall
strains by a company consisting of Jennie Lee,
Sheridan, Forde, and a crowd of others, arranged
on a sliding-scale of stupidity. One or other of
the three artists mentioned is never 'away from
the footlights. Generally speaking, they are all
present at one time, flanked by the afore-
said crowd, and there they remain talking
rot, singing rot, and dancing rot until the
capacity of an Australian audience for taking
punishment without so much as a groan is estab-
lished beyond the shadow of a doubt. The
ordinary minstrel show, or pantomime, is a foun-
tain of sparkling novelty and wholesome fun as
compared with “Little Black-Eyed Susan,” and
although this alleged burlesque-which burlesques
nothing—may be adapted differently as it goes
along and improved by omitting a number of
tailors and bookmakers, who come right out of
Bourke-street in walking-costume to drag one
scene down to the level of a threepenny “gaff
yet the idiocy of the whole concern is beyond
redemption. A flabby ballet appears to be
dropped in promiscuously, and a lady described
as a premiere danseune from, Europe adds her
jnelancholy hop to an accumulation of misery
which is advertised to bring a “ brilliant ” season
to a close at the end of next week. But probably
Widow O’Brien will come to the rescue ere that
times expires.
** * *
“La Mascotte,” away up at the Princess’,
is fetching the multitude as of old. Maggie Moore
and Elton give the libretto with point and humour.
Kelly, as old man Rocco, is likewise a well-
balanced incident, and May Pollard’s contempt
for her lover Leumane has an air of reality about
it, as though the rolling gait of that tenor songster
filled her with a sweet, womanly desire to throw
a plate at his irritating trunk every time he
jerks it forward in caricature of a courtly
obeisance. Leumane grows very desolate and dree-
like of late. He and the ultra-vigorous Knight-.
Aston should club their anatomies together
and divide the proceeds in equal proportions, A
co-operative system would tend to their mutual
advantage, because the one wants toning up,
whilst the other stands in urgent need of toning
down. On Saturday the long-expected “ Gon-
doliers ” put in an appearance, and the full
strength of the old company will be freshened by
some new faces,

descendant—or great nephew, or something—is
collaring £lOO,OOO a year and free lodgings from
the British tax-payer to-day. Fancy getting all
that lot because your great uncle used to hide be-
hind a tree while his myrmidons fired low and laid
’em out, and for sixpence a day, too !
** * *
“Gratitude,” one of Christie Murray’s
new plays, was matineed at Melbourne Opera
House on Monday, with results which bode hurt
and mischief to the author in the event of his in-
flicting this lugubrious, inconsecutive sermon
Upon the dear old mother country. The idea seeth-
ing in Mr. Murray’s somewhat heavy head when
he ground out “ Gratitude ” was that some people
are not duly thankful for favours received, and
other people run to. the opposite extreme. He
works out this inspiration in four foolish acts full
of dull conversation and puerile situations.
Characters come in at one door, go out
at another, and return by a different pas-
sage, for the mere purpose of being there
in couples when the previous couple have
cleared out for the moment. They disclose
secrets to one another without due cause, and
they all talk like penny tracts, except a comic
servant, who acts as escape-valve for Christie
Murray’s spirits when they begin to bubble up
amidst the gloom of his moral purpose. In a
word, “ Gratitude ” is a bad attempt at a play—
weak in construction, dialogue and character, and
although this notice necessarily terminated at the
second act, the fact that it had to catch the
express train was felt to be a blessing. The
author seemed to shape well as an “ old man ”
actor in an uninteresting character, and George
Carey tried not to be too tiring as the nuisance
who is continually grateful. Most of the acting,
however, would have settled any sort of play. To
make matters worse they all seem imperfect in
their parts, the leading lady actually talking such
stuff as—“ The live rose blooms on the tree, but
the withered bud drops where it fell.”
