The one really humorous feature of the
late show at Melbourne Opera House was Horace
Bent, as good old Uncle Tom. A long course of
Christy - minstrelry has blunted the edge of
Horace’s pathos, and when he assumed an atti-
tude of prayer and requested the other slaves to
ioin him in a maudlin hymn the house experienced
a strange sense of something wanting. You see,
he didn’t wear a gigantic collar, or beat time with
a baggy blue gingham. The restraining influence
of stage traditions prevented him from favouring
the Loard with a stump speech. Yet his desire
to do all these things twinkled in the four corners
of Horace’s eyes and made him a heap funnier
than e ver. It was a pity the management couldn’t
give him more scope. When Grade Whiteford
lay dying, and everybody was doubled up by the
thought of losing such a little Eva, what wouldn’t
one have given to hear Horace fire off a forlorn
kind of “yoick,” as he thumped his skull with a
tambourine ? Or he might have wagged his tow-
wig at the fullgrown angel-child and said, “ O
Christmas ! ”
** * *
It is not generally known that William
Archer, now the leading London dramatic critic,
comes of a stock of Australian pioneers, being the
son of Thomas Archer, the Agent-General for
Queensland, one of the Archers, of Gracemere,
Rockhampton. The future authority on the stage
had a turn at bush-whacking up North some years
ago, but a very short experience removed all illu-
sions as to that mode of life, and he soon returned
to London. The present writer can well remember
being present at a debate which took place a good
many years ago on the morality of theatre-going
at the students’ debating society at Edinburgh
called the “Philomathic.” On that occasion a
young student named William Archer made his
maiden speech, and it was such an eloquent vindi-
cation of the theatre that its detractors were
ignominiously defeated. His next exploit was the
pamphlet on Irving, which, whatever its views
may have been, was at least criticism and not the
diluted hogwash so often served out as such. It
is Archer’s method to avoid as much as possible per-
sonal acquaintance with those whom he criticises,
in order that he may not be even unconsciously
influenced by personal considerations. His
Ibsenish proclivities may be ascribed in great
measure to the influence of early training in
Norway, where the family have connections and
interests.
"V. * * * *
Olympia still flourishes at Melbourne
Exhibition Building, but the public animus
against Texas Jack and his Wild West Show
seems to be chronic. That young man’s notion of
a rational entertainment would disgrace the
mental capacity of a goat. One dreadful speci-
men of his brain-work is a drama described as
“The Horse Thief.” Somebody disguised as a
larrikin loafs out on the tan-track prairie with a
triangular horse, and gammons to be wrapped in
slumber with his head on the animal’s broken
ribs, after which a second fellow picks up the
four-legged ruin and takes it nowhere in particular
until four other fellows cease spitting at the far
end of the prairie and proceed to lynch the burglar
of dog’s-meat by dangling him from the electric
light by a bit of string. Then they walk away and
the horse staggers back to his stall to see if he can
find a stray oat, and Texas Jack comes wobbling
out of the stable-yard on his wild broncho. When
Johnny arrives at the man on the string he doesn’t
let the victim down easy, as a lucid cow-boy
would, but he clambers off his 13-hands steed and
commences to blaze away at the strihg with a
blunderbuss until the robber drops down of
his own accord and wanders quietly off to
the cow-shed as though nothing had occurred.
Whereupon the people who have paid a
shilling, shout “Yah!” and Texas Jack bows
his acknowledgments. Another drama consists
of an omnibus being attacked by Indians,
and rescued by Jack and his cowboys, assisted by
a pen’orth of crackers fired off by the manage-
ment. The only solid incident in this scene is the
’bus. Nobody sets much store by the cowboys or
savages because they are generally understood to
be a mob of barrackers from Prahran, those with
feathers gummed on their legs acting as untamed
heathens, and the others pretending to be more
civilised. A prairie flower wearing pink tights
and spangles, casts her maiden smile around the
inside of the ’bus fora bit, but ultimately becomes
a bone of contention in distress until Texas Jack
swoops down as usual and the Wild West Show
dribbles into oblivion. The whole concern is an
entertainment which seems worth a shilling for
saying “dam” to, and as an excuse for insulting
behaviour with extenuating circumstances—not to
mention the canoodling possibilities hinted at in
last week’s number—it ought to catch on like a
lobster,
** * *
The Age’s attack on Maggie Moore was so
strikingly inconsistent with the same journal’s
“criticisms” on another line of shows that even
babes and sucklings have turned the matter over
in their cradles and found an explanation of the
phenomenon for their own little selves.
** * *
A company of Melbourne musicians went
down some time ago to give a concert in Geelong.
Madame Schiller and her brother were of the
crowd. The brother was told off to look after
Madame’s boxes containing evening dress, music,
&c. Dinner at Geelong over at 7.30. Concert
billed for eight. At former hour, boxes missing.
Had been labelled “ Adelaide ! ” Brother sent off
on a wild search after a dress. At 9 o’clock, he
found a squatter’s daughter who was persuaded by
her pa to lend her oldest finery. Madame played
some noise from memory, and the uninitiated said
they had never seen her so well-dressed before.
The concert lasted barely an hour, and the same
musicians have never been there since.
** * *
Miln says that Mrs. Potter is now twenty
times more of an actress than when he saw her in
America, and Mrs. Potter could return the com-
pliment, no doubt. There is a great similarity
between the two cases. The lady walked out of a
sassiety drawing-room, just as Miln descended
from a pulpit, to play “star” characters on the
stage, and they have both been starring ever since.
The tragedian’s parishioners were left like sheep
without a shepherd, and Potter —well, he seems to
be a shepherd without his pet lamb.
** * *
The final appearance of Mrs. Gown-Trotter
and Mr. Curly Blew at Adelaide was a veritable
triumph, for, perhaps, never in the history of the
theatre has such an enormous crowd assembled
within its walls. “Romeo and Juliet” provided
the cause of all this unusual and superfluous
excitement, and to assist matters Inman was
specially imported to play Mercutio. The manage-
ment must have a handsome profit on hand as a
result of the season. G. C. Miln’s term is only
for nine nights, with a fresh morsel of Shakespeare
each evening. Louise Jordan, W. J. Montgomery,
Oily Peering, Athol wood, Carle, etc., support the
tragedian.
The principal baritone of an Australian
comic opera Co. appeared with his right arm in a
sling the other evening. To the many questions
as to the cause, he told the history of a Sunday
excursion and a snake-bite; “but it was only a
whip-snake (!) and so no harm was done.” After
the performance a knot of friends stopped a well-
known comedian who was keeping dark on the
weather bow with a handkerchief round his
face. In answer to anxious queries, he had
fallen asleep while reading in the garden,
and been bitten by a snake. “ It’s really nothing,
a whip-snake, you know.” In a popular club the
conductor was beheld with a wreath of twisted
“hanky” and a bump upon his brow; “Hullo,
my boy, is it new opera or too much hat ?” “ Oh,
nothing much, I was out camping yesterday, fell
asleep and woke up with this disfigurement. They
tell me it is caused by the bite of a whip-snake.”
The good-natured individual who stepped in
between the big baritone and the band-box con-
ductor is most to be pitied.
At Poverty Point.
A Sydney letter-writer recommends ladies to remove
their hats in theatres. Some of them might also take out
their hair-pads ; they would also sit lower if they carried
their bustles in their laps.
On a blackened interior wall of the City Bank (Sydney)
may be seen hanging a photograph of George Rignold,
presented to the manager on his visit to Sydney in ’76—
the only thing that has not been destroyed. George’s
cheek is indestructible.
The four or five members of the Victorian Orchestra
who make periodical dives into the interior under the
title of the Melbourne Concert Company tell some funny
stories of their experiences out of town. On one occasion
they had a crowded house of six, and one of the instru-
mentalists had to stand in the ticket-office ready to return
the money taken, while a lady who was “ably assisting”
warbled “ Dear Love, how hard that we must part I ”
Warner has no opportunities in the new play of “A
Million of Money,” at Drury Lane, and the critics scarcely
mention him.
J. C. Williamson has engaged a splendid scenic artist,
Phil. Goatcher, from New York, and his first paintings
will appear in “The Gondoliers,” at Melbourne Princess’.
Goatcher is even a consolation for Gordon, and the legiti-
mate successor of Voegthn, Denim, and Matt. Morgan in
the States, as witness his magnificent scenery for Robson
and Crane’s “ Comedy of Errors.”
Spagnoletti writes: “Yes, Charley Mathews’ son was
very mysterious, yet not so mysterious as Mr. Murray
Herbert in the suite. He was a good young man, who
died after acting in Woodcock’s ‘ Little Game,’ with Mr.
and Mrs. Mathews at the Melbourne Royal. Y r oung Chas.
Mathews must, of course, not be confounded, as a barris-
ter, with Home Secretary Matthews. Old Charles never
could bear to go about without the boy, who could not
have come out of any industrial school. Besides, he was
a perfect little gentleman, like Affable Hawk.”
They say now that Williamson and Garner won’t go in
again for the lesseeship of the Melbourne Royal. Garner
will shunt for a turn at the Mabille side of the globe, the
top antipodes, while J. Cassius cribs at the Princess’, and
will with difficulty be restrained from acting a bit. Un-
doubtedly the outcome, independent managers all round,
will be better even than the good there has been.
Vastly amusing is the complaint of, perhaps, the fore-
most English contralto on the Oratorio stage, as to the
approaching necessity of her appearance in opera, and in
parts like Maffio Orsini, in “Lucregia Borgia,” and the
page Urbain, in the “ Huguenots.” A friend said it was
distressing to have to compress the female form divine
into hoys’ parts, because you only look like a holster done
up, and not a bit like a boy. “ No,” replied the contralto,
“ the mischief is that my figure is that of a perfect hoy,
and this is the deception to be discovered !” 800, boo !
Critic Archer went to the Ober-Ammergau Passion
Play, discussing it on the spot with Mrs. Bancroft, and
trying to piece up a cast on the London stage. Of course
there could be no question as to who should enact what is
called the Protagonist part, but they could not settle it as
to who was qualified to replace Sara Bernhardt. Archer
won’t enlighten his readers further on the details of his
cast, though they had assigned parts to Pilate, Herod and
Judas.
Chas. Mathews said to Jefferson, “Why, you are only
a one-part actor. Look at all my trunks —wardrobe for
50 parts.” Jefferson: “Which is the cleverer—a man
who acts 50 parts in one way, or one part in 50 ways ?”
Ask Toole, Greville, Appleby, and all the comic lec-
turers, as distinguished from the artists.
Litigation is begun between Brough and Boucicault, as
lessees of the Melbourne Bijou, and Wilson, the pro-
prietor, over a question in the electric-lighting. All along
it has been foreseen there would he friction. There can be
no electric-lighting without it.
Charles Naylor, an amateur vocalist at Bathurst
N.S.W.), has sung at 800 concerts for charitable and
other purposes, irrespective of class or creed and. without
fee or reward.
The Has Beens! Sir Theodore Martin writes a bitter
article, in Blackwood’s, on Ada Rehan’s Rosalind, which
has charmed and witched all the rest of London, but Sir
Theodottery finds that she doubles up her legs not at all
in the graceful way of Lady Martin —Helen Faucit, the
faded fight of other days. “ My wife, sir”—from V.ncent
Crummies down to—never mind.
In last week’s Bulletin mention was made of Ellen
Terry’s daughter, “ Edith Warded, otherwise Ailsa Craig.”
The damsel may be called Warded, out of compliment to
her mother’s second husband, Charles Warded (Kelly).
Her acknowledged father’s name was Godwin, but as she
was born before mamma had been divorced by G. F.
Watts, R.A.—well, Miss Edith’s surname is a point for
lawyers to decide.
J. R. Greville will shortly re-appear at Melbourne
Royal.
Before leaving Melbourne, Mrs. Brown-Potter presented
a well-known actress with a handsome diamond bracelet.
Fame is what one gets for being civil to newspaper-men.
Mr. James Brown-Potter’s “little” girl, now in San
Francisco, is, we read, a big buxom young one, with a
very slight suggestion of Cleopatra in her appearance,
and promises to develope a superb figure, being unusually
shapely for a child of ten.
Emma Wangenheim is married again—to her treasurer.
This, when you come to look at it, is a good piece of
business.
Alice Lingard is coming into prominence again—this
time as the mother of two daughters'who are shortly to
be married. Lulu, the older daughter, is to become the
wife of a lawyer, named Wicks, and her younger sister
Nellie is to marry a young physician, Arthur Godfrey, the
nephew of Dan Godfrey, the bandmaster.
Henry E. Abbey is moving heaven and earth to get
Mary Anderson to fulfill her contract with him, and it is
said that both she and her husband are weakening. If
she does return to the stage, she will play under the name
of Mine. Navarro.
Christie Murray’s acting made a really good impression
in “ Jim the Penman,” in Melbourne. Thoroughly easy,
and the only fault was the German dialect.
Delaney, the well-known musician, and Hinchy, the
amateur tenor, were walking Pitt-street, Sydney, the
other day, when they were asked by a couple of Union
men for assistance. Hinchy gave one of them a shilling,
and Delaney, addressing the other, remarked in his in-
imitable style: “ I cannot afford to give you coin of the
realm, like my wealthy friend, but if you have any spare
time on your hands I don’t mind teaching you the
piano.”
The Standard Dramatic Company, formed by the
secession of Frank Gerald, Stilling Duff, Charlie Berkeley,
R. W. Greville, Mrs. Collett-Dobson, Georgie Smithson and
Mabel Fitzroy' from the St. Maur-David Christie
Murray crowd, gave a classical and sacred concert at
Alexandra South on a recent Sunday evening, on behalf
of the local hospital, on which occasion the resident
minister—a Presbyterian, too !—actually had no service,
in order to give the show a fair chance ! ! ! !
A Gaiety dnnsense once telegraphed to the stage-
manager:—“Cannot come to rehearsal; met my uncle
in the Strand last night." The message ought to havf
read “hurt my ankle.”
Edwin Thorne, now again in Sydney, has some idea of
organising a company to play at the Gaiety. He says
that Australians “are so glutted, gorged, stuffed, and
surfeited with good things that a manager can’t stagger
them. When with Bland Holt we travelled with 200 tons
of scenery, but I found that effects which would have
brought down the house in New York were taken as a
matter of course in Brisbane and Adelaide !”
Sara Bernhardt travels with forty-eight trunks, exclu-
sive of packages. Twenty trunks, made of wood, are from
four feet high, and contain her most valuable dresses.
Her petticoats, linen, boots, &0., are carried in wicker-
work baskets, and her hats in specially-prepared boxes
fixed round with pegs so that they can’t be shaken or
crushed. Her travelling wardrobe altogether weighs
nearly three tons.
Charley Warner's’real name is Lickfold ; he is the sou
of a provincial actor, was intended for an architect, and
made his first start in stage life, at Henley, in “ Bras
Rouge.” He then succeeded Irving in the Batemans’
company and from that time was associated most fre-
quently with Samuel Phelps.
H. Vincent West, who so successfully piloted the
Wangenheim Opera Company through Queensland despite
strikes and floods, is now engaging artistes in Sydney for
the buxom Emma to take part in a Brisbane season, open-
ing on Boxing Night at the Opera House.
Says a London writer Personally, Charles Warner
is one of the most popular of men among his brother
actors. Without a trace of the self-conscidusness or con-
ceit which might, perhaps, be pardoned to one who,
though hardly yet in middle life, has reached the summit
of his profession. A keen sportsman, a bold rider to
hounds, a genial friend and companion, the soul of
generosity to the members of his profession less fortunate
than himself, Charles Warner is the beau ideal of a manly
English gentleman on the stage.” Thanks.
A London paper says that Johnny Toole, having a
theatre of his own, and being a rich man, was foolish
to run the risk of coming to Australia. He ran no risk ;
is too old a bird for that. On the contrary, he received, it
is said, £BOO weekly, for which sum he supplied a by no
means sparkling company, mediocre plays and some not
altogether paralysing acting.
The betting concerning the lesseeship of Melbourne
Royal is strong on Warner, who told everybody that he
would soon be back as a manager, and, of course, he had
“the office” from W. and G. about their clearing out.
For many reasons they would prefer him as a rival to any-
one else, and it is evident he has not struck Babylon
’twixt wind and water. Dampier, it is said, is seized with
that restlessness and desire to get on to a bigger thing
which never allows the entrepreneur to “ take it easy.”
Eille Norwood, of Thornton’s late company, is schooling
an amateur company, who perform his “ Hook and Eye”
and “ Weak Woman,” at Adelaide Theatre Royal, for the
benefit of the Children’s Hospital.
Colonel Henry Mapleson says that Marie Roze has
separated from him because the marriage is illegal, not
having been contracted according to law, and both are
free to marry again. He then adds that he has recently
obtained many good engagements for Madame Marie
Roze in England, “ where she is the idol of the public,”
and concludes by reiterating his admiration of the beauty,
talent, and voice of the fair cantstrice. All the same, it
looks as if the fair Marie were glad of an excuse to drop the
unlucky old entrepreneur.

The Shearers’ Keg.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“Now then, lads, give it a name, I’m stuck,
but I’m blowed if I thought old Billy could ’a
done it. Billy, ole man, I don’t play you no more
games level; twenty out of a ’undred is what I
want from you next time. Come along, old ’uu,
and wet your luck.” Saying which Mr. John
Bagus, landlord of “The Shearers’Arms,” put
his cue in the rack, ducked under the little half-
door that led into the bar, folded his arms on the
counter, and again pleaded with his audience to
“give it a name.”
A scrupulously clean man was Johnny Bagus>
physically, and his bar reflected in every detail
this one pleasing peculiarity of its master. A
room about fourteen feet square, with two
counters, one facing the billiard-room, the other
the bar-parlour, the two remaining sides being
shelved to the ceiling and loaded with gaudily-
labelled bottles, except in one corner where a
small door, decorated with an iron gating about a
foot square, opened into the “ dead-house,”
or temporary asylum for “drunks,” a skillion-
room with another iron gating in place of
a window, furnished with wooden bunks at the
sides, and a case supported by four legs driven
into the earthen floor to serve as a table. Casks
of beer, wines and spirits were ranged under the
bar counters on both sides, and under the tap of
each was a bright pewter measure to catch the
drippings. “ Sukey,” the black gin, a member of
Mrs. Bagus’ domestic staff, scrubbed out the bar
and put things straight the first thing every
morning under the supervision of Sam Golden
the barman, for, as Johnny used to say
“the missus can do what she likes with the
rest o’ the ’house but thisyer bar is where I makes
the spondulix what’s goin’ to put Johnny Bagus
tip top some o’ these yer days, when he’s
a-goin’ to be a J.P., and carry the plate round
among the parson’s lambs, and ’ave a ’orty French
guv’ness for the little Baguses, and by Jingo it's
got to be clean or sumthin’s goin’ to ’appen. ”
The beauty-spot of the room, though, was a
white enamelled earthenware keg, of about ten
gallons, with alternate hoopiugs of blue and yellow
at each end, and in the centre a Vermillion scroll
bearing in large golden letters the legend “The
Shearers’Keg.” By all the shearers and station-
hands up and down the river and away on the
back-blocks the rum drawn from that keg was
held to be superior to the best obtainable on the
Bendigo, or even in Melbourne. Johnny himself
described it as; “The best ole West Ingy rum,
the same as they ’as for christenin’s in Windsor
Castle. It come out of a pirates’ cave what they
found on one o’ them there islands nigh on to a
acre and a-’alf deep. A huncle o’ mine what’s a
bishop over there sends it to me straight; it’s
more’n 50 overproof, and I ’as to water it a bit,
else you couldn’t swaller it, ’cause it ’ud just
scorch the gizzard outen yer. There ain’t more’n
enough in the cave to last another year, and
there’s chaps out a-prospectin’ for another plant
all the time.”
Sain, the barman, had heard that yarn repeated
at intervals for over two years, but it always
made him feel hot, and angry, and thirsty, and
full of a desire to take Bagus by the throat and
choke him, if his poor, shaky, nerveless fingers
had been equal to the task; but he was only the
wreck of a man who had once been Lieutenant
Samuel Golden, cashiered from H. M.
Regiment by the verdict of a courtmartial for
haying been drunk on duty a week after the
allied troops landed in the mud at the head of
the little winding creek dignified by the name of
Balaclava Harbour. A weak and decaying
remnant of a gentleman was “ the Capting,” as
Bagus called him, who was allowed one nip
before breakfast and not another drop until after
four o’clock, when Johnny took charge of the bar
and Sam cleared out to his little room behind the
store and was seldom seen again till next day.
About 14 men rolled up for the offered shout,
exclusive of two who were in the dead-house. Five
or six of them were knocking down their shearing
cheques, and the others were principally township
loafers and “brokers,” encouraged by Johnny
for the purpose of assisting them. Bogolong Billy
had only come in that morning from the Murrum-
bidgee, with the result of ten months’ shepherding
and a ringer’s score at the shed, in his pocket, but
he didn’t mean to stop :
“No fear, mate. I’m a-goin’ through to
Eaglehawk this time, take my oath for it.
On’y I thought I’d just camp here to-night and
say good-bye to the boys and get away agin afore
sun-up to-morrer.” To which Mr. Bagus replied
“ Right y’ar. Billy, and I’ll hev yer ’oss up and
feed ’im fust thing, and call yer myself if yer
dosses it out too long. But yer’d better give me
any cheques or joolry yer’ve got so’s I kin lock it
up for yer, in case some o’ these yer chaps should
take a fancy to go through yer, don’t yer know.”
“Thet’s all right, Johnny. I ain’t a-goin’ to
lush, and I ain’t a-goin’ to stop, and the cheque is
sewed inter my trowsis and I shan’t take ’em off,
so ther ain’t no fear. ”
“ Well, jest as yer like, ole man. Come on now
and I’ll play yer a game o’ billiards on the new
table. Let’s see—we didn’t have no table the last
time yer was down, did we? No, course we didn’t.
I can t play much myself yet, and I ’spect yer can
beat me, Billy, so we won’t ’ave nothin’ on the
game, on’y drinks.” And Bogolong Billy, who
knew as much about billiards as he did about the
belts of Saturn, played with the man who could
make a sixty-break on the spot when it pleased
him, and —won it.
“ Now, boys, let’s ’ave these yer drinks, and
I’m a-goin’ to take twenty from Billy and try and
put ’im through. Hurry up, Capting, and take
some o’ these gentlemen’s orders.”
Just then a roar of laughter went up from the
other end of the counter, where Billy had an-
nounced his determination to adhere to “sass-
parell ” as a beverage, but the storm of chaff was
checked by a remonstrance from Mr. Bagus :
“Why, Billy, ole man, what’s come over yer?
Not but what any man can call for what he likes
at this yer bar when it’s my shout, but if it was
me, Billy, and a-startin’ on the road at daylight
too, I wouldn’t do it. That sort o’ rot-gut ain’t
good for a man like you. Take a little drop outen
the kag, ole son, then yer knows what yer a
drinkin’ of.”
And “ a little drop outen the kag ” it was. Hive
or six rounds followed in quick succession, and
Sam in the rush of business was expectorating
wildly on the floor, until Mr. Bagus gravely
observed : —“Oapting Golding, it seems to me, as
I’ve told you many times, that I don’t allow no
man nor women born to spit on rny bar-floor.
Ain’t them pewter pots good enough fer yer,
that’s what I want to know? Yer a man o’
birth and breedin’, ain’t yer ? Well, see ’ere
now, there ain’t no false pride about me, I’m
on’y Johnny Bagus, born on a doorstep in the
Whitechapel Road, reared by ’and in the
I oundlin, eddioated at Pentonville, and lagged
for refoosin* to drink the Queen’s ’ealth at the
Lord Mayor’s dinner. Yer never know’d me
guilty of such designin’ contrariness, and I’ve got
n igh on to three thousand golden quid in the
bank, blast yer, in this year o’ Manner Dominy
eighteen thousand and fifty-eight. Go ter bed,
yer boozy owl, and don’t provoke me to say things
to yer as I wouldn’t like these gentlemen to ’o«r.”
* # #
At 1 a .m., when the last light was turned
out by Mr. Bagus, that gentleman was the only
sober male person on the premises of “ The
Shearers’ Arms.” Bogolong Billy and three
others were in the deadhouse alongside the two
previous occupants. Billy’s cheque, amounting
to £97 17s. 6d., was in Mr. Bagus’ pocket, and
HE WASN’T TIRED.
GOOD CLERGYMAN: “WHY DO YOU PLAY MARBLES, MY LITTLE BOYS,
ON THE BLESSED DAY OF REST?”
THE WICKEDEST BOY: “DAY OF REST, WHAT ARE YOU GIVIN’ US?
WHY, WE AIN’T TIRED.”
on the back of the bar-shutter was a cuneiform
inscription done in billiard chalk, which, on
being translated for entry into the day-book the
following morning, clearly showed that £23 14s.
of that sum was due by the owner for refresh-
ments supplied to his order during the day.
At (1 a.m. Sam was in the bar according to
custom to superintend Sukey’s cleaning operations.
Mr. Bagus also appeared to take a hand in the
game by replenishing the keg, which bad been
emptied the previous night. That gaudy recep-
tacle for hell-broth was placed on the floor, a large
bung at the top removed, two figs of sheepwash
tobacco broken up small and dropped in, and
followed by the contents of all the pewter
measures, which comprised tap-drippings for the
week of beer, wine and spirits, and the incidental
surplus saliva for that time of the man who chewed
andthemanwhosmoked; next, four gallons of over-
proof rum, some 20 or 30 drops of a thick white
liquid out of a small bottle produced from Mr.
Bagus’ breast-pocket, and the remaining space
filled up with water. The mop was snatched
from Sukey’s hand, the handle inserted in the keg,
and a few vigorous turns mixed the various in-
gredients that together produced that delicious
compound on which the fame of the house mainly
rested. Half-an-hour afterwards there was .a
commotion in the dead-house, Sam drew back the
wooden shutter and the face of Bogolong Billy
appeared at the small iron-grating :
“Is that you, Sam? Quick for the love o’
’eaven, and gimme a drink. My throat's blistered,
and my head’s afire, and I’ve been in hell all
night. Where’s Bagus ? Let me out o’ this so’s
I can get down to the water-hole and put it out! ”
“ Here you are, old man. Bagus has got the
key, but he’ll be in directly. Here’s a ‘ doctor ’
for you, and you just lie down and keep quiet till
he comes.” And a gurgling hiss told that the
“doctor” had disappeared. Billy was re-
placed at the grating by the other thirsty
souls inside, for whom the prescription was
repeated at intervals until they all went to sleep
again. About 11 o’clock Bagus let them out and
Mrs. Bagus had a kettle of soup ready for any
demands. Then the fun began again and lasted
far into the night as before, and the evening and
the morning were the second day.
# # *
So live days passed, during which one man
wandered away into the bush in “the horrors,”
and one fell into the kitchen fire trying to get a
light for his pipe ; he was sent on to the doctor at
the township in a spring cart, fairly sober but
partially skinless, with a message from Bagus
that he had “had a fit.” And presently it came
to Billy’s turn :
“Well, yer know, Billy, I ain’t a ’ard man, and
yer can ’ave what rashuns yer want to take yer
back to the station, and a bottle o’ the real stingo
out o’ the ‘ kag ’to ’elp yer on the road. But yer
shouldn’t ’a done it, Billy. I tried all I could to
stop yer, and it weren’t no good ; yer would ’ave
it, and yer would keep on shoutin’ for all ’ands
every time, till I ses to the missus, ses I, ‘ I never
seen sich a man as Bogolong Billy. He’s the
most blarstedest deterrmnest cove as ever come
into this ’ouse.’ You arst ’er if I didn’t.”
“And what about the ’oss, Johnny, and the
saddle and bridle?”
“ Well, yer sold him to Sam, yer know for ten
quid, and the missus ’erself did ’er level best to
stop yer doin’ that, but I never did see such a
chap as you are, Billy—no, I never did, true’s
I’m a livin’ man.”
* * #
Six weeks afterwards, all that the native
dogs had left of Bogolong Billy was buried by a
sheep-drover near the Pine Ridge, on the Old
Man Plain. There was no public-house there in
those days, and no water within twenty miles.
Bagus fell out of a buggy, and was swept away
and drowned in the big flood that came years
after, and his name was on the list for a J.P.-ship
at the time it happened.
I am able to give all the particulars connected
with the brew of the shearers’ keg, because I kept
Bagus’ books for a month, and was in and out of
the bar a good deal and saw it all myself.
THE BUNYIP.
Madame D’Uzes found heaps of money to
be used by Boulanger. He made her acquaintance
thus ; his officers used to go stag.hunting at hex-
chateaux, but were stopped by Boulanger, who
dropped into the stag-hunting fascination himself.
Now she calls him old and fat. •
Roundabout.
Some two years ago an Auckland soli-
citor, moved by unwonted compassion—or, per-
haps, being intoxicated—gave a sovereign to a
young woman in great distress, and then lay low
till Providence should send him his reward." Tij e
unaccustomed emotion which went through him
on that honourable occasion nearly blew his head
off, and often, in after days, the memory of that
good action warmed his soul and made him feel
better. And on a certain disastrous Friday last
month his benevolence bore fruit, and his good
deed was returned to him a hundred-fold. A
respectable old gentleman with white hair called
in and told him that the woman in question was
his erring daughter, now happily restored to him,
and then he broke down and wept so copiously on
the table that he became unintelligible for nearly 15
minutes. At last, however, he revived, and blew
his nose, and,smothered the feelings which—in his
capacity as a father —threatened to overcome him,
and explained that he had come to refund the
twenty shillings. Business being bad, and his
office-rent in arrears, the lawyer gladly assented,
and then the overwhelmed parent gave him a bad
£5-note with tears in his eyes, and prayed that the
God of Abraham, Isaac and the other fellow
might bless him while he collared the change.
Two hours later the benefactor got on to the
situation, and then he blew the window out with
his language, and tore the poker in two, and had
to drag all his clothes off to avert suffocation, and
fly round the room with nothing on except his
eyeglass. Next time he is called upon to do a
benevolent action he intends to restrain himself if
he has to climb over his mother’s dead body tc
do it.
* # #
A vigilant policeman was shaking the solid
earth with his hoofs in Heffernan’s Lane, Mel-
bourne, one morning last week, when a strange-
looking object met his eye and made him pull up.
It was leaning against a dead wall and looked
like a smashed umbrella covered with old cat’s-
meat—at least it looked as much like that as any.
thing else, but it had no very striking resemblance
to any object that the officer had ever seen or
heard of before. His first idea was to rush iq
and arrest the spectre, but more prudent counsels
prevailed and he hovered round. Then the object
began to look like a shattered harp with ribbon tied
all over it, but when the constable escaped to the
back of it and viewed it from the rear it seemed tc
be a very old corpse in rags. Finally he threw
his baton at it and exclaimed, “ Spake, ye
monkey ! ” and the exploded goblin turned round
and said, “ Ching a ring chow ; hang lee.” That
explained the matter. It was a leper, and the
officer clutched it on a charge of being in a public-
place with intention to commit a felony. He
seized it by the shoulder, but when two pounds of
the heathen spook came away in his grasp he let
go with a yell. It was evident the object couldn’t
stand strong measures, and requited to be arrested
with caution. He got it to the lock-up at last,
however, by running behind it and yelling and
throwing stones, and shaking a stick at it;
and the nefarious thing created a sensation
m court next morning. Nobody knew what
to do with it or where to put it, and as it
couldn’t be fixed, or hung, or bound over to keep
the peace, the Bench at last resolved to send it up
for twelve months, and give it a chance to infect
the other prisoners. The leper is a difficult
problem to contend with. Anybody who wants
to understand the obstacles with which he is sur-
rounded should read the gruesome story of Hanno,
the Carthaginian, in that blood-stained novel,
“ Salammbo.” The revolted Mercenaries tried to
settle a complicated problem in his case by cruci-
fying the obnoxious monopolist in his last stage of
decay ; but even that violent device was only a
partial success, for he flew to pieces on the cross,
and in half-an-hour all that was left was six old
scraps hanging on nails, and a hunk of skin
deposited on the ground. The only wholesome
expedient is to chase the patient into the sea
whenever his complaint is fully developed, and
leave him to be scavengered away by the lobster
and carnivorous prawn. To let him die in a cell,
and then confine anybody—even a fraudulent
Rechabite, or a colonel who wants to “fire low ”
—in it afterwards, is cruelty worthy of the
Inquisition or the Council of Ten in its palmiest
days.
* * *
There are almost as many dangers about
being a private detective as there arc" in being a
Social Purity officer, and sacrificing one’s eye in
the cause of Terewth. Last week a lady residing
at Moonee Ponds (Vic.), hired one of these obser-
vant persons to inspect the goings-on of the old
man and see if his morals were particularly
correct, and with the genius of his tribe he
muddled the job and got into trouble. His plan
of campaign was to consider the patient through
the window and the keyhole, and by way of
corroboration he took no less than three respectable
Christian friends along with him—gentlemen
whose testimony would stand inspection if it was
necessary to swear to anything bad. They went
along together after dark, and if the party could
have climbed the wall quietly all would have been
right, but unhappily the Christians were short
and thick and fat, and couldn’t jump over two-
pennorth of eggs. Moreover, one of them had to
sneeze violently on top of the wall, and next
moment the defendant rushed out with a stick
and hit everybody on the head. He struck out in
all directions with the unreasoning frenzy of a
wild beast, and in about two minutes the whole
party was swelled up to such an extent through
assault and battery that it couldn’t have got
through a gate, and was so much disfigured as to
be unfit for human inspection. The whole four
were in a fair way to be killed, only their assailant
fainted from excitement and over-exertion, and
they got their second wind and retreated on
crutches to the hospital, leaving the field of battle
to the victor, and also the spoils of war—four
shattered hats, two walking-sticks, the atoms of
an umbrella, a maimed eye-glass, a flattened
respirator, a prayer-book, one golosh, and part of
the back of a human head. Next day the conqueror
organised himself afresh, and charged upon the
detective with a summons, and that energetic
public servant was fined £3 and costs. Any
further evidence against the old man will be
collected with a telescope, or else a would-be
suicide will call upon him and chance the con-
sequences.

Zola himself is not a bit disturbed by his
wife’s indifference to his writings. He says that
he married her, not account of her intellect, but
on account of her heart, and thinks that it is a
great mistake for any man, especially if literary,
to do otherwise.

Cardinal Newman, in his “Apologia,”
says he had almost made up his mind to celibacy
at 20, but on two occasions after that he was
seized with doubt, though he ultimately made his
resolve at the age of 30, which was 15 years before
he changed his church. He therefore lacked the
experience of his widower friend, Cardinal
Manning.

Prof. Thorold Rogers, whose death is
cabled, was the model for Dickens’ Gradgrind, in

novelist’s most unjust portrait. The drawing was
more distorted and the colouring less true than
Leiorh Hunt's (“ Harold Skimpole ”) and Laing
as Fang, the Police-Magistrate in “ Oliver Twist.”
Laing relieved many a poor wretch from his own
pocket.
The Prince of Wales is alleged to have
relapsed into a despondent state again. JuJat
present he finds no joy in pretty Yankee girls,
regards eating and drinking as mere vanities,
wearies of dropping his money at whist, and. in a
general sort of way, wishes himself dead. When
Carington comes to call upon poor Tummy he will
probably discover the royal ruin immersed in a
treatise upon diseased livers.

The N.S.W. Legislative Assembly has
had, since the inauguration of Responsible
Government, seven Speakers. The first, Sir
D mini Cooper, was a wealthy merchant; the
second, SirT. A. Murray (dead), a retired squatter;
the third. Sir John Hay. a squatter and banker ;
the fourth, W. M. Arno'd, a farmer on the Pater-
son River, who lost his life at flood-time in
endeavouring to save half-a-dozen pumpkins; the
fifth. Sir George Wigram Allen (dead), a wealthy
solicitor: the sixth, Edmund Barton, a barrister
in active practice ; and the seventh and present,
John Henry Young, a produce-agent, who, having
become insolvent, is now resigning. Sir Wigram
Allen occupied the chair for the most lengthened
period—Bo months.
Father Ahearne, a temperance reformer
on the Hunter River (N.S.W.), has just died.
The father never gave the pledge for life if he
could help it, 12 months being generally his limit.
Towards the end of that time he would wait upon
the “case” and say ; “ Well, Pat, ye’ve done well
this year. Ye’ve a tidy little bit by in the bank,
I’ll be bound—try the pledge for another year.”
And although Pat badly wanted a spree and was
looking forward to his release from the pledge to
indulge in one, he found himself again bound for
another year.
H. R. Nicholls, editor of the Hobart
Mercury, contributes an article on Imperial
Federation to the United Service Monthly for
September. Nicholls is an old “Eureka,” and
one of the half-dozen front-rank journalists of
Australasia. At the great dinner given to the
delegates attending the first Federal Convention,
he made what was admitted by all present the
speech of the occasion. Unhappily, not a syllable
was reported. The visiting reporters trusted to
the Mercury fellows for a “note,” but the ever-
modest Nicholls had ordered his men “ to drop
him.”
There is now living at Mortlake, (Vic.), a
gentleman nonagenarian, Mr. Rowe, who in Ids early
days was a page at the house of the poet Byron.
He was engaged by the poet soon after the latter’s
marriage, and remained with his wife some time
after the separation. Mr. Rowe, who is in full and
staunch possession of his faculties and has a sur-
prising memory, is a believer in Lady Byron, and
lays on her husband all the blame for the unfortu-
nate domestic relations of the pair. It gives one
quite an old-world feeling to talk to one of the
household of a man who had been dead some time
when Macaulay reviewed him sixty years ago.

N.S.W. Assembly now is a member who assigned

to have his seat vacated ; but, then, he supports
the Government.
S.A. Legislative Council has rejected the
provisions for rating municipal properties on the
unimproved value on two occasions, and the
Assembly has now sent it up a third time as a
real challenge of battle.
South Australia, notwithstanding her
vaunted democracy, is only just moving for free
education ! And the State education of N.S.W.
is neither free nor secular. To be justly compul-
sory, education must be both free and secular.
The apparently inevitable result of the
West Sydney contest will, let us hope, teach
Labour a lesson in fighting elections. It is as
well that the lesson is to be learned at a bye
instead of at a general election involving four
seats.
The present Queensland Assembly is com-
posed of 72 members, of whom 35 are Government
supporters and 36 Oppositionists ; that is to say,
the Government have not the power to out-vote
the Opposition, and the latter, on the other hand,
are not able to oust the Ministry.
That “ Honest Tom ” Playford is nearing
his political horizon is certain. His budget speech
was poor, his leadership of the present Ministry
is undecided and weak, and his old Conservative
ideas are now dragging him down. This threatens
to be “ Honest Tom’s ” last Ministry.
“ It would be the best day ever seen if we
returned to the moral condition of society—if we
took what obviously belongs to the whole and
made it the property of the whole, and gave the
full opportunity for man to produce according to
the gifts God has given him.”— Gilmour , the
Adelaide Socialist parson.
Watch for trouble in Holland. A doting
Monarch, of 73, will leave a young Queen-daughter
aged 11, a playmate for the bib-and-tucker King
of Spain. Germany meanwhile gathers up its
coils, being in want of the Dutch seaboard and the
rich Dutch colonies, especially Java, and the
chunks of Sumatra, Celebes, and Borneo.
With the death of the Dutch King, his
Duchy of Luxemburg will revert to the Duke of
Nassau, a German. The town of Luxemburg used
to be the strongest place in Europe, 34 miles north
of Metz, and quite apart from Holland, on the
Belgian frontier. The fortifications have long
been dismantled, on International Treaty, but
Germany will soon set about re-creating them.

tory to say of the last annual gathering of the
Cobden Club than: “The Clubbites celebrated with
such joy of heart and hope as they could the cir-
cumstance that the whole world declined to follow
inCobden’s footsteps.”

With the sentiments expressed by editor
Hoolan in the Queensland Assembly the other
night with respect to the lash, The Bulletin
thoroughly agrees. No man has a right to repre-
sent white men in Parliament who would not be
prepared, in the event of his being lashed in cold
blood for a deed he had done in hot blood, to
commit suicide. To any man with a spark of
sensitiveness in his nature, life after the lash
would be no longer worth living except for the
purposes of revenge.
France’s decline in population, threatens
soon to take the appalling form of there being
more deaths than births. A century ago France
had a population of 2(5,000,000, Russia bad only
25,000,000, Austria 17,000,000, Prussia 15,000,000,
and England 12,000,000. To-day Russia possesses
00,000,000 inhabitants, Germany 40 000,000,
Austria 38,000,000 and France 30,000,000. The
birth-rate is (>*7 per 1000 in Italy, 10 in Ger-
many, 12-9 in Russia, 13-7 in England, and
1-19 in France. The disparity of increase on the
part of France as contrasted with Russia and
Germany, is, of course, not wholly due to a loiler
birth-rate: her territory has shrunk, while theirs
has been greatly extended.

The editor of a moral publication issued
in New Zealand allows his “Constant Reader”
to hold forth in half-a-column of print on the evils
of drink and cigars. Here is a sample :
Every young man entering upon life should pray for
strength to resist the carnal indulgences of drink and
tobacco. They are a clog upon the higher spiritual life,
the life of the soul, and every person who uses either of
them is in danger of taking the downward road. One
case occurs to me particularly—that of two friends whom
I once knew. 1 was able to persuade one of them to
abandon these evil indulgences, but the other refused to
give them up I lost sight of them afterwards, &c., &c.
There is a rather unfinished look about this story,
for the writer never found his two friends again,
and, therefore, we rise to offer him some further
information about one of them. He continued to
abstain from drink and tobacco, and saved up the
price of these worldly indulgences until he was
able to get married and be happy. If he had
wasted his substance on champagne and cigars it
is to say that he couldn’t have got mar-
ried. After a while his wife emptied some boiling
water over him and assaulted him with’ coals, and
when he defended himself with the tongs he was
arrested for violence and got fourteen days. By
the time he came out the lady had taken all the
money and his clothes and the furniture, and
eloped with a hard-drinking individual who smoked
a short clay pipe. Ho never saw her again, but he
remained steady in the faith, and was killed
through the overturning of a kerosene-lamp at a
teetotal meeting. We give these facts from per-
sonal knowledge, as we were the other fellow
alluded to in the above letter. We escaped the
conflagration, as we happened to be away smoking
and having a beer at the time the awful disaster
took place. Some time afterwards wo were in-
duced to reform our ways and become a Recha-
bite, but after two weeks of it we fell away and
were expelled. As the building fell in on top of
the society three days later, we did not regret the
circumstance, and wo have continued to liquor up
at reasonable intervals ever since. Our life haying
been twice saved by drink, we respectfully decline
to take any more risks.

Society
At Redfern railway-station, Sydney, a
areepy sight was seen the other day. A politician
was returning to Sydney after some months’ ab-
sence, when, by way of greeting, another politician
whose leading characteristics are his bad grammar
and his bigotry, rushed up to him and kissed him
on the cheek. Ugh !
# # #
A sensation was caused some years ago by
the elopement from Sydney of a particularly hand-
some woman, wife of a professional man, with a
pallid-faced Soudan warrior with enormous feet.
Word has just reached Australia that he some
time ago deserted her and that she has since died—
probably out of joy at getting rid of him.
* * *
We would be glad to know if the dignity
of the empire would suffer to any great extent
supposing the Admiral should cease to fire a big
gun every morning during his stay in Sydney
Harbour, or, at least, if he should bombard in a
somewhat lower key. Every time the thunderous
old howitzer goes off the patients in the hospital
sit up in affright, and every now and then some
weak and nervous subject is blasted into eternity
by the shock, and then his surviving relatives
grow disloyal, and the invisible “bond” which
unites us to the mother country is damaged m
consequence. The “bond” never did amount to
much, and it can’t stand having a gun fired at it
regularly every morning.
* * *
CARNIVAL REGRETS.
Ouptime is at hand with its bookmaking clamour
And wearisome talk of “twenty to one,”
But the Cup of to-day is bereft of the glamour
Of those that are gone.
No more for the tip of the touter we hunger,
Nor lend willing ear to the man in the street
As of yore when the heart in our bosom was
younger
And faster its beat.
No visions delusive we see in our slumber
Of thoroughbreds sweeping o’er Flemington
sward ;
No spectral official hoists uncanny number
On phantasmal board.
Heigho ! for the days when our hopes’ disappoint-
ment
Dropped as a tear in a Cupfull of bliss,
Like a bruise to the child whose infallible ointment
Is one loving kiss.
Say, are we content to be older and sager,
And richer, and greater in inches around
Than when we believed in a “good double ” wager,
And “did in ” our pound ?
Would we not that the past were returning to-
morrow ?
Those foolish Cup dreams which we formerly
dreamt,
We think of them now with a soft sigh of sorrow,
And not with contempt.
They are envious eyes that, at Carnival seasons,
We turn to the Stand, or the Hill, or the Elat,
Where once we were glad, for the slightest of
reasons,
To throw up our hat.
But now we are portly, and pompous and proper,
And quiet and cool as a corpse in his shroud—
O ! why can’t we flourish our new grey bell-topper,
And bawl with the crowd ?
The spell of the Lawn, lackaday ! it is' broken ;
We love not the beauties who glide to and fro,
Their words are not sweet like the tender words
spoken
So long, long ago.
The reddest of cherries Madge wore, in a bunch, on
The smartest of hats fastened back from her
brow,
We were too much engaged to care twopence for
luncheon —
We care for it now.
Cuptime come again ! bringing troubles and
pleasures
In yearly proportions of twenty to one ;
We welcome it not, but our memory treasures
Those Cups that are gone.
The latest eulogy on that incipient
Governor, Jersey, states that “ Ho is courtly and
business-like, and possessed of a deep interest in
manufacturing pursuits.” This doesn’t seem to
amount to much. The draper’s assistant is
courtly, for example, and the invoice-clerk in
M'Millan’s warehouse is business-like ; in fact, he
has got to be business-like or he will be promptly
fired out. And as for the “interest m manu-
facturing pursuits,” the remark applies equally
well to the man who drives a loom, or the fellow
who repairs a sewing-machine. We are glad to
hear that Jersey possesses these valuable qualifi-
cations, but to make him a Governor with
thousands a year on the strength of them is about
as sensible as to appoint a man who habitually
opens oysters in a restaurant to take charge of
the bombardment of a city on account of his in-
timate acquaintance with shells.
* * #
Lady Hopetoun’s manner towards some
Melbourne sassiety women, who came with floral
offerings to see their Governess off by the Adelaide
express, was something more than chilly. The
Bulletin is very cross indeed with Lady Hope-
toun. Pictorially she is a nice young person
enough, and has a certain quiet air of being able
to say something rather funny behind her fan.
But her cruel treatment of the native nobility—
even admitting that several of them deserve to’ be
crushed with a steam-hammer—shows a want of
consideration for the fact that she was sent out to
tighten those “silken bonds.” The fairy seems to
have proved an awful failure in that respect, and
though one of her husband’s suite may, or may
not have been knocked into pulp by an infuriated
husband, we can’t see that such a casual insult to
the family pride affects the main point. •

some years ago, a manual of book keeping for
schools, and a “ friend of Government House”—
we would not for a moment imply he was in-
spired—sent a copy to a principal official in the
Education Department with a letter suggesting

# * #
L. Cohen is to be re-elected Mayor of
Adelaide. Little Cohen has spent treble the
mayoral screw in entertaining the Adelaide
people, and has been a very good antidote to the
reluctant hospitality of the local Government
House.
* * #
Lady Kintore’s return to S.A. depends
upon private matters, and it is almost certain her
absence will, in any case, be prolonged. Her trip
vid America is to be without escort—her only
companion being her son.
# # #
The site of Adelaide Town Hall is the
block purchased for Matthew Davenport Hill,
“the Hanging Recorder,” alias “the Penal Re-
former,” on the first land-sale of the South Aus-
tralian Company. The purchase-money was £6 ss.
What does the unearned increment tot up to-day ?
# * *
The original manuscript of a diary kept in
V.D.L. and N.S.W. from 1835 to 1850 by a lady
—wife of an officer in the 50th Regiment (the
“Fighting Half Hundred”) —has come into the
possession of a Bulletin man. Landing at
Hobart Town from the Augusta Jessie, convict
transport, on the 114th day “out,” the writer of
the diary was delighted “to receive an invitation
from the pleasing wife of the Attorney-General to
a fancy ball,” and then flashes this side-light on an
historic character :
Our host was one of the most gentlemanly, clever men
I have ever had the pleasure of meeting, notwithstanding ”
(italics Tub Billktis’s) “his telling me that Providence
had intended him for an upholsterer, because he had so
much taste in furniture !”
And she calmly gives her gentlemanly clever host
away by identifying him as the Chief Justice of
New South Wales of a later year.
* * *
Nineteen men are now imprisoned in
Darlinghurst Gaol for not supporting their wives !
The latter must feel much comforted by this
ingenious and effectual legal remedy,
# * *
That Bathurst man who, the other day,
got six months’ for making a bargain for another
man’s wife and marrying her, is not the first of his
class in the west. The Bulletin knows a very
prosperous, pious, and persevering man over the
mountains who bought his wife for a bottle of
“Old Tom,” and lived happily till death put in
its claim.
* # #
Mr. Justice Foster (N.S.W,), in summing
up the evidence in a case of bigamy at Bathurst
the other day, remarked that “ this case was
degrading to the country. ... It was such a
case that in England and other places the people
would stand aghast in horror .” Now, why should
Mr. Justice Foster talk such nonsense ? In
England “and other places” people are not half
so much addicted to being hysterical as are the
legal authorities of happy New South Wales,
which hangs for twenty-three offences as against
two made capital by the English code. Perhaps
one of these days, when England gets to know,
per medium of some travelling Froude or Dilke,
that you can be tied up and flayed at the
triangles under N.S.W. law for taking out your
lead pencil and absent-mindedly writing your
name on a public building, England will “ stand
aghast with horror.”
# # #
Judge Molesworth delivered sensible re-
marks at Melbourne Church Assembly, on Bishop-
importing, pointing out that in 16 years only 10
students of Trinity College, Melbourne, had gone
in for theology, and with an inference that these
were comparative nincompoops. Suppose the
Judges were imported from England, what sort of
a Bar should we have ? A Mr. Justice Goe and a
Chief Justice Saumarez Smith !
# # *
Seventy-four years ago an Australian-born
boy left N.S.W. for England with his father,
a non-commissioned officer in an English line-
regiment. On arriving at the age of 15, the boy
quarrelled with his father, returned to N.S.W.,
and was heard of no more by his relatives, nor did
he hear of them. Years went by, one of the
youth’s two sisters died, and the other came to
N.S.W. as a governess, in the hope, she said, of
finding her only relative, but though she made
during a residence of 50 years all the search prac-
ticable, she never once found trace of her long-lost
brother. Yet, despite all her failures, she ceased
not to make enquiries concerning anyone bearing a
name in the least like that of her family. A few
weeks ago at , in a N.S.W Western town,
the long-estranged brother and sister met, he a
bent man of 76, she but a few years younger. He
did not even know her name, he mistook her for
the sister who had died.
And then they, the brother and sister whose
companionship was of the morning and the even-
ing only, walked hand in hand to the bottom of
the hill at the setting of the sun Not
so. They quarrelled bitterly and parted for
ever within a few hours, for had not he allowed
his dead wife to bring up her daughters in a
religion alien to that professed by her sister !
* * *
The robberies perpetrated on respectable
married men by the nymphs of a certain Mel-
bourne bagnio are a scandal to the morality of
that city. It is right enough that erring bachelors
should be compelled to leave all their portable
property (except the clothes they get drunk in)
upon the altar of Venus, but the married inno-
cents must really be let off cheaply for the sake of
their wives and children. And the law must
provide for a special reduction in the case of a
K.C.M.G., or other representative person. Such
as Sir , but no matter.
* # #
A memorable ass writes in a British
magazine that Australian loyalty is likely to fade
md -ss a prince comes round occasionally to freshen
the old sentiment up. Unluckily a prince is
gen 'rally the chief cause of disloyalty, at least wo
never heard of any active disloyalty except when
there was a prince about. To be disloyal—in the
ordinary acceptation of the term—without one is
like trying to get up an insurrection in an unin-
habited country. Moreover, even assuming that
the presence of a monarch rr her relatives is rn
I noentive to loyalty, it is worth noting that there
are between forty and a hundred such individuals
in the United Kingdom, and the population has
started to hiss when it sees them ; therefore, if all
this gathering cannot keeps things level in two
small islands, what influence can possibly be exer-
cised on a whole continent by a mere fleeting
glimpse of the meanest and least dignified of the
whole Imperial crowd ? If a hungry horse could
be fed upon the photograph of one solitary oat
the scheme might work—otherwise it has got to
collapse,
• * #
The Rev. Stacy Chapman, D.D., who is
now before the public with an anti-strike
pamphlet, happens to be one of the league that
announced some weeks ago that they would not
perform the marriage ceremony for people once
divorced under the present Victorian Divorce Act.
Bnt, then, Dr. Chapman probably considers that
he is not in this connection “on strike,” and an
agent of “ social oppression ” abominable in
intention and morally illegal, also a breaker of
the laws of the land.
# * *
When William Henry Leighton Bailey
assured a Melbourne Judge the other day that he
had not run away from .Sydney with the funds of
the defunct Australian Prudential Insurance Co.
he asserted the simple truth. The charge- never
yet answered—against the directors of that
society was that they never had any funds worth
speaking of. The concern was the funniest of the
many funny things floated as companies in
Sydney. Its original capital was £5OO borrowed
on the life-policy of the secretary.
* tt ' #
It is gratifying to learn that the Premier
Building Society defendants will not be required
to occupy the dock at their trial, like any
ordinary, degraded individual who is suspected of
prigging a handkerchief or two boots. Also they
will not be searched. Any plain, common,
humble defendant, when he surrenders to his
bail, is taken downstairs and inspected lest he
should have come provided with firearms or some-
thing, and should open fire on the court when lie
is convicted; but the policeman who looked for
hidden weapons on ten eminent citizens, all well
provided with cash, would probably be struck by
lightning for conspicuous impiety towards the
upper classes.
* * *
Melbourne Tramway Co. is doing well by
the strike, owing to the large reductions made in
the mid-day train service on all suburban lines.
* * *
This is about the time of the year when
the Australian clergy have their annual conferences
in Melbourne. They like to be there to help the
officers of the Fleet to contend against the devil’s
work during Cup time.
* # *
There’s a prominent member of the Angli-
can Assembly down South who never attends
divine worship except on the special occasions
when services to welcome new-coming or speed
going bishops are held. And yet he says he
attends frequently.
* * #
Mr. Crick is moving in the N.B. Wales
Assembly for the papers in the case of the Mount
Rennie cabman, Sweetman, sentenced to 14 years
and two floggings under circumstances unspeak-
ably creditable to the administration of the law.
It is understood that the prisoner, who was saved
from the floggings by medical veto, is now crippled
from paralysis.
* * *
Mrs. Bourke, of Sydney, has been tried
and acquitted on one of those charges of child-
murder which are so often incidental to what is
politely termed baby-farming. Without reference
to the correctness of the verdict in Mrs. Bourke’s
case, The Bulletin unhesitatingly predicts that
these acquittals will follow one after the other so
long as the death-penalty hangs to the offence and
the recommendations of juries are persistently
disregarded by Judges and Ministers.
* * *
The Cable mentions another awful and
fatal fire at a fashionable American hotel. How
about the Australian Sky and other Palaces ?
Some night you will see millionaires in their
night-shirts, crawling on the parapet, 100 feet
high, worse off than Dives in Hades, and they
will dive on the telegraph wires, which will cut
them to pieces.
* # #
What the Church doesn’t know about
assimilating the £ s. d. is too small to be worth
mentioning. At a holy bazaar recently held out
West, a small room was curtained off from the
hall, and male Christians were admitted at a
charge of 3d. Inside, twelve of the prettiest girls
in the town could be kissed for a shilling apiece,
or a hungry client could kiss the whole lot for
half-a-sovereign. If he insisted on hugging the
patient it was two-and-six. Most of the visitors
took the whole ten shillingsworth, and that night
the entire town could hardly have raised the price
of a pint of beer. If a conflagration with a pesti-
lence behind it had raged through the district,
the population couldn’t have been more utterly
cleaned out. The success of this experiment leads
us to make a suggestion. Why not let the prettiest
girls in the church take turns at passing the plate,
and let them hug each worshipper who subscribes,
say, half-a-crown or upwards. As a financial
device this would knock the plain, unvarnished
gospel into a cocked hat, and many a low, coarse
scoffer who now holds aloof would be gathered in,
and would listen to the voice of Terewth.
* # #
It has been decided on the authority of a
BrifXh magistrate that a British female who
doesn’t own property has no right to be alluded
to as “Mrs.” A certain leather-headed person
named Colonel Ford was the individual who
struck this amazing fact. Someone persisted in
alluding to a witness—the wife of a bricklayer—as
“Mrs. Lansdowne,” till the Bench came down on
him like an avalanche of broken crockery, and
told him to call her “ the woman Lansdowne,”
and she was carefully alluded to as “ the woman ”
during the rest of the proceedings.

At Melbourne District Court last week,
one John Mitchell was fined sixty shillings “for
being wilfully guilty of not allowing another
person, when practicable, to pass on the right or
off side.” The other person was the Governor,
and Mitchell’s offence consisted in not immedi-
ately clearing his buggy off the vice-regal track at
the bidding of a mounted lacquey. Seeing that
the evidence of felonious intent was purely sup-
posititious, inasmuch as Mitchell’s turning to one
aide, instead of to the other side, only “seemed
wilfully done in order to obstruct,” the penalty
inflicted by Magistrate Panton can only be justi-
fied by that unwritten law of “loyalty” under
which no Melbourne citizen can breathe freely
when his Excellency is nigh. It was more than
sufficient anyway without the gratuitous insult of
a speech from the ultra “loyal” beak, who told
defendant that, although he is an American sub-
ject, “his own good sense ought to tell him to
make way for the Governor.” What made
Panton an authority on good sense ? The Beer-
thirst incident, we presume.

THE STREETS OF MELBOURNE.
When the Governor drives abroad
To or from his free abode,
All who board and feed my lord
Are condemned to clear the road ;
Man or matron, boy or lass,
Strong or feeble, old or young,
Must take heed to let him pass
Whilst the loyal song is sung—
Clear the way ! the Gov. is coming,
Ye that make his hay !
Drivers, riders, poor outsiders
Turn, and clear the way !
Subjects dutiful of the Queen,
Staunch adherents of the Throne-
Fat or lean, or in between,
Cannot call their souls their own
When his lordship takes the air,
Guarded well before, behind
To approach him none must dare,
Otherwise they will be fined.
Clear the way ! good people tremble !
Hopetoun’s out to-day !
That’s the matter, scoot and scatter
Turn and clear the way !

in an Adelaide daily the proprietress oi a

* * *
A Melbourne G.P.O. lady employe got
married some time ago, but could not be persuaded
to resign, so she was sent up country far away
from her husband, also an official, until a little
family inconvenience made her glad to come to
terms with the Postmaster-General.

At a meeting of the Servant Girls’ Union
at Hughenden (Q.) it was proposed that the rate
uf wages should be fixed as follows Laundresses,
waitresses, general servants, barmaids, &c., £1
per week; servants in private houses, 15s. per
week. And agreed that the Society be affiliated
with the Women Workers’Association at Charters
Towers. A Miss M'Grath then asked chairman
MDonald : “Can a union woman marry a non-
union man?” (Laughter.) Mr. M'Donald : “No,
certainly not.” Miss M‘Grath then proposed that
all Union girls not married in six months be fined
£lO.
# * #
Frith’s “ Railway Station ” has been sold
at Christie’s for 300 guineas. It originally fetched
£4500, and £750 in addition for the right of
exhibition, and thousands of people flocked to see
it, but £315 is about its true value as an example of
photographic and vulgar art. The central figure, it
may be remembered, is that of , the famous
English forger, who until lately walked Sydney
streets. The likeness was good.
» * #
622 women of Teralba, Minmi and Greta
have forwarded a petition to Parliament in favour
of Creer, M.L.A.’s Bill to abolish barmaids. The
basis of the hatred felt by the average woman for
barmaids is plain enough. It is against custom
for a woman to enter bars and take her drinks
like a man, hence a bar is to the fair sex a mys-
terious place. So lovely woman determines to
destroy that in regard to which she cannot gratify
her curiosity.
* # *
The approaching final departure of Lady
Carington has left an aching void in sassiety, and
the fat woman of Sydney lifts up her voice and
refuses to be comforted. There is a hollow yearn-
ing which victuals cannot fill up nor hash
alleviate, and on which ham cut thick exercises no
mitigating influence.
# # *
In Muckiferous Melbourne there died in
August last 35 people over 75 years old, one of 92.
Is cleanliness really healthy 2
* * #
A London tailor, we read, had a customer
who in one year purchased from him 365 pairs of
trousers, and in the course of time “let him in”
for several hundred pairs.
* * *
Some fathers pay their “tarts” alimony.
Every father pays his baker alum-money.
* * *
Nearly 9000 children attended the Sydney
R.C. Cathedral the other morning to take the
temperance-pledge. As the revenues of the R.C.
church in Australia emanate at present in so large
a degree from the drink traffic, this strong young
temperance movement strikes one with great
force as being a social step in the right
direction. It is almost as pleasant and radical a
reform as if one of these days we should see all
the Hebrew children troopintothe Synagogue, and,
in a loud voice, take the pledge against usury.

Memo, for special constables.—The best
way to arouse a drunken man is to pinch him
under the arm.
* * *
Now that there is some money on the
N.S.W. estimates for the defence of accused per-
sons in capital cases, “me brother-in-law, the
barristher-at-lavv ” will get some briefs ? Alas,
no ! The choice of counsel lies with the prisoner,
who is usually a more accomplished connoisseur in
barristers than the authorities appear to be, judg-
ing from the sort of hiccups you hear a lot of the
Crown Prosecutors making.
* * *
The proper method of procedure with
regard to the tuberculosis scare is quite obvious.
Let all cattle be inspected with the same care as
is exercised in regard to meat consumed by the
Jews. The chief Rabbi of each Australian
province would, if consulted on the matter,
doubtless be glad to give his valuable advice in this
matter and to assist in procuring the services of
skilled persons who would prevent lots of meat
now unsuspectingly consumed by the general
community from literally passing into “con-
sumption.”
S. Australia has cultivated 1,842,961 acres
under wheat, 10,679 under barley, and 10,297
under oats.
Baron Carington is getting well through
his course of farewell dinners with the aid of
of Marshall’s Tonic Nervine.
A 23-knot big paddle steamer, the Hygeia,
is being fetched from England to knock spots out
of the Ozone, in Melbourne Bay traffic.
A Sydney paper announces the invention
of “a machine for cleansing rum.” It occurs to
us that the best way to cleanse rum would beto
wash it with soap and then hang it out on a line
to dry. Anyone who attempts to purify a
collection of soiled lush by boiling it with soda
will find himself left.
The P. and O. Co. charters steamer
Afghan, for coal, from Japan to Australia.
Japan’s export of coal already amounts to about
£1,000,000 a year, but China is the greatest coal-
region of the world, with 200,000 square miles,
and next comes the United States, which has
190,000 square miles against England’s 9000.
W. S. Gilbert has bought a dog that is
supposed to be the biggest in the world—several
sizes larger than the monster, now dead, owned
by Du Maurier, and so monotonously introduced
into his Punch pictures. By the way, there is a
canine Cranbrook to be seen in Melbourne, follow-
ing at the heels of Yon Kaufmann, the water-bore
man.
An ejectment case at Bathurst last week
brought a number of very old residents into the
witness-box, some of them dating long before the
time when D’Arcy Wentworth was police magi-
strate. One octogenarian proudly boasted that he
was an Englishman and came out here in ’36 as a
convict (not many on ’em left now), while the
father of the plaintiff was a soldier on board.
Another witness was Mr. J. N. Macintosh, soli-
citor, who was admitted to practice in October,
1840, and so celebrates his professional jubilee this
month. He served his articles with “ Old ”
Norton.
When the first triennial Parliament met
in Melbourne in the year 1859 the honoured name
of L. L. Smith appeared conspicuously on the
list as member for South BoUrke. That was
thirty-one long years ago, and the apothecary
with the frilled shirt is still there, and seemingly
as fresh as ever. But where, O where, is the
young man who didn’t feel well in that forgotten
era ? If he is dead, what did he die of ? and if
he is alive does he feel well to-day, or does he still
experience the physical troubles which induced
him to send along his guinea, together with a
stamped and addressed envelope, to the late resi-
dence of his Excellency the Governor, in the
giddy times when the country was still young
and addicted to wickedness, in consequence of
which it suffered from “symptoms akin to the
above?”
Editor Bulletin.— -Sir, —ln a recent issue
of The Bulletin I notice a paragraph stating
that a Victorian named Keeble, who has just
settled on the Manning River, N.S.W., istheman
who made the golden horseshoes and nailed them
on Mr. Cameron’s horse in Beechworth in 1855.
As a resident of the Beechworth district from
1853, who has read and heard many fables about
the golden shoes, I will, with your permission,
give a correct account of the affair, which can be
corroborated by many of the old miners of the
once famous Woolshed Creek : —ln October, 1853,
while I was boarding at Mrs. Clarke’s Franklin
Hotel, on the Upper Woolshed, seven miles
from Beechworth, a man named Brown
(long i-ince dead, but at that time or soon after-
wards owner of the Rook Station, near Wagga
Wagga) came there with his circus, and
as there were about 17,000 men on the creek,
earning, say, £7 a week a-piece, he did a big busi-
ness. To make his circus a greater attraction, he
bought some gold and got Tommy Toofield, a
goldsmith, working as a smelter at the time, to
make four horseshoes (or rather, slippers) weigh-
ing about soz. each. Brown had a black mare,
and during the evening’s performance the shoes
were tacked on, she was led around the ring, her
feet lifted up, and the shoes shown to the audience.
The mare was then led away and the shoes taken
off. I have seen it stated that Cameron rode from
the Woolshed to Beechworth on his horse shod
with gold; but anyone who knows that rugged,
granite, mountainous road could easily imagine
how much would be left of the shoes by the time
he had ridden half-way. I worked in Keeble’s
claim for some time. His partners were—Wren,
Oheeseman, and Frank Nichols (the latter is at
Parkes now), I should like, for the sake of the
working man, to see such times again. Money
was plentiful; for instance, the qualification for a
member of Parliament then was £2OOO or a certain
income. When Cameron was proposed as a mem-
ber, although his claim was yielding him £l5O a
week, a few of the bosses met at Mrs. Clarke’s
and put down £l7OO. In the afternoon they went
down the creek and collected the remaining £3OO.
—Yours. S. T. Bishop, Gulgong.
Two thousand three hundred and twenty-
two small Victorian boys were camped out at
Langwarrin last week, and put through a course
of military tomfoolery suited to their tender
years. Only the bespangled swaggerers in charge
of these baby battalions, and the weak-minded
parents who contribute offspring to the Cadet
cause, can contemplate their annual gathering
without a groan. At a hasty glance the toy
camp, strewn with orange-peel, lemonade-bottles
and sandwich-bags, seems a joyous joke, yet very
little reflection is necessary to convince the
thoughtful observer that moral poison lurks in
each pound of cake served out to ‘ ‘ our young
defenders.” Of all the juvenile instincts, the
military instinct is one that should be repressed
rather than nourished on buns and sticky liquors.
The bump of destruction is lamentably prominent
on the heads of most boys, and their natural
tendency to pelt stones at the passer-by,
especially when he happens to be a China-
man, is deplored by society at large and
the victims in particular. Almost the first
duty of parents and guardians is to convince the
rising generation that there are grander objects in
life than the indulgence of an inherent craving
for destruction and the exercise of armed force.
Tommy is forbidden to arm himself with a stick
because a boy so armed is unable to resist the
desire of assaulting the nearest defenceless civilian
boy upon his shin. If Tommy be not checked in
the days of childhood he becomes, just according
to circumstances, a dangerous larrikin or a bell-
toppered representative bully, who believes in
putting things down with a firm hand, sir. The
immediate object of giving boys a cadet training
is to stimulate their natural passion for assault and
battery, whilst subjecting it to a system of
physical discipline, -which is accepted as highly
beneficial by people who associate a course
of drilling—“military' drill” being the
popular term with uniforms, sham fights
and professional swagger, just as though
boys couldn’t be taught to walk pro-
perly, or turn right wheel with neatness or
despatch, unless they are first figged out in
miniature trappings of war. The wearing of a
uniform, the swilling of lemonade “ under
canvas,” and the turning out to muster at bugle
call are by no means necessary to a boy’s educa-
tion in obedience or manly deportment. The
worst use to which we can put a boy is to make a
sucking soldier of him —to hand him over to the
temporary care of a full-sized fool of a colonel,
who thinks that teaching the young idea to shoot
involves the assistance of fire-arms. The mis-
guided kid naturally is brought to regard mili-
tarism as something to be admired for itself
alone. In after life his heart warms at the sound
of a kettle-drum, and he can’t hear two bars of
the “ Minstrel Boy” without wanting to cry
“ Havoc !” and let loose the dogs of war on some
person or persons unknown. Surely tlie chubby-
cheeked Australian cadet is a sad thing in the
the sight of God at any time, and twas no
wonder that the spectacle of some twenty-three
hundred being drawn up in battle array at
Langwarrin caused the Heavens to weep copiously
upon their silly little heads.

are overheated by alcohol, the lips get so parched
that the tongue has to do frequent duty.

his high office at an advanced age. He was a
grubby old anointed, whose open countenance was
much disfigured by snuff; also, he was toothless
and grumpy and fond of onions, and had one eye
that was dull and filmy, and one eye that was
drunken and red. Owing to his unwholesome
habits the throne has been scrubbed down and
disinfected for the use of his daughter and sue
oessor.

When Sir Charles Dilke, in his “ Problems
of Greater Britain,” referred to the late Professor
Badham, of Sydney University, as a classicist
“with a local reputation,” lie was simply giving
the “authorities” who had coached him in “local
colour ” very neatly away. Badham, as a Grecian
and text-editor, had a European reputation ; and
if all the professorial reputations imported into
Australia before or since the Doctor’s time—and
we don’t forget Woolley, Wilson, or Hearn-were
placed one on top of another, they wouldn’t reach
Badham's shoulders.

The Yankees use so much gold for tooth-
filling, &c., that;, says a medical statistician, in
three centuries the cemeteries of their country
will contain 35 million pounds’ worth of gold.
Our Yankee friend errs. As the people are
buried the undertakers’ assistants steal the golden
mouth-plates, &c., and sell them to the jewellers,
who scrape off the gold and make wedding-rings
of it. If they don't, then the Yankee uadertakers
simply don’t know their business.

On another page is an illustration of the
new Wentworth Hotel, Church Hill, a build
ing conspicuous for its architectural grandeur,
commanding position and accessibility to all
the central portions of the city. From basement
to roof (from winch there is a magn iicent view of
our beautiful harbour) this establishment is
furnished with everything that mod rn art and
science can suggest. The commercial rooms
are connected with the telephone exchange,
and, like the public and private drawing rooms,
dining, sitting and bed rooms, are richly furnished.
All the rooms are lofty and well ventrated, and
are fitted with electric-bells. Hot and cold water
baths are to be found in various positions. Alto-
gether there is a general feeling of comfort com-
bined with elegance and good taste that render
this hotel attractive to visitors and country friends
who wish to enjoy a good table, light cheerful
rooms at, perhaps, the most central and quiet
house in the city. Mrs. Hayes (the proprietress),
keeps only the best‘brands of w ; 'es, spirits. &c.,
and the terms are very moderate.*
