Where do they goto? What is the secret of
their astounding success ? Questions easily
answered. They go into every civilised land on
the earth’s surface, and they are to be found in
the pockets of every people who wear clothes.
The more there are made, the more there are
wanted, and the only real difficulty in connection
with them is to keep up the supply. They have
an infinite variety of recommendations, but the
main one is th ir wonderfully accurate perform-
ances as timekeepers. The invention of the
Waterbury doomed expensive watches to gradual
extinction, for the Waterbury is exact and trust-
worthy.
Among late Purchasers of the
WATERBURY

(now Emperor)

bury.”

bury. ”

Keferee
Cup Cay at Caulfield was a great financial
success for "the V. A.T.C. and as ruinous as usual for the
miutirs, who seem never to weary of watering this book
makers’ paradise with their tears. The big meeting
whereat many of the races bring out good horses with a
fair percentage of “triers” amongst them—is generally
more disastrous to backers than the off days at Caulfield,
when about five of every six steeds are so defunct that
they can be smelt from afar by all save the innocents who
have no noses for such scandals. Still the attendance of
victims increases steadily, it not rapidly, every year, and
this time the number of sanguine individuals who cherish
hopes of working off the dead horse at Flemington is
absolutely appalling. The improbability of anyone
spotting a Caulfield Cup winner on its previous “form”
lias been more than once referred to in Tiik, Bulletin.
The length of the race and the nature of the course are
such that a dozen animals which have a poor chance of
being “in it” for the Melbourne Cup are not “out
of it” here. And although the race is a favourite
with the clever people, and “patient divisions,”
and notwithstanding that a “ bottled-up ” one generally
wins,” there is no getting away from the fact that Caul-
held heroes seldom do anything heroic after their one day
out Vengeance was a “good thing” (for the Ballarat
people who backed it) on Saturday, hut not sufficiently
[mod to carry a stone penalty home at Flemington, we
fancy. Nor is the half-head-beaten Loyalstone, for both
horses appeared to be tiring and coming hack to the
others as they struggled past the post. Next week, how-
ever, will be time enough to try for the winner of the
Melbourne Cup.
# * #
The o’d yarn about the “Ring” being hit
heavily will doubtless be told of this year’s Caulfield Cup.
It can’t be warranted to wash, all the same. When
Vengeance’s number went up there were, perhaps, a
couple of hundred people who cheered, and about ten
thousand who remarked “ dam,” in a dull, dissatisfied
kind of way. Many others had hoped to see Loyalstone’s
(idiotic name!) number hoisted, and they laid a heavy
accent on the D accordingly.
#* • *
Chintz was not let loose for last Saturday’s race,
after all, but will—all going well—prove an eleventh bom-
sensation in the Melbourne Cup betting. In her stead
the mysterious Jas. Wilson people ran The Serf, who never
gave them a ghost of a show to win their money, and
whose defeat was a negative source of satisfaction to the
racing world at large. The stable “accepted ” with three
horses, Swing being scratched on the morning of the race,
and Chintz’s number remaining amongst those of the in-
tended starters until a short time before the saddling-
hell rang. Presuming that the mare never left her train-
ing quarters at Queenscliff, it should be within the power
of "the stewards to demand an explanation of this delay in
scratching. Not that such little matters are very strictly
looked after at Caulfield. Only 20 minutes previous to
starting time for the last race several scratehings » c,, e
registered on the paddock black-board.
# * #
The placed horses in the Caulfield Cup have
ever shown themselves to have a good say for the Mel-
bourne Cup, taking, for instance, Sheet Anchor, Dunlop,
Silverraine, Grace Darling and Bravo.
* * *
It is said that Tom Haydon executed a £20,000
commission for the Vengeance party.
# # *
The result of the recent drawn G oddard-Jackson
light at Melbourne, in which Goddard gave “ as ; o d as
he got” and a good deal better, in an eight-round
fight, must decidedly send the former’s stock up, as it
will undoubtedly send Jackson’s stock down. If the so-
called Broken Hill smasher (whose connection with the
Barrier is slight) can, in an eight - round match,
make a draw with one of the cleverest big men who ever-
entered the ring, he must, with his undoubted pluck and
hitting power, be equal to anything on the list in a fight
to a finish. Should things go on like this, Slavin may
soon have something else to do besides abusing Sullivan
The Bulletin has always believed in Slavin’s ability to
thump Jackson in a square fight, and now it is beginning
to believe that there are two men, at least, with whom
Jackson could not hold his own in a raw-meat and-grass
contest under London prize-ring rules—a contest under
which is, after all, the only true criterion of a man’s
ability as a real fighter. “Goddard had all the best of
it,” said one wire from a well-known sporting man—“ and
would certainly have got stakes by huge majority had
verdict been left to crowd.” It must not he forgotten that
the darkey was too cocksure to train properly, and that
the small ring was a point in favour of Goddard, the
rusher, against Jackson, the scientific fighter who likes to
fight at arm’s length.
#. # #
Peter Jackson, before going over to meet
Goddard, was made a Mason at Balmain The goat
must have disagreed with him.
# # #
The boxing-benefit for the strike-fund, at Sydney
Gaiety Theatre, on Saturday night last, is said to have
realised £5O. Many good boxers, including Griffo,
Dooley, Maher and Ryan, showed, and Larry Foley, who
officiated, received a tremendous ovation when Ire replied
to the thanks publicly tendered him by the representa-
tives of the strike-fund committee, and offered the use
of his theatre on some subsequent occasion. Larry, how-
ever, was dumb concerning the invitation to come out for
West Sydney tendered him. Should In- ever get in Parlia-
ment, it’s long odds he’d never be asked to come outside.
# , # #
Hackford, the only man to finish, won the
Launceston seventy-two hours’ go-as-you-please with
a seore of 373 miles 14 laps—eight miles further than
Raynor's, the previous best Australian record. Raynor
has challenged him to walk six days and nights for £lOO.
# # #
Paddy Mor.au, said to be (Foley says no) the
“ unknown ” matched against Griffo, met Jack Hall, about
the smartest lightweight Sydney has seen, in the cellar of
Foley’s White Horse Hotel'. Moran stood before Hall for
fourteen rounds, when Hall knocked him down with the
right, and the noise when Moran’s head met the concrete
floor might have been heard in Pitt-street. Moran
graduated in the same school as Billy M'Carthy, Steve
O'Donnell, and other good men.
* * *
Jack Conway is back in Melbourne, where in
one year he was captain of the boss cricket eleven, South
Melbourne, and captain of the boss football team, Carlton.
Then he got cheered as a cricketer on the ground where
he got hooted as a footballer, and vice versa.
# * *
Sydney sports say that Megaphone's defeat is
due to the fact that he has not had half enough work
since going into Higinhotham’s hands. He is a glutton
for work. Others say he can’t run equally well on right-
handed and left-handed courses.

Py ttie scratching of Shotover for the Caul-
field Cup one South Australian in Melbourne went down
for a big sum. The denizens of the Australian Nazareth
were nearly all ou the up-country nag.

A London paper thus sums up Joe M'Auliffe :
. M'Aiiljffc appears to ho an over-grown ruffian who is 100
11 ( e to work, and therefore lives on gate-money. Persons
wishing to share the spoil with him induced him to stand
up before Slavin, who is admitted to be an athlete with
wonderful powti and skill in the use of his hands.
Nothing "cod could come of asking Slavin to chase this
fat man round a ring. The poor fellow may have been
anxious only to earn a living, but those who put him up
must have known of the sham.” Which is yet another
proof that the fallen pugilist has no friends.
* # # a
R. Davis became the champion bicyclist of Aus-
tralia by securing the 10-mile race in Adelaide the other
day. Davis is a thin, weak-framed-looking athlete, and is
about the last person one would choose for a champion-
ship of any kind. He is a clerk in S. A. Railway Depart-
ment, and has secured the’ “Austral” championships
three times.
* * *
One applicant for the post of Inspector of Stiff-
Tins to the V.R.C. gave as a qualification that he knew
nothing about racing, and, consequently, was sure to ho
impartial.
# # #
Breeders anxious of securing unique stock
should keep their eyes on the sale to be held at Olenlofty,
near Elmhurst, Vic., on Wednesday, 12th November,
when, according to a catalogue issued by Mr. U. Hadland
on behalf of the owmers, the Messrs. M’Phee, there will
be auctioned, among other valuable animals, “two two-
year-old geldings, in foal to Claptrap.”
* * *
John L. Sullivan is said to be a stupendous
draw as a star actor, and an enterprising manager now'
wants Frank Slavin to appear in “ After Dark.” After a
darkey is probably what is meant.
# * *
A British fashion paper recommends its readers
to learn the art of self-defence, on the ground that
“ boxing refines and polishes a women.” Which means,
w'e presume, that noble thoughts and lofty aspirations are
aroused in her gentle soul when she has been plugged in
the wind, and bashed on the listener, and landed heavily
on the conk, and also that it expands her mental horizon
to be severely fibbed and heavily grassed, and then to get
up and have her eye split, and to get a tremendous lift on
the jaw. All these things help to elevate the feminine
intellect, particularly when they are followed up by a
smash on the kisser.
# # #
Lord Hopetoun, the flying two-year-old, was
not entered for the Maribyrnong Plate.
* * #
The two races that Lady Betty woip at Thirsk
would be very dead birds for the mare, we imagine.
Thirsk is an out-of-the-way spot where the Duke of Hamil-
ton, or ironmaster Houldsworth, used generally to send
one decent horse to scoop whatever pools were worth
taking
# * #
Phillips, the Melbourne bowler, who has just
returned from his annual engagement at “ Home,” gives
a fair account of the Australian Eleven. He says that
none of them looked too fondly on the beer when it w'as
brown, and that their apparent failure was attributable
to the strength of the opposing teams. Apropos to which
it is noticeable that London papers excuse the indifferent
“county” record of t v, c Nottingham eleven by saying
that the best men were overworked in outside matches
against the Australians, played for the M.C.C. and other
people.
# # *
Titan, the £4OOO gelding, whose fresh and artless
young owner, having dropped over h'm nearer £30,000
than £20,000, now calls him “ a cur at heart,” recalls the
Marquis of Hastings’ Kangaroo, for which he gave
£12,000, and which, they say, descended to pulling a cab.
Reversal of the case of the famous Godolphin Arab, which
started business in a Paris water-cart.
# # *
Frank Allen now bosses the Warrnambool
Cricket Club, having returned to the district where he
firs! bowled a potato against a cabbage-stump. Grace
always sticks up for Allan, as the bowler of a century,
though his luck was out in mizzly England.
# * *
The departed Curtis Reid, a veteran and en-
thusiastic cricketer, reported a match for one of the
Melbourne papers, and his running fire of exclamations
kept all within car-shot delighted. Yet his report was as
dry as a bone. The reporter sitting next him did net
know a thing about technical cricket, but coolly embodied
in his report all Curtis’ remarks. The result was me t
graphic and original, fetching compliments on all hand s .
# * #
Jim Hall, Sydney’s dandy middle-weight,
would have knocked “Dummy” Mace cut inside the
stimulated six rounds in Melbourne, had not Mace so re-
peatedly dropped to avoid Hall's right-hand rib-benders
and left-hand face stabs that the referee was compelled to
give a decision against this usually game boxer.
# # #
Walter Higinbotham does not appear to know
the constitution of either Titan or Megaphone. Titan
may have left his form behind him at two years, but
Megaphone could scarcely have fallen away in speed to
such an extent since his Randwick running. Anyway,
the stable had no idea he had gone off, as “Plunger”
Brodribh laid £l4OO to £5OO on the colt for the opening
race at Caulfield.
# * #
Captain Merry “dished up” a sensational three-
column yarn for the ’Frisco Breeder and Sportsman* out
of Sir Modred’s historical scratching, whereby Bank-
Manager Holt came to grief. He tells of Holt’s unholy
Friday’s breakfast, and finishes up by saying that Holt is
now confidential adviser on colonial affairs to the head
office of—well, a certain big bank.
# # ■#
Last Saturday a punter in a small way put his
little half-sov. on Singapore in a “ tote” for the Caulfield
Cup, and then wandered round Sydney nervously await-
ing news of the result. To pass away the time he had a
game of billiards with a friend, and then the pair went to
a cookshop for a snack. They were seated near the
speaking-tube connecting with the kitchen when a waiter
nearly broke the ear of the punter with the shout of
“ Sausages, one!” “Just my luck,” said the disgusted
punter, “ I didn’t even know there was such a horse in
the race.”
# # *
Cricket is fast becoming a thing of the past in
Sydney, and the cause does not take much looking for.
It lies in the so-called juniors being represented by bald-
headed, well-on-to-forty men, who have proved failures
in senior cricket and have drifted back to the ranks of
the juniors. Take, for instance, some of the men picked
to play in the coming Junior Intercolonial match against
Victoria, say, Camphin, Ellis, Kayser, Luland and others.
These men have tried senior cricket, failed, and now they
block the way of promising bona-fide juniors, who might,
if they got the show, develope into something good
enough to recruit the ranks of our seniors. It would
really be a benefit to the cricketing public —mind you,
we don’t say the general—if someone would go roui d
with an axe and knock these everlasting juveniles on the
head.
}■ * * *
It appears to be certain death for an athlete to
contract typhoid fever, and the stronger and more power-
fully-built the man, the quicker the end comes. Poor T.
K. Bond,' the pedestrian, who died at Little Bay Hospital,
is the last athletic victim. 11. M. Johnson, the most
wonderful sprinter America ever produced, recently died
from typhodial peritonitis. Johnson, once editor of the
Sr. Lons slnws, and winner of a Sheffield handicap, is said
to have run 100 yards in 9j secs., but his record is given
as 0 4-sth secs.
* * #
In the match at Scarborough between Lord
Londcsborough’s Eleven and the Australians (which Aus-
tralia won by eight runs), Briggs, the Lancashire profes-
sional, achieved a remarkable performance in the first
innings, actually securing every one of the Australian
wickets, nine from his bowling, and the tenth thrown out.
# # *
Rowing on board the Sobraon, lying in Ply-
mouth Sound, after dusk in a small boat, Sammy Join s
got lost and is said to have contracted a severe cold
through being exposed all night. Thus is added another
to the fine assortment of first-class colds and thing
caught by the unhappy Sammy.
* # #
“Old times Rocks !” “Ted ” Evans now does his
daily practice on Sydney Domain with the Warwicks. The
writer saw him handling the ball the other afternoon and
breaking to either side at will. The “ Fakir” picked up
his wonderful command of the ball at his father’s back-
blocks station, where ho used to practise with half-a-dozen
balls at a single stump, a trained sheep-dog doing the
fielding.
* # *
Some of the Melbourne papers “selected” as
many as eleven horses, by various prophets, to win the
Caulfield Cup, but not one of them gave the winner a
place,
• * *
\ leading Australian journalistic sporting
authority, it is said, refused the other day to take the
hand of a racehorse-owner who had scored a big success,
on the ground of his alleged in-and-out running.

THE BULLETIN (Published at Sydney, N.S.W)
The Unique Weekly, Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
The Bulletin, the Premier Australian Literary
Journal, is the only paper which has a con-
sistent and distinctive Australian National
Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
Payment of Members.
One Man, one Vote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System,
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces —
Religious Interference in Politics.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the other half that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started seven years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become a vast property, because it pos-
sessed a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have, during the
last year, spent some thousands of pounds in
fitting their buildings with the latest appliances
for high-class printing and the production of illus-
trations, and they have now issued The Bulletin
in an entirely new form—printed with new type
of improved legibility on the finest paper, and
adorned with sketches from the 'hands of the
ablest and best-paid newspaper artists Australia
has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The voice of The Bulletin is the beacon-fire
of national progress: it is the forceful summons to
the crusade against the monopolies in land,
wealth, power, and privilege—the direct cause of
half the misery which now afflicts British
humanity. Against the claims of avaricious
clergy who claim to monopolise salvation, against
the dark despotism of grasping plutocrats, and
against the exercise of any human authority
which has not been granted by the people over
whom it is exercised, The Bulletin will fearlessly
and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary w r eekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pcarl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in tho New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the
most popular city publication and the organ of tho
ntelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
The circulation of The Bulletin in Sydney is
unrivalled by that of any other weekly journal,
and more than a hundred copies of it are sold in
Melbourne to one of any other paper printed out-
side that city. Perhaps the strongest proof of The
Bulletin’s popularity is found in the unchallenge-
able fact —easily ascertainable from any postal
official —that, of all journals issued in Australasia,
The Bulletin is the one most frequently re-
mailed from one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

We notice with pleasure that the Equitable
Mortgage, Discount, and Deposit Bank, of 4
Moore-street, Sydney, has recently been incor-
porated, and is now opened for business. This
bank has been formed for the purpose of assisting
those who may at any time be in pecuniary diffi-
culties and wish to avoid paying the excessive in-
terest which is too often charged. The bank has
secured the able services of Mr. Henry A. Birk-
myre, which should be a guarantee that clients will
be fairly dealt with. In these days of extortion, the
above institution should about fill a long-felt
want. *

“The Conventional Lies
of our Civilisation.”
THE ECONOMIC LIE.— CHAPTER IV.
BY MAX NORDAU.
r X I HK sentiment in regard to the unendurable con-
ditions of affairs in the economic world is
universal. The wretched operative whoso daily
hunger keeps the subject always in his mind
knows that he produces wealth by the labour of
jus hands, when he is demanding his share of the
riches he thus creates. But he commits the
mistake of founding his demand upon all sorts of
reasons that do not stand the test of criticism.
There is only one single true and natural argument
which he can call to his aid, and that is un-
answerable : the argument that he has the power
to take possession of the goods which he produces,
that the rich are in the minority and unable to
prevent this appropriation, consequently, that he
has the right to keep what he makes, and to help
himself to what he needs. The whole of the
present structure of society is built upon this
argument as its sole foundation. This argument
makes the weaker individuals and peoples slaves
of the stronger; it makes millionaires out of
shrewd and unscrupulous men, and sets up Capital
as the absolute master of the whole world. The
minority —the loafers and plunderers—make con-
stant use of this argument to silence the demands
of the labouring and plundered classes. But
the wages-receiver, whose mind, in spite of
all its Radicalism, is still entangled in the
meshes of the ideas of right and mora-
lity, inculcated by Capital, hesitates to em-
ploy this unanswerable argument, based upon
the laws and instincts of nature. He prefers
to seek the justifiableness of his claims in
all kinds of out of the way excuses and ideas,
among which Communism is the most widely
accepted and believed. Thus, in the most foolish
manner, he enters upon territory in which he is
sure to be defeated.
* * *
Capital has no difficulty at all in proving the
absurdity of this theory. In fact, Communism,
as all socialistic schools comprehend and preach
it, is the outgrowth of a preposterous chimera,
evolved from the inflamed imagination of certain
dreamers, deaf and blind to the realities of the
world and human nature. Actual community in
property, or the negation of individual rights in
property, has never existed since the world began.
That condition of property-holding which a super-
ficial observer might consider to be Communism,
and of which several examples have occurred in
historic times, some even existing in a few isolated
places at the present day, is founded upon the
basis of individual ownership of property, separate
from the mass of property existing in the world at
large. When such a perfect cohesion and sense of
fellowship exists among a small number of indi-
viduals owing to their common descent or other
causes, that a family, a village, or a whole tribe
considers itself as one single being of a higher
order of creation, then it is conceivable that this
collective individual should possess an indivisible
collective amount of property, which the single
individual could not control and use for his own
advantage and to the prejudice of others. This
kind of collective property holding, which exists
in several places in Europe at the present time,
such as the Russian Mir and the united households
of the Croatians and the Slavonians, has nothing
in common with Communism, that is, fundamen-
tal and universal community in property through-
out the world, as can easily be demonstrated. J ust
let an outsider, an individual not accepted as a
member of the circle of joint owners of the com-
mon property, let him attempt to get possession
of the smallest fragment of it! The entire tribe,
village, Mir, &c., will rise up in arms at once to
repel the intruder. The joint possessors of the
common capital are imbued so strongly with the
sense of proprietorship in it, that they rebel
against any appropriation of any part of it by out-
siders, with as much liveliness of indignation as
an individual proprietor would experience if an
attack were made upon his purse. And even this
collective proprietorship, which is by no means
actual Communism, but only a more primitive
form of personal ownership of property, can only
exist so long as every member of the community
experiences directly and profoundly his cohesion
and fellowship to and with the rest. Its perpetua-
tion depends also upon the similarity of the
labour performed by the members, so that the
efforts made by each can be compared easily and
directly with those of the rest, and no doubts
arise as to their relative value or importance. As
soon as a division of labour takes place and differ-
ent kinds of production are carried on in the
collective community, the necessity will arise
to compare, the relative values of certain
kinds of labour, each useful in its way, but
differing completely in every other respect, it will
be impossible to estimate justly and satisfactorily
to each member the utility and pecuniary value of
his labour, as it differs in kind from that of those
around him, consequently the collective proprietor-
ship in the results of the efforts of all must neces-
sarily come to an end, and the ownership of pro-
perty individualise itself in a very short time.
Thus we see that the solution of the economic pro-
blem is not to be looked for in Communism. It
is a natural condition possible only in very low
forms of collective associations and could not exist
in a form of animal life so highly developed as in
our human society. Individual possession is the
natural condition not only of men, but of most
animals. The source of the impulse for individual
proprietorship is the necessity for the gratification
pf individual wants. Every animal must supply
itself with nourishment and many require also an
artificially-prepared shelter or natural hiding-place.
Ihe food and the nest or den which it has found or
made for itself, is considered by the animal as its
property. It feels that these things belong to it,
and to no other being, and will not submit with-
out resistance to being deprived of them by any
other individual. A life that makes foresight and
provision for the future a necessity leads to the
extension of this sentiment of proprietorship and
to the development of the impulse for acquiring
increased individual possessions. A beast of
prey, which lives upon fresh meat, fixes the limits
°f bis proprietorship in the total amount of fresh
meat existing, at the quantity which he requires
•or one single meal. But an animal which lives
upon a vegetable diet, if his home is in a region
where there is a winter with a cessation of vegeta-
tion, helps himself from the common store-house
of nature to far more than is necessary to supply
his immediate wants. He accumulates more food
than he can possibly require during the coming
months, thus decreasing without any organic
necessity the amount of food at the disposal of
other animals, he becomes a capitalist and an un-
scrupulous egotist. In this way squirrels, field-
mice, marmots, &c., heap up quantities of nuts
and fruits of all kinds in their holes to provide
for the coming winter, which is not all consumed
at the return of spring, when they can find food
again in the fields and forests. They not only
realise the possibility of their personal proprietor-
ship but they accumulate wealth, they become
rich, in the sense of owning more than they re-
quire for their actual wants. Man belongs to the
category of animals to whom provision for the
future is necessary. The acquisition of individual
property, to increase it beyond what is actually
required for the moment, to defend it against the
encroachments of others, these are natural vital
actions and instincts in him to which he is im-
pelled by the fundamental impulse of self-preser-
vation, and which are impossible to eradicate.
Even under the most violent compulsion of laws
framed in opposition to them, they would assert
themselves again and again with their elementary
strength.
* * *
But if individual proprietorship be a natural
instinct, and hence utterly refuses to be sup-
pressed, there is one application of the right of
personal possession against which reason abso-
lutely revolts, and for whose existence no natural
causes can be produced—this is inheritance.
It is true that the impulse for the pre-
servation of the species impels all living
beings to care for their offspring and to
provide the most favourable conditions of exis-
tence possible for them. But this care never
extends beyond the moment when the young crea-
tures are sufficiently developed to care for them-
selves without outside assistance, as the parents
did before them. There is only sufficient stored-
up food in the seed of the plant or in the white of
the egg, to supply the embryo with nourishment
during its earliest stage of life—the time of abso-
lute helplessness. The mammiferous animals
give milk to their young only as long as they are
unable to graze or hunt food for themselves, and
the parent birds cease to bring worms to their
little ones as soon as they have successfully ac-
complished their first independent flight.
# # #
Man alone wishes to provide his descendants
with their stored-up food, their albumen, their
milk and their worms, to the third and fourth, to
untold generations. Man alone is anxious to keep
his children and great grand-children, into the
most distant future, in the embryonic condition
in which the young of all animals are provided for
by the beings to whom they owe their existence;
he will not abandon them to their own re-
sources. Wfcen a man accumulates a fortune, he
wishes to bequeath it to his family in such a way
that its members will be, if possible, relieved for
ever from the necessity of earning their own
livelihood. This is contrary to all of nature’s
laws. It is a violent disturbance of the regular
arrangement of the world, according to which
every living being is compelled to win for himself
his place at the great table of nature, or else
perish. This disturbance of nature’s regulations
is the cause of all the evils of the economic world.
And while it condemns enormous masses of in-
dividuals to wretchedness and want, it at the
same time, takes its revenge upon its originators.
It is in vain that the rich withdraw from the
commonwealth their accumulated possessions
with unconsciously criminal egotism, in order to
ensure a life of luxury and leisure to their chil-
dren and their children’s children forever, they
never accomplish their design. Experience
teaches us that no wealth lasts through
several generations without some business efforts.
Inherited fortunes never remain long in a
family, and even Rothchild’s millions may not
protect his descendants of the sixth or eighth
generation from poverty, unless they possess those
qualities which would have enabled them to win
a high place for themselves in the world without
any inherited millions. These facts show the
operation of an implacable law, which is con-
stantly striving to bring about an equilibrium in
the economic life of society, so grievously dis-
turbed by the unnatural conditions of inherited
property. An individual who has never been
confronted with the necessity of calling his most
primitive organic instinct—the acquiring of food—
into play, soon loses the ability to retain his pos-
sessions and to defend them against the
greed of those without possessions, who en-
croach upon him on every side. Only when
all the descendants of a family are absolutely
mediocre natures, and live far from all public and
private agitation, in complete obscurity, the world
forgetting and by the world forgot, leading a
regular vegetable existence, can they hope to
retain undiminished the possessions that form their
heritage. But as soon as this family produces an
individual gifted with more imagination, who sur-
passes in any direction the standard of mediocrity
prevalent in the family, with passions or ambi-
tion, eager to shine or at least to appreciate
life’s possibilities, the family inheritance is
decreased or ruined, because this off-shoot of the
wealthy family is absolutely incapable of
replacing even one penny of the sums he spends
in the gratification of his whims. It is with
wealth as it is with an organism. The latter must
have vital activity to maintain life ; as soon as the
vital processes cease in its cells it falls a prey to
corruption, and is consumed by the microscopic
beings with whom nature is teeming, seek-
ing whom they may devour. In the same
way we can say that life becomes extinct in a
fortune in which the vital processes of exchange
and circulation are not carried on, so that it is
preyed upon and soon devoured by the greedy
companions of corruption, the parasites, swindlers,
cheats and speculators. The body of a fortune
can be artificially protected against decay and
putrefaction as well ns a human body ; the latter
by antiseptics, the former by a special law—
which ensures the perpetuation of the property
intact, that is, the law of entail. This law of
entail is an invention which affords us an interest-
ing proof of the fact that the rich egotists have
always had a dim suspicion of the unnaturalness
of the right of inheritance. The man of wealth
feels that he is committing a crime against
humanity and that nature will take her revenge
upon his descendants for his contempt of her
laws, consequently he erects a last barrier against
her assault. He foresees that his children will not
have arms strong enough to hold fast to their
heritage, so he ties it to their bodies with ropes
and c rds that no one can unfasten. But even the
law of entail, this carbolic acid bath for dead for-
tunes, loses its efficacy after a while and ceases to
protect the inherited wealth against corruption
and decay and the family against economic ship-
wreck.
* * *
The right of inheritance must be abolished.
This is the only natural and hence the only pos-
sible cure for the ulcers in the body of society
caused by the present conditions of political
economy. Such a proposition seems extremely
radical at the first glance, appearing to be practi-
cally the confiscation of all individual property.
But examined closer, we find that it is only the
consistent development of certain phenomena now
existing, which cause no one uneasiness. The
right of primogeniture is maintained in those
countries which cling most tenaciously to the
feudal organisation of society. This right con-
sists m the systematic disinheritance of all the
children, all the descendants, with the exception
of one, the first-born; so that it is identical with
my proposition, with this one exception. Hence
we see that the most conservative peer of England
carries my proposition into action, although it
may seem so revolutionary to some of my readers.
If we see nothing wrong, and certainly nothing
impossible, in the exclusion of all the children and
descendants of an English nobleman, except the
first-bora, from their share in the enjoyment
of the fortune he leaves behind him at his
death, why should we consider it wrong or
impossible to treat all the children of the
man of wealth in the same way? It is
true that the peer who disinherits his younger
children gives them other possessions, educa-
tion and training, which enable them to take
their places in society. But if all accumula-
tions of property passed into the possession of the
community upon the death of the accumulator
the State would be able to give all the youth of
the land an education and training adapted to
their capacity, and all the disinherited would
have at least the same advantages as are enjoyed
to-day by the disinherited younger son of the
peer. But the peer provides for his younger chil-
dren to w T hom he bequeaths none of his wealth, by
employing his family and political connections to
obtain situations for them in the service of the
State, community, or among his friends, which
have more or less the character of a benefice or
perpetual office. What is this more than an
organised solidarity, which offers the individual
even greater securities for a comfortable existence
than an independent fortune ? It is true this soli-
darity is narrow and selfish ; it is confined to one
caste and has for its sole purpose the plundering
of the majority for the benefit of a few parasites.
Let us imagine the limits of this solidarity
widened to include the whole community, and its
purpose, not to support parasites, but to perform
necessary and useful work ; let us imagine a state
which provides instruction and —if the parents
are incapable of bearing the expense*—food, cloth-
ing and shelter for all the children within its
limits until they are old enough to enter upon
their business career, and when this time arrives,
supplies them with tools and materals for inde-
pendent labour. In such a community of fellow-
ship would not each individual be well provided
for, and would the absorption of the father’s
wealth at his decease into the public treasury be
an act of injustice against the children?
(to be continued.)
A True Bill.
Dr. Morgan T. Willis, a recognised American
authority on cerebral disease and insanity, writes:
“ It is well for us to know that the emotions cause
more unhappiness and crime than any other func-
tion of the brain. Human beings are governed by
their emotions, and it is well that they should be,
though it is emotions that wear away the brain.
It is the emotions such as anxiety, fear, sorrow
and love. I consider that eight hours are suffi-
cient for a man to use his brain, because if he
exceeds that time he becomes nervous and fretful,
and an exhausted brain is an irritable brain. You
may not feel the evil effects of the stress of brain
work at the time, but you will sooner or later,
when it will be too late. The men that work at
night with their brains are the ones that expose
themselves to danger and death, which will surely
come unless the great strain on the mind is
lightened.
Any man that neglects the first warning of a
brain or nervous system that is becoming ex-
hausted, overtaxed or about to break down, is not
only a fool but a criminal. These signs are not
many, but they tell the story of coming dangers
only too plainly. Headache, sleeplessness, irrita-
bility of temper, neuralgic pains about the head
and heart, unrefreshful sleep, nervous dyspepsia,
dull eyes, heaviness of the head, and stupid feel-
ing after meals, worry about trifles, unreasonable
anger, tingling and numbness in the limbs, cold
feet and hands, flushed face and burning ears,
palpitation of the heart, and irregular, weak and
unsteady pulse. When you note these symptoms
beware; the brain and nerves are about to break
down, and it may be insanity, perhaps death.”
Eor all such troubles we recommend Clements
Tonic. This is a scientific specific, brain and
nerve food, and positively repairs the ravages of
overwork, time, disease, &c.
If Clements Tonic was not genuine, would the
Kiama Reporter call it “ The remedy of the
day,” or the Tamworth News say it was “A
specific for all diseases of debility,” or the Goul-
burn Post say it was “A Radical Cure,” or the
Newtown Chronicle say it was “ A Remedy of
approved efficacy,” or the Nepean Times call it
“A really first-class Tonic,” or the Macleay
Argus say “It is a reliable article,” or The
Bulletin say “It is a remedy of the highest
value,” or the Grafton Grip say “Praise is
superfluous,”or theBiNGERA Telegraph designate
it “A valuable medicine,” or the Presbyterian
say, “It can be confidently prescribed.” Yet all
these papers have printed these words in con-
nection with Clements Tonic. The press is far
too conservative to endorse an unproved article,
but we can show hundreds of similarly eulogistic
comment from the press similar to the above.
We don’t need to guarantee when we can get dis-
interested part’es to speak of Clements Tonic as
the journalists do.

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, October 22, 1890
Mv Dear Moorabinda,—
The concert organised by Miss Woolley in aid of the
N.S.W. Women’s College and held at the Sabbath-day’s-
Journey-from-tovvn situated University, on 16th instant,
was a mild, unexciting success. Baron and Lady Caring-
ton attended, the lady appearing in pale-pink striped
silk, white lace and diamonds, with a cluster of white
roses in her hair, and a wrap of pleated chiffon ; Miss
Walenn wore yellow silk and brown velvet, crimson
velvet opera-wrap; Mayoress Burdekin, black lace over
maize satin, with fawn gloves and diamond bracelets. The
card was most artistically arranged if the assisting
Cecilia choir was not. They were all in white, certainly
but some had trimmed themselves with a contrast 0i
velvet and flowers, and they wriggled like eels in
evolving the choruses—one massive lady with diamond
earrings carried on when the music grew allegro as if she
was learning to spar. Miss Pedley, in a fine blue gown
figured in white splashes, unwisely exercised her mezzo-
soprano in a contralto song (“ He thinks I do not love
him ”) ; Mrs. Mason, in cream Russian net and satin and
ostrich-feathers, evolved “An Olden Tryst” as if she
were drawing corks with her teeth ; Mrs. Simmons (Miss
Nancy Evans), in black, played piano soli, one of which
sounded as if she was tickling the übiquitous instrument,
and a Mr. Dobbie and the Sydney Liedertafel made very
tuneful stop-gaps. All the fair soloists were presented
with flowers.
A distressful screech has lately gone up over the in-
creasing numbers of those conscienceless people who
attend private balls, “at homes,” and so on, uninvited,
and shake hands confidently with the bewildered host and
hostess. To the man in a dress-suit, or the damsel who
possesses a striking d6collet6, it’s easy to force an entry
to such festivities, as “ tickets, please,” are not demanded
at the front door, and the servant unquestioningly admits
anybody who turns up in a “cloud ” or a Scotch cap.
One way out of the difficulty would be for each hona-fide
guest to bring his or her (the uninvited are generally
“her,” by the way) pasteboard permit along, and anybody
who couldn’t produce it should be hurled into outer
darkness—and yet such a rale wouldn’t work everywhere.
In a house with a large garden, for instance, the irre-
pressible female panting for the social whirl could get
over the rear-fence, deposit her wraps under a laurel, and
turn up smiling in the conservatory.
Lady Hopetoun has vanished in what (from a strictly
plebeian standpoint) looks like a huff with hubby, who
was unconcernedly tottering round Bendigo inspecting
the pound and the watch-house and the pump while his
colleen embarked on the first stage of the homeward
journey from Spencer-street surrounded by a gang of
sycophantic adorers of both sexes who were thirsting to
discover what is the correct vice-regal number of hat-
boxes to pile on top of the porter. In the farce “On an
Island,” “She’’says, “We must be as utter strangers 1”
and “He ” : “ Yes, just as if we were married.” But not
even a C.M.G. expects the aristocracy to wave loving fare-
wells from the pier-head with its bandanna tied to the top
of its umbrella.

goes on the snowy warpath on her own account. From

If a bald-headed Darby is her idea of a matrimonial mark,
why doesn’t she learn to kick with a view to joining a
comic-opera troupe ?
Quite the latest for summer weddings is to have every-
thing white, from the bride’s cheeks to the china. The
male guests don’t wear white, but come as near to
harmony as they, can in vests, ties and gloves, and the
table-decorations must have no ferns or colour of any kind
about them. White weddings are a very cool, sentimental
idea, but very suggestive of a matrimonial frost.
There is a feeling of general joy among the nursing
profession of Victoria just now. They are glad because,
at a meeting of the Charities Commission, it was urged
that the status of the nurse be elevated as much as
possible, and now the Melbourne Doras go about with a
Vere de Vere smile and a far-away gleam of pedigree in
their forget-me-not eyes, and put jelly to the feet and
mustard-plasters inside the patient who doesn’t rank with
the aristocracy. It was also mooted that their wages (the
coarse Commission called it “wages”) be raised, hours
shortened, and the “course” be divided between the
different hospitals, so that Sister Mary will be quite hope-
lessly muddled as to what is the right, ointment to rub on
a wooden leg. But it is the improved status idea that
takes the trick. The chief fascination of the nursing pro-
fession (in itself a hideous occupation for a woman who
can find anything else under Heaven to do) is the off-
chance of becoming Mrs. (“Doctor”) Lancet. Alas, poor
man, ever art thou a victim to thy environment 1
There is only a slight love thread-running through " A
Pair of Spectacles,” at Sydney Criterion, with Lilian
Scccomhe as the bazaar-haunting sweetheart in a black
gown with a blue how at the neck surmounted by a
Hamlet hat with jutting black, hearselike feathers and
bows of ribbon ; but as Mrs Goldfinch, Mrs. Brough
wears three special costumcsfr Dock first is a “ straw-
berrtcs and-cream ” morning gown, the over-dress of
cream silk flowered with strawberry-pink flowers, the
fronts and high collar lined with strawberry silk, undo ,
dress of white mull with folded bodice, the scarf tin.
loosely in front and the edge of the underdress finished
with pink fringe. Thrill two is a sage-green cashmere
with skirt panels, French bodice and sleeve-epaulettes of
sage-green silk brocaded in dark-green velvet leaves. The
last is lavender-grey poplin with sleeves, and deep, van-
dyked band round the feet of deep grey velvet to match.
In the rather forced curtain piece, “On An Island,”
where the dialogue is as scrappy as if the author had
shaken all the sentences together in a hat, Mrs. Brough
wears a simple dress of navy-blue pongee tellingly sue
mounted by scarlet poppies—a few grouped at the waist
with good effect.
I wonder who is responsible for the weird and wonder-
ful curtsy of the well-matured concert singer ? Is it
the teacher’s idea still clung to by the otherwise modern
pupil? Or is it an unfortunate internal upheaval coming
on like a stroke? For the concert curtsy is a strange>
complex manoeuvre beginning with a wriggle, with a
duck in the centre and a final climactic circular scoop to
the horizontal, accompanied by smiles of gratifying width
and sweetness, and an upward glancing of the eye dimly
suggestive of fits. The latest authorities assert that th e
correct platform bearing is a barely perceptible inclina-
tion of the head to the admiring house, and there is at
least one teacher in Sydney whose pupils sometimes
mount the giddy platform, who in very vivid English
altogether forbids them to curtsy. An artiste is only
tolerable corkscrewing around her train when she is being
showered with bouquets ; scudding under bare poles, so
to speak, the wreathed grins, the undulations and the
oglings seem like giving the company over-weight.
In reading the list of successful competitors at Sydney
’Varsity exams, one is reminded of a manifest injustice
done to a great number of capable governesses in the
country who train their pupils up to passing-point, single,
handed, only to be told that “ Pa ” thinks Maria ought to
get a metropolitan brush-up and intends sending her to
a “finishing” city school for a quarter, where she very
often only recapitulates her studies ami then goes
through the mill and passes in the name of the academy
At the recent exams, one girl who rubbed through had
been but eight weeks at the high-class establishment
which got all the credit and the big advertisement. Such
is life.
Somebody wails to your Sappho that Sydney Beauty
doesn’t furbish up her masticators to ivory-point, as she
should do if she wishes to smile a white, glittering smile,
and not a yellow, mossy grin, at her fellow-beings. But
‘he truth is the long, yellow Australian fang won’t take a
Howir
polish, and only gleams like oiled orange-peel even after
weeks of elbow-grease. And when it isn’t yellow, it is of
that transparent pearl-grey which indicates stopping and
filling and hauling out, and, no matter how much charcoal
is used, the dentals always look like boiled-beans while
there are any left. And as there are enough false fangs
in Australia to make a girdle round the world, and as the
moral aspect of false teeth requires that they be taken
out and soaked in water every night, the charge of
unclean ivories can’t have much foundation,
I am told that a Sydney musician, whose amaranthine
locks float on the dusty breeze, washes his hair before he
goes out of a morning and dries it as he gets townwards
on the top of the ’bus. One day somebody dropped a little
soda in the water, and the gifted artiste had to go about
ail day with a head like a sea-egg.
At a Patey concert the other evening somebody threw
Mr. Patey a nosegay, which he didn’t see. When
Madame came on to take an encore, her practised eye
detected the bunch of greens, which she picked up and
kissed in a fetchingly effusive way, and bore off in
triumph. Poor Mr. Patey! Perhaps he saw it and. was
too bashful to know what to do with it. He could hardly
have kissed it rapturously as his wife did, because—well he
is quite bald enough already.
1 have been to interview the Phonograph, now squeak,
ing in the Centenary Hall. Its re-productions of instiu-
mental music are absolutely fascinating, and if the voice-
re-productions are true to tone, I must congratulate Glad-
stone in possessing a voice the J'ac-*imUc of Robert
Brough’s. I was beguiled into warbling into the long tin
funnel, and screeched thereinto with a shrill head-note on
which I rather plume myself. When if was given hack it
sounded like escaping steam, mixed with the yell of a
badly-lost soul, and as everybody said it was just like my
voice, I suppose I should have felt satisfied.
A direful style, sure to be seized by ladies of an artistic
turn who possess gardens, or live next door to them, is to
merely trim your dashing top-dressing with a handful of
vegetables, taken from the face of Nature and held in
perky place by a long, homicidal bonnet-pin. Artisti-
cally-arranged flowers look very graceful when put up,
hut unless you have water on the brain to keep the stalks
in, don’t lock in the glass at your dandy hat after a
quarter of an hour of sun. Summer roses arc constructed
to fade and moult their petals at critical moments, and a
“shapo” garlanded with hyhiscus, which folds into purple
umbrellas when two minutes off the tree, or pansies which
shrivel up like fried mushrooms, or westeria which turns
up its toes at a zephyr, will look after half-an-hour as if
the bearer had emptied the contents of a neglected vase
upon her devoted skull. A woman crowned in natural
blossoms sounds poetic but looks a guy—as does the
sweet innocent who garlanding herself with flowers lie
sent, goes to a ball, and finds after twenty minutes of
hopping that a shower of petals and one long, bare,
reproachful stalk is all she has to go upon.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

People who don’t know whether zephy rs are fashion-
able for children will learn with avidity that Lady
Carington has ordered frocks in pink, blue, and white
zephyr for her little daughters who are also being made
“fishwife” frocks to wear on the home voyage, packing
operations for which will commence at once.
A few enthusiasts rolled tearfully up to Government
House, Sydney, on 18th inst., mistaking it for the date of
the farewell Saturday reception. The sobbing soldier on
guard leant upon his sword and explained matters in a
choked voice, and the deputation put its handkerchief
back into its pocket and stowed the uncontrollable
anguish until 25th inst.
Mr. and Mrs. D. C. Bell, of Double Bay, gave a most
successful musical “At Home ”on 16th inst. Mrs. Bell
wore cream surah relieved with heliotrope ruches, and
sang “Dear Heart” effectively. Mrs. Bell, sen., wore
amber silk draped in black lace and bouquet of butter-
cups ; Miss Nightingale (Melbourne) appeared in a hand-
some black corded silk gown. The supper-table was very
daintily set out.

Mr. O. O. Dangar, who had a pleasant
habit of talking with the N.S.W. Opposition—
to which he nominally belonged—and voting with
the Government, to which he was a good friend,
has been solemnly fired out by his alleged party.
Mr. J. H. Young having reluctantly
resigned--not because ho was insolvent, but
because it seemed probable that he would be fired
out for being insolvent—the N.S.W. Assembly
will, ere this is published, probably have elected a
new Speaker. That new Speaker will presumably
be J. 1*. Abbott, a slippery and ungraspable politi-
cian of whom both parties enthusiastically desire
to get > id for ever and ever, Amen !

In a Sydney court of law recently one
Cork was arraigned on a charge of false pretences.
This really is poetical justice. It’s quite time
the poor label had a rest.

Mr. Dowel, M.L.A., it is said, contem-
plates proceeding against the S. M. Herald for
libel with respect to its action in insisting on the
accuracy of a report published by it to the effect
that Mr. Dowel had been formally expelled from

When Bradlaugh was contesting North
ampton, at a time when the Tories expected his
defeat, his opponent asked for his autograph. He
defiantly and prophetically wrote: “House of
Commons. Admit Mr. to the Strangers’
Gallery.— Charles Bradlaugh. ”
Sydney still rings with stories tolling
against Lord Carington’s vice-regal predecessor,
and it is expected, says the Hawk, that Lord
Augustus Loftus, who used to have a pungent
pen, will, in his reminiscences, retort in a style to
make the New South Welsh squirm.
The late Judge Molesworth took no holi-
days in 30 years. What a model! The rule is for
a judge to be a used-up barrister, whose doctors
imperatively order a trip to Europe when he has
been about 18 months on the bench. Thencefor-
ward it is regarded as a cruel hardship for him to
sit at all.
The premises of Messrs. Wallach in York-
street have been lately adorned by the insertion
of, perhaps, the largest plate-glass window in
Sydney where are exposed for the inspection of
passers by various handsome specimens of the
upholsterer’s art, draperies and curtains of diverse
hue and materials. Inside the building is hand-
somely decorated, the ceiling ornamentations being
of zinc, and the wall decorations tasteful. One
showroom is wholly devoted to the display of
drapery drawn from all parts of the globe ; another
to carpets, rugs, and floor-coverings of similar
genesis ; yet another solely to dining-room furni-
ture, including a suite which gained the gold
medal at the Melbourne International Exhibition.
The next show-room is laid out with bedroom
suites of the most delicate and tasteful, yet dur-
able, character. On the first-floor are show-rooms
devoted to the display of drawing-room and fancy
furniture, including the wickerwork chairs so
suitable to this climate. In the show-room ad-
joining may'be viewed office and library furniture
of every conceivable size and shape, ingeniously
planned to economise space and save trouble ; hall
furniture, &o. On the next floor is shown bedroom
furniture of variform character and less expensive,
but equally excellent, dining-room furniture, &c.
The third-floor contains pianos, American organs,
billiard and library furniture ; while the basement
is given up to kitchen furniture and other house-
hold requirements. This notice would be incom-
plete did we neglect to mention the firm’s
enormous stock of artistic ornaments, chimney-
pieces, vases, fountains, and other articles of
indoor and outdoor decoration and utility.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Pel'cy Warphy, Es<p, J.P., of Colliils-
fefcfwefc, East Melbourne, of Blank House, Elstern-
AVlck, of Wat phi ri Station on the Lower Darlitlg,
and of Warphy ’s Plains Station in the “NeVer-
NcVer ” country, was, prior to the bursting of the
land-bubble in 1889* Otic bf the greatest of Vic-
toria’s great men. He was so very great that he
bOnldaifofid to contemptuously despise the Parlia-
mentary honours which both Upper and Lower
Ittihse constituencies would willingly have thrust
Upon him. Hehad .no ambition to seat himself
amohg Parliamentarians; he preferred to use
them. From the inner of the two small rooms
which composed his offices he pulled political and
financial wires that made other men who loomed
very largely indeed in the public eye step it
through puppet dances Which had all the grace of
■ spontaneous movement, and which, therefore, de-
ceived the owners of the eye into the reversal of
an old adage. They paid the piper, but they
did not Ordefi the tune. It was Mr. Warphy who
attended to that part of the game. He would
order a Railway waits, or a Tramway quadrille,
or a Public Company polka; and his puppets
Would dance gaily ; and the public paid gladly,
and bowed down before Mr. Warphy for giving
them the privilege of playing. Unquestionably a
great man, Mr. Warphy. So far as was popularly
known he had but two weaknesses. ( hie was an
admiration for the Tichborne claimant and a
belief in the authenticity of his claim —Mr.
Warphy was accustomed to say that the man who
formulated the profound maxim, “that them as
has no brains but lots o’ money is made for them
as has brains but no money,” was too wise to bo
associated with a fraud; and the other was an
unreasoning worship of his only (and motherless)
daughter, Alice.
He loved her so passionately that he had refused
for her an alliance with a ducal family because
he could not tolei’ate the idea that she should live
out of the colonies. Suitor after suitor had wooed
her .* some, heirs to leading squatting families;
others, with a gold or a silver mine in their
pockets; and he had refused them all, for they
were not worthy of her, he thought. The girl did
not complain, she was too happy at home. Beanti-
fui and cultivated, mistress of a private house
th a t would have supported half-a-dozen middle*
class families in comfort, and the centre of an
adoring circle, her life was passed amid all the
satisfaction possible from leisure adorned with
seemingly boundless Wealth, till her twenty-third
year. Then the inevitable man came; He was a
young barrister, with a little money, intelli-
Rent and pure-living,—and briefless. Mr. War-
phy’g influence soon corrected the solitary defect,
y OUU g tdah had two qualities which help k
iiotable career wheh backed up by much money;
Jjj ce i oV ed him; and as the young couple consented
ye w j fc jj him, Mr, Warphy agreed to their en-
gagement.
Tliey had been engaged just a year at Cup-time
1888, and their marriage was to take place at
All Saints, in the week succeeding the great race-
d The cer emony was to be one of the events

Mr. Percy Warphy’s midnight visitor laughed

“And you came through at once—that was kind

“No,” he said, “I shall not stay to-night—

iny excuse for troubling you now, for of course

“ Your business,” rejoined Mr. Warphy,
unctuously, “will command my attention at any

“No, I’ll not stay ; and I’ll defer the pleasure
of offering my congratulations to Miss Warphy ”
—Mr. Warphy was leading the way upstairs to

oan we have a few minutes together?”
Mr. Tasker had imported a degree of decisive-
ness into the soft modulation of his voice that
made his host lose a little of his vivid colour ; just
the instantaneous paling of the cheek that attacks
a man upon whose present well-ordered life the
masked battery of an old-time sin suddenly opens
fire; and the observant blue eyes of the midnight
guest noticing it, gleamed more brilliantly.
“Well, I’m sorry you’ll not honour us by
remaining,” said Mr. Warphy; “we shall be midis-
turbed in the library.” With that, he preceded

Tobn Tasker had made his speaking voice a
study as great vocalists do their singing organs,
and he used it as artistically. Half his power lay
in his bank-book ; but the magic of much of ms
mighty personal influence was contained in the
flexibility and range of his voice. Now, when he
was about to utter terrible words, his tones pre-
served their music. They rose and fell in a
perfect cadence.

Before, however, we report wnat John
Jasker said, let us say who Tohn Tasker was.
“John Tasker, speculator,” as he called himself
when he had to state his “ trade or profession, ’
was the substance of whom Percy Warphy, Esq.,
T.P., was a mere shadow—one of many shadows ;
for Tohn Tasker’s personality presented many
f a cets to the sun. There were many Warphys ;
there was only one Tasker. The one used .the
many; made them dance as they in their turn
made others dance ; they, too, were simple
puppets in his hands. In gold, in silver, in tin,
in wool and grain, in ships and coal, in lands and
houses, in almost every form of industrial enter-
prise, in Government stocks, in that most mer-
chantable of commodities—political honour; in all
these things Tohn Tasker speculated and jobbed,
Not three men in the Australias knew the
greatness of his power, or the ramification
G f his affairs ; but when Tay re-
ceived a cable message signed “ Tasker, he
thought twice over it, and Rothschilds had a code
of which the duplicate never left Tohn Taskers
pocket-book. Nominally the employer of a
solitary clerk—a grim-featured and taciturn corn-
bination of shabby tweed and dirty collar—Tasker
set and kept going the labour of an army. An
army? No; a people.
His shadows did not know one another. The
most substantial shadow of the lot, Warphy, did not
know three others who stood towards Tasker in a
like relation, though he believed there were scores,
When Warphy received a brief memo, to the effect
that the purchases of the current year’s clip or
harvest were to go “home” in Messrs. Mayne
and Maste’s new steamship, Mr. Warphy might
BU t but did not ,know for certain that M. and
constituted another of Tasker’s shadows,

Finance Association (paid-up capital, £2,000,000)
had an imposing (sham) directorate, but was ruled
by J asker; and it was Jasker who communicated
a “ code cable ” to the Honourable the Treasurer
to warn him that if the new five-million loan was
put on the market before such a date, it would be
made a failure.
People knew he was rich, but as to the extent of
his wealth, any more than as to the range of his
business, no one, save his old clerk, could speak.
Once a lady, Who, was as Well acquainted with him
iii 3 anyone, asked him, frankly: “What are you
Worth, Mr. Jasker?” And he had made her this
response : “If you mean morally, madam—l am
Worth nothing ;if you mean monetarily—l cannot
siy. You must ask my clerk, John Denham.”
Men in the inner circles of finance put him men-
tally down as a bi-millionaire in his own right, and
as commanding ten millions of foreign capital.
However, none could assert with truth that he
could ever guess at John Jasker’s means. The
physical shell of the man was, as we have hinted,
agreeable to the sight. Slightly over the medium
height, his fifty-odd years of life had left his hair
untinted with grey, and had done no more in
passing than press a gentle finger on his forehead.
His eyes were deep-set, and penetrated with a
steady sharpness into any opposing nature, but, as
as a rule, they looked liquidly on men and things.
His voice we have already described.
He had never married. When taxed with his
bachelordom, he would reply : “ I am wedded to
—business —” And he would complete the sen-
tence under his breath: “And my business is
revenge. ”

To go on with our story.
As Jasker took his seat, by a ’seemingly careless
sweep of his arm, he tilted a lamp-shade. The
light of the lamp was, in this way, thrown on
Warphy’s face, while his own sank into deeper
shadow. Mr. Warphy’s palate grew dry as he
noticed the action. He kpew then that he was to
be placed in the rack whereon he had so frequently
placed others.
“You marry your daughter to-morrow, Mr.
Warphy ? ”
“ Yes ! ” For the life of him Mr. Warphy could
nob prevent his fear imparting itself to the petty
monosyllable.
“ And — you will excuse the question to an old—
friend—may I ask what dowry you propose to give
her ? ”
“ Twenty-five thousand 1 ” was the stammered
reply.
“ Of my money ? ”
You would haVe thought Mr. Jasker Was utter-
ing an endearment. There was a caress in every
syllable of the startling question. Startling it
was; it shocked the blood from Mr. Warphy’
countenance and left it a dull, ashen grey. He
did not answer, dnd the interrogatory was re-
peated ; repeated with a softer music.
“ What—do—you—mean ? ” he gasped at last.
“ Listen —listen intently ! ” said Mr. Jasker.
As though arty mart not a Notfolk Island “ demon”
or a Format of New Caledonia would not listen
intently to his death-sentence ! And Mr. Jasker
spoke at length, putting sibillanoes and cadences
into his speech as if he were wooing a capricious
beauty.
“ Warphy, you are hi difficulties! Warphy,
you are ruined ! Warphy, you are in danger of
Queen-street —and the dock ! And yet you can
wear a smile—and marry your daughter! You
are a brave man, Warphy. That is, if you are not
the most consummate of rogues ! ”
“Not that—not that,” moaned Warphy. The
man had shrunk visibly ; his very body seemed to
have withered; and, as to his face, it was distorted
like a paralytic’s.
“Don’t interrupt, please,” Mr. Jasker pro-
ceeded. “The most consummate of rogues—or
else a very brave man. For you greeted me with
a smile and a word of hearty welcome, and ten-
dered me a hand-clasp which would have been
tVarnl, doubtless, had I accepted it. Did you notice
I didn’t take your hand ? I am not a punctilious
man—you know that —but I could not take your
hand —knowing what I know —and intending to
do what I propose.” But this last assertion he
breathed to himself.
“ And what is your position ? Suppose I answer
my own question? You have over-speculated in
land—you helped to originate a ‘ boom’ which has
already ruined hundreds, and will yet ruin thou-
sands. You were not to be ruined, of course ; you
were to build a huge fortune on others’ downfall.
But, as it happens, you are ruined—you are
another Frankenstein—tortured and destroyed by
the monster you created. You will observe”—and
he paused pleasantly to throw a gibe at the wretch
before him —“ a man of your literary tastes ’’—here
he pointed to the book-cases —“cannot have failed
to observe that I did not speak of Frankenstein
as being the monster —you are not suffering from
the Frankenstein, for you are Frankenstein. But
I do not mean to imply you are not a monster as
well. That is where Mrs. Shelley’s romance is
weak. She should have made Frankenstein and
the monster different aspects of the one dual per-
sonality. But she couldn't do so, for she tried too
soon—she was not acquainted with the land-boom
organiser of this day.” He paused to laugh .
merrily. The ashert face across the table twitched
in agony. The scalpel had cut a nerve.
“Yes,” he went on, “ the forces you originated,
have destroyed you. Ycrur buyers cannot’ com-
plete their contracts, they have forfeited deposits
and thrown a score of blocks on your hands ; and
your dummy companies cannot unload on to the
public. The confiding public is confiding no
longer—will be more-distrustful still a month—a
Week hence. You have not, it is true, forfeited
your deposits to your Vendors; you have honour-
ably kept your engagements—honourably, I say,
have you not ? You have carried out contracts un-
flinchingly. To-day your name stands higher
than ever because you have done so. Men say on
’Change, in the banks, in the auction-rooms, that
you have not been ‘hit’—that you have made
money when men hitherto reputed to be as bril-
liant financiers as your are, have lost it, because
you have not been afraid to look losses boldly in
the face. Men say so, about you—and I know
differently, ’’
The massive library-clock struck one . The
stricken man’s head bent lower as the deep respn-
ance echded through the room. It brought to his
mind the soul-bell. Mr. Jasker waited till the
sound died away.
“ I asked for half-an hour—l have already taken
an hour, and am afraid I must trespass yet a little
longer on your time. But you Will forgive me, I
know, considering the urgency.” Again the
cadence of his laugh rang out pleasantly.
“ You —and I—and my confidential clerk, John
Denham, know differently. We three understand
how it is that your name and your bills have not
gone to protest together—how it is your banker
still replies “ Good for any amount!” to arixious
queries as to your standing—how it is that, with
a shrinkage of nearly a-fifth of a million in the
values of your properties, a shrinkage that must
double itself within six months, you can still keep
your credit good and a smile on your face —and
how it is you can marry your daughter !”
Warphy rose up, the man within him flickered
into mad gestures for a moment only. Throwing
himself again into his chair he groaned and
sobbed. And Mr. Jasker looked on and smiled.
When he resumed his talk he purred into a more
sympathetic softness.
“Wfe know—we three—that you have used
trust funds—money entrusted to fOti bjr me ; that
ypu have also taken advantage of my carte-blajich'e
to draw on Jephson’s, London, as against wool
and grain purchases which you have not made;
that you have obtained huge advances on scrip
belonging to me but lodged in your hands for
facility of operation. You have done these things,
and more. You have done them all, of course,
with the intention of repaying the amounts when
you realised on your properties, and the shares
of the shoddy companies were transformed into
sovereigns that had nothing to do with shoddy.
And the properties are unrealised—and the public
refuse to be bled—and I want my money !”
He stopped for, it might be, two minutes.
Then, as there was no response, except the palsied
shaking of the man on the other side of the
table—what other could there be ?—he began
again.
“You are discourteous, friend Warphy—very!
To a man to ‘ whom you owe so much,’ too ! You,
who have the reputation of being so square and
polite to everybody except your unfortunate
debtors, to be rudely silent when a man asks you
for his money— it is surprising ! And so small a
sum, too! —merely a matter of one hundred
thousand pounds ”
Warphy glanced up. His face seemed less
ashen for a moment— it even took upon its outlines
a faint tinge of relief.
“Oh, if lam in error, please correct me ! Did
I overstate the amount—or understate it ? Sup-
pose we say twnrhundred thousand pounds,—a
quarter of a millioiT—three hundred thousand ”
The flash of colour passed from Warphy’s tor-
tured features as the hope that Jasker did not
know the extent of his fraudulent appropriations
receded from his heart.
“Let it stand at three hundred thousand— f 0
the time being. Now, Mr. Warphy, where is niv
money?—my three hundred thousand pounds?”
He waited for a reply—and waited. At last
the thing across the table whimpered, in child mi!
phrase : “ I haven’t got it!”
Mr. Jasker laughed gaily.
# * *
“Of course you haven’t! ” Jasker went
on to say as soon as he had had his laugh out
“ If I thought you had I shouldn’t have asked
you for it. But it has gone; and you cannot meet
my first demand ; and you are civilly liable to me ”
Again Warphy lifted his head ; again something
like hope glanced across his features. Jasker’s
next ff'o'rds bent the head, however, lower and
lower, and drew the shutter down over the loom
hole through which the hopeful gleam i lt Sj
filtered.
“ Civilly—arid criiriinally. Aye,- criminally (
Not to allude to what I don’t kriow, and speaking
only of what I know, I accuse you of criminal
conduct unsurpassed in our Australian annals
People have termed you a great man, Warphy.
You have done that which may—nay, does ill
entitle you to be called a great criminal. Your
crimes, like your ambitions, Warphy, have been
large—immense—mammoth ! You deserve g
chapter all to yourself in the Bingraphia Flagiiiosi
You understand the phrase, Warphy? It would
be an impertinence in me to translate it—for these
book-cases prove to me that you have kept up youy
studies in the intervals of your financial coups—
and your crimes. ”
He paused to yield himself once more to the
spirit of jocoseness that was fed by his own irony
When he spoke next, he had changed his tone. ly
was now respectful, if not cordial, with an inde-
finable masterfulness about it that implied to the
over-acute sensibilities of Warphy both the desire
arid the power to help Him in the extremity where
the antecedent speech had lodged Him,.
“Listen, again, Mr. Warphy,” Jaskef’ gifij
“You remember your father ?” Warphy rioclded
assent. “He was a good father to you ?”
“He was—a very good father.” Surely this
not the bold, resolute - tone of Mr. Percy
Warphy ?
“ You reverence his memory ?”
Again the sign of assent: to which Jasker
rejoined : “ That is well—you can the more
readily understand how I can reverence a parent.
Well, I am hei’e to-night to fulfil a parent’s wish!
My mother —whose memory I reverence as I
reverence nothing else —owes your father a debt.
She wished me to pay that debt to your father!
I could not —he died. But I may, I can, I will
pay it to his son.”
A great hope was shaping itself in Warphy’s
consciousness. He thought he understood that
prompted by some instinct of gratitude, Jasker
proposed to avert the disaster which his previous
words had threatened to unloose.
“My mother’s debt I will pay to your father's
son. You know who your father was? A prin-
cipal official of the convict establishment in
Sydney. But you do not know who my father
was, do you? He was—a convict—a transport
per ship Ocean. His name was John Jasker, too,
and his register number was No. 38,800. He was
transported for embezzlement embezzlement,
Mr. Warphy. Curious, isn’t it, how things

crime, Mr. Warphy, for you were my servant
