Mrs. HAYES
Proprietress.
years. My mother worked her fingers to the
bone, as the saying is—your daughter will never
have to do that, Mr. Warphy!—first, to support
herself and child—l was the child—and then to
earn our passages out, and followed him after
three years. On arrival, she was advised by a
good friend —one John Denham —yes, the same
John Denham who is now my clerk, to apply for
my father’s assignment to her. Such things were
done daily, but her application was refused; she
was not rich enough to grease the palms of the
officials ; and such things were seldom done when
people could not pay for them. But your father
heard of her story and put himself in communica-
tion with her. He saw her beautiful face —she
was very beautiful then, Denham says—and he
listened to her charming voice—people say I have
a pleasing voice, but Denham says her’s was perfect
music—and he promised her, not compliance with
her request, but more. He promised her his free-
dom. He had great power; as for pardons, such
a one he could have for the asking. He obtained
my father’s pardon ”
“ That was like him! ” interjected Mr. Warphy.
“He was ever generous!” A portion of the
man’s portliness came back to him as he reflected
that his thought was right. Jasker could mean
nothing else than to forget and forgive because of
that ancient service!
“Yes, he was generous,” Mr. Jasker exclaimed.
“He exacted only one condition—one slight con-
dition—and on the same condition, I will grant
you a release from your indebtedness to me, and
thus save you from the legal consequences of your
crime. That will be virtually giving you your
freedom, will it not?” Warphy lowered his
head.
“ Dreedom for Freedom ! ” Jasker resumed.
“You agree to that? I desire a verbal answer,
please.”
The hope leapt now into full life in Warphy’s
breast, and an overpowering thankfulness, also,
that his father had done that kindly act in the far-
distant past and thus interposed his dead hand
between his son and ruin—shame—death ! The
hands of the dead thrust themselves into the face
of the living, he thought, how often do they hold
within their grasp some dread penalty and some
immortal sin. But his father held out to him
from the grave, how blessed a good ! A spasmodic
exultation sent the blood coursing with new
vigour through his veins, and his manhood re-
turned to him for a second, as he uttered impetu-
ously—“ Yes, yes ! ”
“I am glad of that,” said Jasker. “Freedom
for freedom—freedom with a condition for free-
dom with a condition—that is fair ! The mother’s
debt paid to the father’s son. That is fair ! By
the way, does it occur to you that freedom for
freedom is, after all, but an application of the Lex
talionis? Do you see this ? Answer, please.”
“Yes !” murmured Warphy, who did not know
where all this was leading him.
“Ah, I am glad you see that! ’cried Jasker,
and, rising, he threw a terrifying stridence into
his next words ; “An eye for an eye—a tooth for
a tooth —a life for a life—freedom for freedom—
honour jor honour !”
For the second time that night, Warphy asked
the other what he meant.
“ What do I mean ?” Jasker hissed. “ I mean
that I will grant you immunity and safety —if you
grant me your daughter's honour /”
The clock here struck two, but it is doubtful if
either heard the gong. Warphy had risen, and
stood glancing at the man who had cast at him
that last vile sentence.
“My daughter’s—honour ! You are mad !” He
gathered at length sufficient power to say those
words.
Mr. Jasker went back to his seat, and, smiling,
adopted again the rippling softness of voice in
which he had spoken during the first part of the
the interview.
“No!” he said, “I am not mad! But listen
once more !”
And then he proceeded to describe to Warphy
one of those shameful transactions which was so
common in the palmy days of the System.
“There was my mother, beautiful, but friend-
less—for Denham had left Sydney—and starving,
with her child, in a strange land ! There was my
father—who could work to support his wife
and child if he were free, but who was “ under
bond.” There was your father—and he promised
to free the husband on condition that the wife
abandoned to him—herself, her honour. Do you
hear, Warphy? her honour—that was the condi-
tion on which alone he would help her.”
The sadness that Avas audible in the strong
man’s tones was not noAv a trick of the voice. It
expressed an epic grief.
“ She fought long before she yielded—she went
Ayithout food herself for days, she was threatened
with eviction from her paltry lodging into the
street; she would have invited that fate, with its
consequence of being sent to Parramatta Factory
as a vagrant —she would then, at least, have won
food for her boy—but for the dreadful companion-
ship of the Factory drabs. And at last the hour
came when her child cried for food, and she had
none to give it. She had seen that day, in the
iron-gang in the Domain, my father. She had
watched him at his work —they were quarrying on
the Woqlloomooloo aide—and had seized an
opportunity to speak to him, and to ask him how
he fared! “For God’s sake! Phoebe,” he whis-
pered, “move Heaven and hell to get me out of
here, or I die !” She could not tell him then of
her extremity—and that night your father had
his will.”
IB“ For some minutes there was no sound in the
room, save the beat of the clock, but to both men
the silence Avas vocal with the myriad tragedies of
the System.
“But I will say this for your father—he was
not like many men who did the same thing—who
promised to make the same hideous barter and
failed to perform their share of the bargain. He
did obtain my father’s freedom two days later.
And what do you think my father did with it
when he got it ? My mother told him her story—
how she had won his release. He listened without
a word ; and the following day he earned ten
shillings. With that money he bought a horse-
whip, and with that whip he flogged your father
in Phillip-street, in the very presence of the
Governor himself.
“Yes, he —the newly-emancipated convict—
flogged a powerful convict-official! Of course, to
such an act there could be only one sequel. They
took him to Charlotte Place, then and there, and
ordered him three hundred lashes; and from
Charlotte Place they dragged him to Barrack
Square forthwith to receive bis punishment.
They ordered out a new cat-o’-nine-tails—a new
“Slyde’s Improved”—but their care, that he
should suffer properly, was wasted. His head
drooped on his shoulders at the seventeenth
stroke ; he was unloosed j but he was dead.
“I never think of my father without feeling
proud of him for those two things—that he
thrashed your father and that he cheated the
bystem out of 283 lashes. God ! How angry
the authorities must have been when they were
robbed of their just dues ! Denham tells me that
your father was wild over the matter. ‘ I would
not have minded,’ he said, ‘if the beggar had
died at the 217th cut, but at the 17th !’ No
doubt it was preposterous conduct on any father’s
part, and for the offence he thus gave to your
father. I now, on behalf of my father, apologise
to your father’s son. It was ungrateful, deuced
ungrateful, of my progenitor, was it not to thus
annoy his benefactor ?” He had given utterance,
one would have suspected to a capital joke, he
infused so much heartiness into his laugh. He
was still indulging in his self-applausive merri-
ment when the door-bell rang suddenly.
Warphy, who had re-seated himself, started up.
The last revelation made by Jasker had driven
away his assumption of courage, and he was a
mere heap of clothes and shaking nerves when the
bell shocked him into a more violent tremor.
“What —what—is that?” he convulsively ejacu-
lated.
“It is a good thing I know you,” sweetly
answered Mr. Jasker, “otherwise I should have
thought you had an uneasy conscience. I have
forgotten I should have told you that a friend of
mine would call for me shortly at two o’clock,
if I failed to appear by that time at a certain'
spot.” Again, the bell rang, and Jasker sug-
gested that they should go to the door. “ Your
porter must be asleep, and you will not care to let
the whole house and your guests know of these
late callers.” Mechanically, as in a dream,
Warphy followed his tyrant.. The spacious man-
sion was hushed in repose, the last guest had long
since retired ; and a weary footman who had sat
up to receive the final commands of his master,
slept soundly in a chair at the foot of the stair-
case. Warphy stretched forth a limp hand to
rouse the servant, but on Jasker’s remark that he
would open the door, desisted. The latter flung
wide the stately door, and the hall-ligh*ts shone
upon two men standing in the verandah.
“ All right, then, Jasker,” said one of them.
“Yes —but come in, Denham. Come in, both
of you. You will forgive my asking my friends
in, Mr. Warphy? You know Denham, do you
not, Mr. Warphy ? And this other gentleman is
a de-tec —tive, Mr. Warphy. I thought it
better to prepare for emergencies, you know.
Men have gone into houses at midnight on more
agreeable errands than mine before now, and
never left them alive. Now, gentlemen all,
come!” He preceded them into the library.
That great man, Percy Warphy, drunk with terror,
had to be helped into the room by John Denham
and the officer of the Criminal Investigation De-
partment. When they had shuffled the poor
sinner into a seat, Jasker said to the detective :
‘‘Take a chair and wait outside, please.” The
man obeyed. The clock chimed three-quarters
past two.
Let me resume where I left off, W arr ,t „
said Jasker. “After my father’s death— t.
exact, I should say after his murder—my mr f? ° e
position was worse than before, for the inc’rt 8
had made her sha —the transaction-town ftf
One man only came to her aid—Denham herew
had been away from Sydney on business but’ Kb
returned, and lie gave her means—” ’ nacl
“Lent her money—it was repaid with interest »
said Denham. “b
Jasker went on ; “ Denham had conic int,. „
perty. He had been foully wronged when^ 0 '
assigned. servant by his mistress ; but she
amends, for she left him her estate at her dc> ti
Occasionally, you must know, Warphy, the ? '
tem was guilty of an act of atonement, wjif
Denham had gone to take possession of his est t ’
when the—transaction—occurred. On his retur
he heard of it, as did everybody, and he earned’
my mother’s assistance. He set her up in b U s'°
ness ; she made money ; she bought property • J!'
became rich, very rich—thanks to the advice of
Denham. She sent me to Cape’s school-d 0 V n
remember the good old man, Warphy? You wer>
there, you know ; you were a junior when I w<l !
senior, and I, saved you from many a piece of
bullying, for upon your head, even then, Warphy
were some of your father’s peccadiloes beinc
visited. I was sent to Oxford, and while I Wa i
there my mother—died.”
“She left me a great heritage in estates and
money; but that was small wealth to her other
legacy—a transcendant hate! You, Warphv
whom men think so clever, are a fool, for you ham
thought that in the accumulation of wealth was
to be found the greatest joy, the greatest good of
existence. Fool—fool, thrice over ! A mightv
emotion, a surprising love, a transcendent hate
will confer greater bliss than a monopoly of A
Broken Hill or a Bischoff. My mother ruined
your father, sent him to his grave a broken-
hearted and despised creature. But she was not
satisfied—she passed on her hate to me—and in a
letter which she left with her will and in which
she told me her story—bade me ruin you and
yours. lam here to-night to obey the command
Perhaps now yon will understand how it is I have
been so friendly to you, and why I have granted
you such extraordinary monetary facilities. My
friendliness was a trap—which has been eminently
successful!” The shattered wretch moaned.
# * *
“ You have not mentioned the condition
Jasker,” said John Denham.
“ I did so, but I do not think our friend—our
enemy here fully understood ! Did von
Warphy ? ” 3 ’
“No; no!” shrieked the badgered scoundrel,
and he thrust his hands out as if to avert the im-
pending wickedness.
“ I will restate it, then,” said Jasker, calmly.
“ Listen, for the last time ! The one condition on
which I will save you from those vultures of society
which, else, will to-morrow —no, this very day-
chant their Te Deuth Laudamus over your carcase
—is listen! —your daughter’s honour, your
daughter’s shame ! ”
“Blackguard! Fiend!” cried Warphy. Denham
chuckled. Jasker only smiled.
“You are addressing those epithets to the
memory of your father,” responded Jasker. “ But
why should you object ? Have you not, indeed,
already agreed that Freedom for Freedom, Honour
for Honour, is a fair bargain ? Your freedom for
my father’s, your daughter’s honour—or shame—
for my mother’s. What can be fairer? Besides,
Mr. Warphy ”•—he spoke with the gentle patience
which a man uses to persuade an irrational, pig-
headed person—“you who are a pillar of the
Church will appreciate this argument—the sugges-
tion has scriptural sanction. The Mosaic law, you
know ” ;_ M
“You mean—to marry her?” Warphy asked
piteously. He could not realise that Jasker had
made to him the most shameful proposition that
could fall from human lips.
“ Marry her ! ’’ laughed Jasker and Denham in
derisive unison. “ Marry her ! ” Jasker exclaimed,
“No ! ” And Denham said ; “ That would be a
curious way of visiting upon her head the sins of
her father, and upon your head, those of your
father! ”
The other scoundrel—the tormented and mad-
dened one —rose, and flung himself at Jasker. The
detective officer, hearing the scuffle, opened the
door, and came in. The creature that had once
been Percy Warphy fell back, cowed, impotently
furious, sobbing. Denham whispered to the man,
and he withdrew.
“ Then do I understand,” urbanely queried Mr.
Jasker, “that you refuse freedom and safety on
this one condition, Warphy?” The Thing ad-
dressed sat dumb. ’
“ Think, Warphy, a receipt in full, in exchange
for your daughter’s honour ! ”
“ Then, Warphy, I speak to you for the last
time—are you man enough to die ? At four
o’clock we will come back, and if we find you,
alive, then —the warrant, Warphy, the warrant! ”
They went out. On the landing the detective
joined them. The three passed noiselessly through
the hall; by the sleeping footman; out into the
dawning. When they returned an hour later from
watching the sun toss his beams from ridge to
peak, and from peak to pinnacle of the cloudy
Alps which lay piled in the East, they found that
the master of the house had been man enough, or
coward enough, to act on Jasker’s hint.
